Wills of an Apostate Woman: Is She Like a Dhimmiyya?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on apostate woman's will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the different views within the Hanafi school on the validity of an apostate woman's will and identify the relied-upon position based on the commentator's argument.

Core Idea
An apostate woman's will is valid like a dhimmiyya's because she is not killed, but this is contested by some Hanafis.

The ruling on the will of a woman who apostatizes from Islam (murtaddah) is disputed within the Hanafi school. The author of al-Hidayah considers her like a dhimmiyya, making her will valid. Al-Sighnaqi reports that some scholars hold the opposite, arguing she is not left upon her belief. The commentator in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq weighs in, favoring the view that her will is valid. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the critique of an attempted reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue: apostate woman's will
The question is whether a woman who leaves Islam (apostate) can make a valid will. Distinguish from a male apostate.
The male apostate is killed or must return; his will is suspended.
2
State al-Hidayah's position
The author of al-Hidayah says the most correct view is that her will is valid because she remains upon apostasy (not killed) and is thus like a dhimmiyya.
Arabic: 'الأصح أنه تصح وصاياها لأنها تبقى على الردة... فجعلها كالذمية'
3
State al-Sighnaqi's reported position
Al-Sighnaqi reports a disagreement: some say she is not like a dhimmiyya and her will is invalid. The difference is that a dhimmiyya is left on her belief, but the apostate woman is not.
Arabic: 'لا تكون بمنزلة الذمية وهو الصحيح لا تصح منها وصية'
4
Analyze the commentator's critique of al-Inayah
The author of al-Inayah claimed no contradiction because one authority used 'sahih' and the other 'asahh'. The commentator refutes this, arguing that 'sahih' implies preference, not mere correctness.
Use the commentator's own argument: 'لا شك أن مراد من قال في الخلافيات هو الصحيح ترجيح هذا القول على الآخر'
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentator prefers the view that the apostate woman is like a dhimmiyya, so her will is valid. This is also supported by Qadi Khan.
Commentator's conclusion: 'الأشبه أن تكون كالذمية تحوز وصيتها'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو مختلف ماله المسلم أوذمي . وليس في معتقدهم كما إذا أوصى بالحج وببناء المساجد للمسلمين أو بأن تسرج مساجدنا لأنه معصية عندهم إلا أن يكون لقوم بأعيانهم فيصح باعتبار التمليك. ومنها ما فيه وهو ما إذا أوصى بما هو قربة عندهم وليس بقربة عندنا كبناء الكنيسة لقوم غير معينين ونحوه . فعند أبي حنيفة يجوز ، وعندهما لا يجوز. فإن كان لقوم معينين يجوز في الكل على أنه تمليك لهم، وما ذكره من الجهة من تسريج المساجد ونحوه خرج منه على طريق المشورة لا على طريق الإلزام حتى لا يلزم أن يصرفوه في الجهة التي عينها هو بل يفعلون به ما شاؤوا ولأنه ملكهم والوصية إنما صحت باعتبار التمليك لهم. وصاحب البدعة إذا كان لا يكفر فهو في حق الوصية بمنزلة المسلم لأنا أمرنا ببناء الأحكام على ظاهر الإسلام وإن كان يكفر فهو بمنزلة المرتد فيكون على الخلاف المعروف في تصرفاته . قال صاحب الهداية في المرتدة: الأصح أنه تصح وصاياها لأنها تبقى على الردة بخلاف المرتد لأنه يقتل أو يسلم فجعلها كالذمية وقال السغناقي في النهاية : ذكر صاحب الكتابة في الزيادات الخلاف على هذا وقال بعضهم : لا تكون بمنزلة الذمية وهو الصحيح لا تصح منها وصية والفرق بينهما وبين الذمية أن الذمية تقر على اعتقادها، وأما المرتدة فلا تقر على اعتقادها ا هـ . وقال صاحب العناية بعد أن نقل هذا من النهاية : والظاهر أن لا منافاة بين كلاميه لأنه قال هناك الصحيح وههنا الأصح وهما يصدقان ا هـ . أقول : هذا ليس بشيء إذ لا شك أن مراد من قال في الخلافيات هو الصحيح ترجيح هذا القول على القول الآخر لا بيان مجرد صحته مع رجحان الآخر كما أن من قال هو الأصح ترجيحه على الآخر بل قوله هو الصحيح أدل على الترجيح من قوله هو الأصح، ولا ريب أن ترجيح أحدهما على الآخر نافي ترجيح الآخر عليه ولا يمكن أن يصدقا معاً. قال الراجي عفو ربه : الأشبه أن تكون كالذمية تحوز وصيتها لأنها لا تقتل ولهذا يجوز جميع تصرفاتها، وكذا الوصية كأنه أراد بقوله صاحب الكتاب صاحب الهداية. وذكر السغناقي أن من ارتد عن الإسلام إلى النصرانية أو اليهودية أو المجوسية فحكم وصاياه حكم من انتقل إليهم فما صح منهم صح منه وهذا عندهما، وأما عند أبي حنيفة فوصيته موقوفة ووصايا المرتدة نافذة بالإجماع لأنها لا تقبل عندنا. وقال قاضيخان المرتدة الصحيح أنها كالذمية فيجوز منها ما جاز من الذمية وما لا فلا.
It is different: his property whether Muslim or dhimmi. And not in their belief, like if he bequeaths for Hajj and building mosques for Muslims or that our mosques be lit because it is disobedience in their view, unless it is for specific individuals, then it is valid by consideration of ownership. And among them is what is disputed, which is if he bequeaths what is a good deed in their view but not a good deed in our view, like building a church for unspecified individuals and the like. According to Abu Hanifa it is permissible, according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is not permissible. If it is for specified individuals, it is permissible in all cases as it is ownership for them. And what he mentioned of the direction of lighting mosques and the like, it came from him by way of consultation not by way of obligation, so they are not forced to spend it in the direction he specified, rather they do with it what they wish, and because it is their property, and the will is only valid by consideration of ownership for them. And the innovator if he is not considered a disbeliever, then he is like a Muslim regarding the will because we are commanded to base rulings on apparent Islam, but if he is considered a disbeliever, then he is like an apostate, then the well-known difference regarding his dispositions applies. The author of al-Hidayah said regarding the apostate woman: The most correct is that her will is valid because she remains upon apostasy, unlike the apostate man because he is killed or becomes Muslim, so he made her like the dhimmiyya. And al-Sighnaqi said in al-Nihayah: The author of the book mentioned in al-Ziyadat the disagreement on this, and some said: She is not like the dhimmiyya, and that is correct: her will is not valid. The difference between her and the dhimmiyya is that the dhimmiyya is left upon her belief, but the apostate woman is not left upon her belief. End quote. The author of al-Inayah said after quoting this from al-Nihayah: The apparent is that there is no contradiction between his two statements because he said there 'correct' and here 'more correct', and both can be true. End quote. I say: This is nothing, for there is no doubt that whoever says in disputed matters 'it is correct' means preference of this statement over the other, not mere statement of its correctness while the other is more preponderant. Rather, his saying 'it is correct' is more indicative of preference than his saying 'it is more correct'. There is no doubt that preference of one over the other negates preference of the other, and they cannot both be true. The one hoping for his Lord's pardon said: The more similar is that she is like the dhimmiyya; her will is valid because she is not killed, and therefore all her dispositions are valid, and likewise the will. It is as if he meant by 'author of the book' the author of al-Hidayah. And al-Sighnaqi mentioned that whoever apostatizes from Islam to Christianity, Judaism, or Zoroastrianism, the ruling on his wills is like the ruling on those he converted to; whatever is valid from them is valid from him. And this is according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). As for Abu Hanifa, his will is suspended, and the wills of an apostate woman are effective by consensus because she is not killed in our view. And Qadi Khan said: The correct view is that the apostate woman is like the dhimmiyya; whatever is permissible from the dhimmiyya is permissible from her, and whatever is not, is not.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of bequests from dhimmis (non-Muslim subjects of an Islamic state) and apostates. The main principle is that a bequest is valid if it amounts to a transfer of ownership (tamlik), even if the designated purpose is not considered a religious good deed in Islam. The author distinguishes between three cases: (1) When the dhimmi bequeaths for purposes that are good deeds in Islam (like Hajj or building mosques) but not in his own religion, the bequest is void because it is not in his belief, unless it is given to specific individuals, in which case it is valid as ownership. (2) When he bequeaths for a purpose that is good in his own religion but not in Islam (like building a church), there is a dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa permits it because it is a transfer of ownership to the beneficiaries; the Sahibayn (Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) disallow it because it is for a sinful purpose. (3) The same applies to an innovator (sahib al-bid'ah): if his innovation does not make him a disbeliever, he is treated as a Muslim; if it does, he is like an apostate. The passage then discusses the apostate woman (murtaddah). Imam al-Hidayah states that the most correct view is that her will is valid because she remains upon apostasy and is not killed, unlike the male apostate. Al-Sighnaqi reports disagreement, and some hold that she is not like a dhimmiyya because she is not left upon her belief. The author of al-Inayah tries to reconcile the views, but the commentator rejects this, arguing that 'sahih' (correct) and 'asahh' (more correct) are not equal. The preferred view is that the apostate woman is like a dhimmiyya, so her will is valid. For conversion to another religion, the ruling on wills is according to the new religion. Abu Hanifa suspends the will of a male apostate until his return or execution. The overall ruling is that a dhimmi's bequest for a purpose not good in Islam is valid only as transfer of ownership; for churches, Abu Hanifa permits, Sahibayn prohibit. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) likely follows the Sahibayn due to later preference, but the text does not explicitly state tarjih.

Key Terms
مرتدة (murtaddah): A woman who has apostatized from Islam.
ذمية (dhimmiyya): A non-Muslim woman living under the protection of an Islamic state, whose religious practices are tolerated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason given by some scholars for invalidating an apostate woman's will?
A) She is killed like a male apostate.
B) She is not left upon her belief, unlike a dhimmiyya.
C) She has no capacity to own property.
D) Her apostasy voids all contracts.

Key Takeaway
The apostate woman is treated like a dhimmiyya for the validity of her will because she is not executed, though this view is contested within the Hanafi school.

Wasiyyah — Bequest

Harbi's Bequest to a Muslim: No One-Third Limit
Addresses the validity and limitations of bequests from a harbi to a Muslim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a harbi's bequest exceeding one-third and explain why the one-third limit does not apply.

Core Idea
A harbi's heirs have no legal right in Islamic law, so the one-third limit on bequests does not apply.

In this passage, we examine the second scenario of cross-border bequests: an enemy combatant (harbi) leaving a bequest to a Muslim. The key question is whether the standard one-third limitation applies. The Hanafi ruling is clear: it does not, because the harbi's heirs have no recognized right under Islamic law. However, there is an alternate opinion that if the harbi has accepted aman and his heirs are present, he becomes like a dhimmi and the one-third limit applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a bequest (wasiyyah) from a harbi to a Muslim. The ruling: it is permissible (ja'iz) even if it exceeds one-third or covers the entire estate.
2
Locate the dalil (reasoning)
The proof is rational (dalil aqli): the prohibition of exceeding one-third is to protect heirs' rights; harbi heirs have no such right—they are 'dead in our view' (amwat fi haqqina). Also, the aman (safe conduct) was for the testator, not for his heirs.
The text uses 'laysa li-warathatihi haqq shar'i' – no legal right.
3
Note the alternate opinion
There is a qīla (it was said) that if the harbi's heirs are with him and he has accepted aman, he becomes like a dhimmi, so the one-third limit applies unless the heirs permit more.
This alternate opinion is not the main view.
4
Evaluate the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the harbi's bequest is valid without the one-third limit, as it is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
The alternate opinion is not preferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني وهو ما إذا أوصى الحربي لمسلم فلأنه أهل للتمليك منجزاً كالهبة ونحوها فكذا مضافاً، ولو أوصى بأكثر من الثلث أو بماله كله جاز لأنه امتناع الوصية بما زاد على الثلث لحق الورثة وليس لورثته حق شرعي لأنهم أموات في حقنا، ولأن حرمه ماله باعتبار الأمان والأمان كان لحقه لا لحق ورثته وليس لورثته حق شرعي وقد أسقط حقه فيجوز . وقيل : إذا كان ورثته معه لا يجوز بأكثر من الثلث إلا بإجازة منهم لأنه بالأمان التزم أحكامنا فصار كالذمي. ولو أوصى ببعض ماله نفذت الوصية في الثلث ورد الباقي لورثته، وكذا لو أوصى لمستأمن مثله ولو أعتق عبده عند الموت أو دبره جاز ذلك كله من غير تقييد بالثلث لما بينا، وكذا إذا أوصى له مسلم أو ذمي بوصية جاز لأنه ما دام في دار الإسلام كالذمي في المعاملات ولهذا تصح عقود التمليكات منه وتبرعاته في حال حياته فكذا عند مماته. وعن أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف : وصية الذمي للحربي المستأمن لا تجوز لأنه في دراهم حتى يمكن من الرجوع إليها، والأول أظهر لأن الوصية تمليك مبتدأ ولهذا يجوز للذمي لأنهم التزموا أحكام الإسلام فيما يرجع إلى المعاملات.
And as for the second, which is if the harbi bequeaths to a Muslim, because he is capable of transfer of ownership on spot like gift and such, so likewise when deferred. And if he bequeaths more than one-third or all his property, it is permissible because the prohibition of bequest exceeding one-third is due to the right of heirs, and his heirs have no legal right because they are dead in our view, and because his property is protected by reason of aman (safe conduct), and the aman was for his right not for the right of his heirs, and his heirs have no legal right, and he has dropped his right, so it is permissible. And it was said: If his heirs are with him, it is not permissible for more than one-third except with their permission, because by the aman he has bound himself to our laws, so he becomes like a dhimmi. And if he bequeaths part of his property, the bequest is executed in one-third and the rest is returned to his heirs. Likewise, if he bequeaths to a musta'min like him, and if he frees his slave at death or makes a tadbir, all that is permissible without restriction of one-third as we explained. Similarly, if a Muslim or dhimmi bequeaths to him, it is permissible because as long as he is in Dar al-Islam, he is like a dhimmi in transactions, and therefore contracts of transfer and donations from him are valid during his life, so also at his death. And from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: The will of a dhimmi to a harbi musta'min is not permissible because it is in dirhams until he can return to them. And the first is more apparent because the will is a new transfer of ownership, and therefore it is permissible for a dhimmi because they have committed to the laws of Islam concerning transactions.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage covers the second main topic: bequests from a harbi (enemy combatant who has been granted safe conduct or is in Dar al-Harb) to a Muslim. The key issue is whether the limitation of one-third applies. The ruling is that a harbi's bequest exceeding one-third is valid because his heirs have no recognized right under Islamic law (they are equivalent to dead in our view). The prohibition of exceeding one-third in Islamic law is to protect the rights of Muslim heirs; since the harbi's heirs have no such right, the full bequest is valid. However, there is an alternate opinion (qīla) that if the harbi's heirs are present with him in Dar al-Islam and he has accepted aman (safe conduct) and thus bound himself to Islamic law, then he becomes like a dhimmi and the one-third limit applies. The text then extends the ruling to bequests to a musta'min (a person from Dar al-Harb who has safe conduct) and to other acts like freeing slaves. It also states that a Muslim or dhimmi can validly bequeath to a musta'min because in Dar al-Islam he is treated like a dhimmi in transactions. The last part deals with the reverse: a dhimmi bequeathing to a musta'min harbi. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, this is not permissible because the musta'min is not considered a reliable recipient (the text says 'because it is in dirhams until he can return' which is unclear; likely it means until he can claim it). The first opinion (that it is permissible) is considered more apparent (azhar) because the will is a new transfer, and dhimmis are bound by Islamic law in transactions. This demonstrates the complexity of cross-border bequests in classical Hanafi law.

Key Terms
حربي (harbi): An enemy combatant, a person from Dar al-Harb without a safe-conduct agreement.
مستأمن (musta'min): A harbi who has been granted safe conduct (aman) to enter or reside in Dar al-Islam temporarily.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a harbi allowed to bequeath more than one-third of his property to a Muslim?
A) Because harbis are not bound by Islamic law at all
B) Because his heirs have no legal right in Islamic law
C) Because the bequest is considered a gift during life
D) Because the one-third rule only applies to dhimmis

Key Takeaway
A harbi's bequest to a Muslim is not restricted by the one-third limit because his heirs have no recognized right in Islamic law.

Dhimmi's Bequest to a Musta'min: Permissibility Dispute
Addresses the validity of a dhimmi's bequest to a musta'min harbi and the intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a dhimmi can bequeath to a musta'min harbi and state the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
The first view (valid) is more apparent: a dhimmi's bequest to a musta'min is a new transfer, and musta'mins are like dhimmis in transactions.

This passage also addresses a related but distinct issue: can a dhimmi (a non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state) leave a bequest to a musta'min (a harbi who has temporary safe conduct)? There is an intra-Hanafi dispute, with Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf holding it invalid, and the later preferred view (mufta bihi) holding it valid. The reasoning hinges on whether the musta'min is considered a reliable recipient.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
The act is a bequest from a dhimmi to a musta'min harbi. The disputed point: is it valid or not?
2
State the two positions
First position (general Hanafi view): valid. Second position (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): not valid.
The text attributes the second view to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf specifically.
3
Examine the evidence for each
First view: the musta'min is like a dhimmi in transactions; a will is a new transfer (tamlik mubtada'). Second view: 'because it is in dirhams until he can return to them' – unclear reasoning, possibly relating to the musta'min's lack of presence or trustworthiness.
The phrase 'fi darahim' may be an OCR error; likely meaning 'in his possession' or 'in his guarantee'.
4
Apply the tarjih
The author says 'al-awwal azhar' (the first is more apparent), indicating the preferred view. The mufta bihi is that such a bequest is valid.
The tarjih authority is the author of the text (Al-Haskafi or Ibn Abidin? The text is from Bahr al-Ra'iq).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني وهو ما إذا أوصى الحربي لمسلم فلأنه أهل للتمليك منجزاً كالهبة ونحوها فكذا مضافاً، ولو أوصى بأكثر من الثلث أو بماله كله جاز لأنه امتناع الوصية بما زاد على الثلث لحق الورثة وليس لورثته حق شرعي لأنهم أموات في حقنا، ولأن حرمه ماله باعتبار الأمان والأمان كان لحقه لا لحق ورثته وليس لورثته حق شرعي وقد أسقط حقه فيجوز . وقيل : إذا كان ورثته معه لا يجوز بأكثر من الثلث إلا بإجازة منهم لأنه بالأمان التزم أحكامنا فصار كالذمي. ولو أوصى ببعض ماله نفذت الوصية في الثلث ورد الباقي لورثته، وكذا لو أوصى لمستأمن مثله ولو أعتق عبده عند الموت أو دبره جاز ذلك كله من غير تقييد بالثلث لما بينا، وكذا إذا أوصى له مسلم أو ذمي بوصية جاز لأنه ما دام في دار الإسلام كالذمي في المعاملات ولهذا تصح عقود التمليكات منه وتبرعاته في حال حياته فكذا عند مماته. وعن أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف : وصية الذمي للحربي المستأمن لا تجوز لأنه في دراهم حتى يمكن من الرجوع إليها، والأول أظهر لأن الوصية تمليك مبتدأ ولهذا يجوز للذمي لأنهم التزموا أحكام الإسلام فيما يرجع إلى المعاملات.
And as for the second, which is if the harbi bequeaths to a Muslim, because he is capable of transfer of ownership on spot like gift and such, so likewise when deferred. And if he bequeaths more than one-third or all his property, it is permissible because the prohibition of bequest exceeding one-third is due to the right of heirs, and his heirs have no legal right because they are dead in our view, and because his property is protected by reason of aman (safe conduct), and the aman was for his right not for the right of his heirs, and his heirs have no legal right, and he has dropped his right, so it is permissible. And it was said: If his heirs are with him, it is not permissible for more than one-third except with their permission, because by the aman he has bound himself to our laws, so he becomes like a dhimmi. And if he bequeaths part of his property, the bequest is executed in one-third and the rest is returned to his heirs. Likewise, if he bequeaths to a musta'min like him, and if he frees his slave at death or makes a tadbir, all that is permissible without restriction of one-third as we explained. Similarly, if a Muslim or dhimmi bequeaths to him, it is permissible because as long as he is in Dar al-Islam, he is like a dhimmi in transactions, and therefore contracts of transfer and donations from him are valid during his life, so also at his death. And from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: The will of a dhimmi to a harbi musta'min is not permissible because it is in dirhams until he can return to them. And the first is more apparent because the will is a new transfer of ownership, and therefore it is permissible for a dhimmi because they have committed to the laws of Islam concerning transactions.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage covers the second main topic: bequests from a harbi (enemy combatant who has been granted safe conduct or is in Dar al-Harb) to a Muslim. The key issue is whether the limitation of one-third applies. The ruling is that a harbi's bequest exceeding one-third is valid because his heirs have no recognized right under Islamic law (they are equivalent to dead in our view). The prohibition of exceeding one-third in Islamic law is to protect the rights of Muslim heirs; since the harbi's heirs have no such right, the full bequest is valid. However, there is an alternate opinion (qīla) that if the harbi's heirs are present with him in Dar al-Islam and he has accepted aman (safe conduct) and thus bound himself to Islamic law, then he becomes like a dhimmi and the one-third limit applies. The text then extends the ruling to bequests to a musta'min (a person from Dar al-Harb who has safe conduct) and to other acts like freeing slaves. It also states that a Muslim or dhimmi can validly bequeath to a musta'min because in Dar al-Islam he is treated like a dhimmi in transactions. The last part deals with the reverse: a dhimmi bequeathing to a musta'min harbi. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, this is not permissible because the musta'min is not considered a reliable recipient (the text says 'because it is in dirhams until he can return' which is unclear; likely it means until he can claim it). The first opinion (that it is permissible) is considered more apparent (azhar) because the will is a new transfer, and dhimmis are bound by Islamic law in transactions. This demonstrates the complexity of cross-border bequests in classical Hanafi law.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who has a covenant (dhimma) guaranteeing protection and religious freedom.
مستأمن (musta'min): A harbi who has been granted safe conduct (aman) to enter or reside in Dar al-Islam temporarily.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon Hanafi view regarding a dhimmi's bequest to a musta'min harbi?
A) It is valid (al-awwal azhar)
B) It is invalid (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf)
C) It is valid only if the musta'min is present
D) It is invalid because the musta'min is a harbi

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that a dhimmi may validly bequeath to a musta'min harbi, as the musta'min is like a dhimmi in transactions.

Bequests to Non-Muslims of Different Religious Affiliations
Rulings on bequests involving non-Muslims of different categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on bequests to dhimmis of different religions, to harbis, and to musta'mins, and identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on bequests to harbis.

Core Idea
A bequest to a different-religion dhimmi is valid; to a harbi is invalid (with an alternative); to a musta'min follows two narrations.

The Hanafi school treats all non-Muslims as one community (kufr millah wahidah) for inheritance and bequests, but territorial differences (tabayun al-darayn) can block these rights. This passage from al‑Bahr al‑Rā’iq presents three cases: bequest to a dhimmi of another religion, to a harbī, and to a musta’min. Understanding these distinctions is essential for drafting valid wills in multi‑religious and cross‑border contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of non-Muslim legatees
The passage considers three types: a dhimmi of a different religion (khilāf al‑millah), a harbī (enemy combatant not under Muslim protection), and a musta’min (harbī with safe conduct).
2
State the ruling for each category
For a different‑religion dhimmi: valid (jāza). For a harbī: invalid (lā yajūz) according to the main view; an alternative narration permits it. For a musta’min: two narrations—one invalid, one valid.
3
Understand the legal basis: analogy with inheritance
The rule for bequests follows the rule for inheritance because the will is the 'sister' of inheritance (aqtuha). Inheritance between non-Muslims of different religions is allowed (since disbelief is one), so bequests are allowed. Inheritance between a dhimmi and a harbī is blocked by difference of territory, so bequest is blocked.
4
Locate the ikhtilāf within the Hanafi school
Imam Muhammad’s narration in al‑Jāmi‘ al‑Ṣaghīr permits bequest to a harbī by analogy with a Muslim. For the musta’min, the same two views apply. The author of al‑Bahr al‑Rā’iq does not choose between them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أوصى لخلاف ملته جاز اعتباراً بالإرث لأن الكفر كاملة واحدة. ولو أوصى لحربي لا يجوز لأن الإرث ممتنع كتباين الدارين فكذا الوصية لأنها أخته، وعلى رواية الجامع الصغير ينبغي أن تجوز كالمسلم. ولو أوصى لمستأمن في دار الإسلام ينبغي أن يكون على الروايتين المذكورتين في المسلم والله أعلم.
And if he bequeaths to someone of a different religion, it is permissible by analogy with inheritance, because disbelief is one and the same. And if he bequeaths to a harbi, it is not permissible because inheritance is prevented like the difference of the two abodes, so likewise the will because it is its sister. And according to the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, it should be permissible like a Muslim. And if he bequeaths to a musta'min in Dar al-Islam, it should be according to the two narrations mentioned regarding the Muslim. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This short passage presents three rulings on bequests between non-Muslims of different categories. First, a dhimmi may bequeath to another dhimmi of a different religion (e.g., a Jew to a Christian) because, in the context of disbelief, all are considered one community (kulluhum kafirun). This is analogous to inheritance, where difference of religion within non-Muslims does not prevent inheritance. Second, a dhimmi bequest to a harbi (an enemy not under Muslim protection) is invalid, just as inheritance between them is prevented due to difference of territory (tabayun al-darin). However, there is a narration from al-Jami' al-Saghir (one of the six core books of Zahir al-Riwayah by Imam Muhammad) that suggests it should be permissible, like a bequest to a Muslim. Third, a bequest to a musta'min (a harbi with safe conduct) residing in Dar al-Islam follows the same two narrations as for a Muslim: the general view disallows it (as stated earlier by Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and the alternative view allows it. The author does not resolve which is preferred, leaving it as 'God knows best'. The key principle is that bequests follow the same rules as inheritance regarding capacity and territorial considerations.

Key Terms
خلاف الملته (khilaf al-millah): Of a different religious community (e.g., Christian vs. Jew).
تباين الدارين (tabayun al-darayn): Difference of territory (Dar al-Islam vs. Dar al-Harb) preventing inheritance and some other legal relations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a dhimmi bequeathing to a harbi (enemy) not in Dar al-Islam?
A) Valid without restriction
B) Invalid because of difference of territory
C) Valid only if the bequest is less than one-third
D) Valid according to all narrations

Key Takeaway
Bequests to non-Muslims of different religions are valid; bequests to harbis are generally invalid, with a conflicting narration; and bequests to musta'mins follow the same ikhtilaf.

The Executor's Acceptance and Rejection of Executorship
Introduces the executor's acceptance/rejection rules and emphasizes the condition of the testator's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which an executor's rejection is effective and apply the rule to scenarios involving the testator's knowledge.

Core Idea
Rejection is only effective if the testator knows of it; uninformed rejection is not binding.

This lesson examines a foundational ruling for the executor (wasi) in Islamic inheritance law. The central issue is when an appointment becomes binding. The text from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that the testator's knowledge is the decisive factor for acceptance or rejection to take effect. Understanding this distinction prevents misunderstandings in practical estate administration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the appointment and rejection of an executor. The ruling is that writing a will is a strongly recommended sunnah, as indicated by the hadith. For the executor, acceptance or rejection in the testator's presence determines binding effect.
The hadith cited emphasizes the importance of having a will written.
2
Locate the condition for effective rejection
The passage states that rejection 'at his presence' (عنده) is effective. Al-Dhakhirah clarifies that this means with the testator's knowledge (بِعِلْمِهِ). Rejection without knowledge, even if physically present, is not effective.
The key distinction: physical presence vs. actual knowledge.
3
Understand the reasoning
The testator does not have authority to force the executor to act, and there is no excuse from the executor's side because the testator can appoint someone else. Therefore, if the testator knows of rejection, he can act accordingly.
The rationale protects the testator's ability to manage his affairs.
4
Note the source authority
The ruling is drawn from Zahir al-Riwayah, specifically from al-Mabsut and al-Dhakhirah. It is considered the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
This gives the ruling its weight in Hanafi fatwa.
5
Apply to a scenario
If an executor privately refuses the appointment without the testator's knowledge, the rejection is not binding, and the executor may later accept. If he refuses in the testator's presence (with knowledge), the rejection is final.
Practice distinguishing between public and private refusal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الوصي وما يملكه فرغ من بيان أحكام الموصى له شرع في بيان أحكام الموصى إليه وهو الوصي، وقدم أحكام الموصى له لكثرتها وكثرة وقوعها فكانت الحاجة إلى معرفتها أمس. قال رحمه الله : (ولو أوصى إلى رجل فقبل عنده ورد عنده يرتد يعني قبل عند الموصي لأن الموصي ليس له ولاية إلزامه التصرف ولا عذر من جهته لأنه يمكنه أن إلى غيره. قال في الذخيرة : المراد بـ«عنده يعني بعلمه ورده بغير علمه سواء كان عنده أو في مجلس غيره. قال في المبسوط : مسألة مشتملة على فصول : فصل في حق الإيصاء وكيفيته، وفصل في قبوله ورده، وفصل فيمن يجوز إليه الإيصاء ومن لا يجوز، وفصل في عزله. الرجل إذا حضر الموت ينبغي أن يوصي ويكتب وصيته لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «لا يحل لرجل يؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر يبيت إلا ووصيته تحت رأسه» (۱) ويكتب كتاب الوصية هذا ما أوصى فلان بن فلان فإنه يشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وأن محمداً عبده ورسوله وأن الجنة حق والنار حق، وأن الساعة آتية لا ريب فيه وأن الله يبعث من في القبور وأن صلاتي ونسكي ومحياي ومماتي لله رب العالمين لا شريك له وبذلك أمرت وأنا أول المسلمين. أي في هذه الوصية لما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال من كان آخر كلمته شهادة إلا لا إله إلا الله وأني رسول الله وجبت
Chapter of the executor and what he possesses. He finishes from explaining the rulings for the legatee and begins explaining the rulings for the one to whom the will is made, and that is the executor. And he prioritized the rulings of the legatee because of their abundance and frequent occurrence, so the need to know them was more urgent. He said, may God have mercy on him: (And if he appoints a man as executor, and he accepts in his presence and rejects in his presence, he is rejected. He meant accepts in the presence of the testator, because the testator does not have the authority to force him to act, and there is no excuse from his side because he can appoint someone else. He said in al-Dhakhirah: By 'in his presence' is meant with his knowledge, and rejection without his knowledge, whether he is present or in another gathering. He said in al-Mabsut: The issue includes several sections: Section on the right of appointment and its modality, Section on its acceptance and rejection, Section on who is eligible to be appointed and who is not, and Section on his dismissal. A man, when death approaches, should make a will and write his will because of his statement, peace be upon him: 'It is not permissible for a man who believes in Allah and the Last Day to spend the night except with his will under his head.' And he writes the document of the will: 'This is what so-and-so son of so-and-so wills: He bears witness that there is no god but Allah, and that Muhammad is His servant and messenger, and that Paradise is true and Hell is true, and that the Hour is coming, no doubt thereof, and that Allah will raise those in the graves, and that my prayer, my sacrifice, my life and my death are for Allah, Lord of the worlds, no partner has He, and with this I am commanded, and I am the first of the Muslims.' That is, in this will, because it is narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'Whoever has his last words the testimony that there is no god but Allah and that I am the Messenger of Allah, becomes obligatory...'
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter 'The Executor and What He Possesses' (Bab al-Wasi wa ma Yamlikuhu). The author explains the transition from discussing the legatee (musa lahu) to the executor (wasi). The first ruling: if a testator appoints a man as executor and the man accepts in the testator's presence, it is binding; if he rejects in his presence, he is considered to have rejected. The key distinction is whether the testator has knowledge. The source al-Dhakhirah clarifies that 'in his presence' means with his knowledge (bi-ilmihi), so rejection without the testator's knowledge (even if the executor is physically present) does not count as rejection. The Mabsut states the issue contains four sections. Then the author emphasizes the recommendation to write a will, citing a hadith: 'It is not permissible for a man who believes in Allah and the Last Day to spend the night except with his will under his head.' The recommended format includes the testimony of faith and other core beliefs. The hadith quoted at the end indicates that the last words should be the shahada. This passage sets the stage for detailed rules on the executor's appointment and duties.

Key Terms
وصي (wasi): The executor appointed by the testator to carry out the will.
الموصى إليه (al-musa ilayhi): The person to whom the will is made, i.e., the executor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is an executor's rejection of the appointment effective?
A) Only if done in writing
B) Only if done in the presence of witnesses
C) Only if done with the testator's knowledge (in his presence)
D) Only if done after the testator's death

Key Takeaway
The executor's rejection is only effective when the testator knows of it; uninformed rejection does not bind.

Witness Knowledge and Verbal Declaration in Wills
Conditions for valid testimony on a will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for valid testimony on a will and explain why nodding is insufficient.

Core Idea
Testimony without knowing the will's contents is invalid; a nod cannot replace speech when the testator can speak.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a crucial condition for testimony on a will: the witnesses must have actual knowledge of its contents. The author cites the Prophet's hadith requiring certainty like the sun. He then considers a scenario where the testator nods but does not speak — a common point of confusion. The Hanafi ruling clarifies that speech is required when the testator is capable, preserving the clarity and solemnity of the declaration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage deals with testifying (ishhad) on a will (wasiyyah). The ruling: testimony without knowledge is not permissible (la tajuuz).
This is a prohibition (haram) but the hukm is wajib for the witness to testify only when knowledgeable.
2
Locate the dalil
The Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wasallam) said: 'If you know like the sun, then testify, otherwise leave.'
The hadith is narrated in Bukhari and Abu Dawud; it is ahad but accepted in fiqh.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'ishhadu' (testify) is conditional on knowledge ('idha 'alimta). Hanafi usul: al-amr yufidu al-wujub, so testifying is obligatory only when knowledge is certain. The phrase 'like the sun' emphasizes extreme certainty.
Without certainty, the command to testify does not apply; instead, one must desist.
4
Analyze the specific case: nodding
If the testator nods 'yes' after the will is read but does not speak, the testimony is not valid. Why? Because the testator's declaration requires speech (nutq) when able.
Gestures only substitute for speech in cases of permanent incapacity (e.g., muteness).
5
Note the principle and thamarah
The principle: shurut (conditions) for valid testimony on a will: (1) witnesses know contents, (2) testator verbally declares. Practical consequence: if a testator only nods, the will cannot be executed based on that testimony.
This ensures the testator's intent is clearly expressed, avoiding disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما يصح الإشهاد إذا علم بما في الصك والشهادة على الوصية بدون العلم لا تجوز لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام للشاهد إذا علمت مثل الشمس فاشهدوا وإلا فدع ولو قال الشهود بعد ما قرؤوا الصك نشهد عليك فحرك رأسه بنعم ولم ينطق لم تجز شهادتهم
And testification is only valid if [the witnesses] know what is in the document; testimony about a will without knowledge is not permissible because of his saying, peace be upon him, to the witness: 'If you know like the sun, then testify, otherwise leave.' And if the witnesses said after they read the document, 'We testify against you,' and he moved his head with 'yes' but did not speak, their testimony is not valid.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for valid testimony on a will. The author states that witnesses must have actual knowledge of the contents of the will; testimony without knowledge is invalid. The proof is the Prophet's ḥadīth: 'If you know like the sun, then testify, otherwise leave.' Then a specific case is examined: if the testator merely nods his head in affirmation after the witnesses read the document but does not utter any words, the testimony is not accepted. This is because speech (nuṭq) is required for the testator's declaration; a nod does not substitute for spoken words when the testator is capable of speech. The ruling is based on the principle that gestures are only accepted as a substitute when there is permanent incapacity, not temporary.

Key Terms
الإشهاد (al-ishhād): The act of making witnesses testify to a will or document.
الشهادة على الوصية (al-shahāda ʿalā al-waṣiyya): Testimony given regarding the contents or execution of a will.
الإشارة بالرأس (al-ishāra bil-ra's): Nodding the head as a gesture of affirmation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the testator verbally disowned the will.
C) Because speech is required when the testator is capable of speaking.
D) Because nodding is only valid if the witnesses are relatives.

Key Takeaway
Valid testimony on a will requires the witness to know the contents and the testator to verbally declare consent; a nod is insufficient when speech is possible.

Gestures of a Temporary Mute: Not Equivalent to Speech
Dispute over whether gestures replace speech in will testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school distinguishes between permanent and temporary muteness in accepting gestures as valid legal expression, and evaluate the qiyas argument of the opposing school.

Core Idea
Temporary muteness lacks despair and established signs, so gestures remain ambiguous and invalid.

This passage presents a key dispute between the Hanafi school and both Abū Yūsuf and al-Shāfiʿī regarding the legal validity of gestures when a person temporarily loses speech. While the permanent mute's gestures are accepted due to custom, the temporary case is distinguished by the possibility of recovery and absence of a history of signs. We will examine the qiyas reasoning and the Hanafi response, and note an intra-Hanafi variation for prolonged conditions.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad: gestures of a temporary mute (mu'taqil al-lisān) are not valid substitutes for speech in legal acts like wills.
This is the muftā bihī for short-term conditions.
2
Identify the opposing position and its proof
Abū Yūsuf and al-Shāfiʿī hold that gestures are valid, using qiyās to the permanent mute and analogy to prayer where inability to recite is excused.
The opposing dalīl relies on the common illa of inability to speak.
3
Analyze the qiyas and its rejection
The Hanafi response refines the illa: the key factor is not mere inability but despair of recovery (ya's) and existence of established signs. Temporary muteness lacks both.
The prayer analogy is dismissed as specific to worship, not general legal expression.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on prolonged condition
If the temporary condition lasts a long time (e.g., days), some scholars (including a narration from Abū Ḥanīfa) accept gestures because customary signs develop.
The prolonged case is treated like a permanent mute by some later authorities.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in a will or testimony, a temporarily mute testator's gestures are not accepted in the relied-upon Hanafi position, but would be accepted by Abū Yūsuf and, for prolonged cases, by some Hanafis.
This affects validity of legal transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن اعتقل واحتبس لسانه روي عن أبي يوسف أنه تجوز وتعتبر إشارته وهو قول الشافعي له أن الإشارة تقوم مقام العبارة حاله عجزه عن النطق والعبادة قياساً على الأخرس لأن العجز عن النطق متى تحقق يستوي فيه العارض والأصلي فيما تتعلق صحته بالنطق كالعجز عن القراءة فإنه تجوز صلاة الأخرس بغير قراءة وتجوز صلاة من اعتقل لسانه بغير قراءة فكذا هذا ولنا أن الأشارة تدل على النطق والعبادة إنما تتصل إلى البدل حالة الياس عن النطق وهنا لم يقع اليأس عن النطق لأن اعتقال لسانه واحتباسه لا يدوم بل بعرض الزوال والانتقال في كل ساعة فلا تقوم الإشارة مقام العبارة، وأن الإشارة محتملة غير معلمة إلا أن في الأخرس تقدم منه إشارات مفهومة وآلة واضحة على مراداته الباطنة فزال الاحتمال عن إشاراته فقامت مقام نطقه وعبارته، وهنا لم يتقدم منه لم إشارات معلومة حتى يعلم بإشاراته مراداته فبقت إشارته محتملة غير مفهمة فلا تقوم مقام عبارته، فإما إذا طالت الغفلة أو الحبسة في لسانه ودام هل تعتبر أشارته؟ اختلف المشايخ فيه، قيل لا تعتبر اعتباراً للمعنى الأول وهو أنه لم يقع اليأس عن النطق فلا تقوم إشارته مقام عبارته، وقيل تعتبر وقد روي هذا أبو عمر والصغاني عن أبي حنيفة اعتباراً للمعنى الثاني لأنه لما طالت الغفلة صار له إشارة معهودة فتقوم مقام النطق كما في الأخرس
If his tongue becomes tied and restrained, it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that it is permissible and his gesture is considered, and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i because gesture stands in place of expression in his state of inability to speak and [perform] worship, by analogy to the mute, because when the inability to speak is realized, the temporary and permanent are equal in matters whose validity depends on speech, like inability to recite: the mute's prayer without recitation is valid, and the prayer of one whose tongue is tied without recitation is valid, so likewise this. For us [Hanafis], gesture indicates speech, and worship is only connected to the substitute in the state of despair of speech, and here despair of speech has not occurred because the tying and retention of his tongue does not last but is subject to cessation and change at any moment, so gesture does not stand in place of expression. And gesture is ambiguous and not informative, except that in the case of the mute, understandable gestures and clear signs of his inner intentions have preceded from him, so ambiguity is removed from his gestures, so they stand in place of his speech and expression. Here, no known gestures have preceded from him by which his intentions could be known, so his gesture remains ambiguous and incomprehensible, so it does not stand in place of his expression. As for when the suspension or retention in his tongue is prolonged and continues, is his gesture considered? The mashayikh differed: some said it is not considered, based on the first meaning that despair of speech has not occurred, so his gesture does not stand in place of expression. Some said it is considered, and this was narrated by Abu 'Umar and al-Saghani from Abu Hanifa, based on the second meaning: when the suspension is prolonged, he has customary gestures, so they stand in place of speech as in the mute.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the legal effect of gestures by a person who temporarily loses the ability to speak (mu'taqil al-lisān). Abū Yūsuf and Imam al-Shāfi‘ī hold that gestures are valid because the inability to speak is analogous to a permanent mute; they support this by analogy to prayer where both a mute and a temporarily speechless person may pray without recitation. The Ḥanafī position (attributed to Abū Ḥanīfa and Imām Muḥammad) is that gestures do not substitute for speech in such a case because: (1) there is no despair of recovery; (2) the temporary nature means that customary gestures have not been established. A later opinion within the school holds that if the condition becomes prolonged (e.g., days), then gestures may be accepted because they become an established means of communication. The passage thus highlights both inter- and intra- Ḥanafī ikhtilāf.

Key Terms
الأخرس (al-akhras): A person who is permanently unable to speak.
اعتقال اللسان (i'tiqāl al-lisān): Temporary loss of speech due to illness or accident.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Ḥanafī school (Abū Ḥanīfa), why are gestures of a person with temporary speech loss not accepted?
A) Because analogy to the mute is invalid.
B) Because there is no despair of recovery and no established signs.
C) Because the testator is not in a state of worship.
D) Because the witnesses must hear the words.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, gestures of a temporarily speechless person are not accepted as substitutes for speech unless the condition becomes prolonged and customary signs develop.

Scope of an Executor’s Authority in Hanafi Law
Scope of executor's authority when appointment is general

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the scope of an executor's authority and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
An executor appointed for some matters is executor for all matters by default in the Hanafi school.

When a testator appoints an executor, does the executor’s authority extend only to the explicitly mentioned matters or to all matters of the estate? The Ḥanafī school holds that appointment for some matters implies appointment for all, based on the testator’s presumed trust and the need to avoid multiple appointments. The Shāfi‘ī school restricts authority to what is explicitly stated. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify exactly what the Hanafi school holds: appointment for wealth or for some matters makes the executor executor over all matters, including children.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that the Shafi'i school disagrees, limiting authority to the explicitly mentioned matters.
Imam al-Shafi‘i is the opposing authority.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof
Analyze the Hanafi reasoning: necessity (no need for another executor) and the testator's satisfaction with the chosen person.
This is a qiyās jaliy (clear analogy).
4
Examine the Shafi‘i response and Hanafi rebuttal
Understand the Shafi‘i reliance on literal wording and the Hanafi counter that limiting would contradict the testator's intent.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: under Ḥanafī, one executor handles all; under Shāfi‘ī, multiple appointments may be needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أوصى إلى رجل في ماله كان وصياً فيه وفي ولده، وإذا أوصى إليه في أنواع وسكت عن نوع في نوع يكون وصياً في الأنواع كلها عندنا خلافاً للشافعي لأنه لو لم تعم وصايته تقع الحاجة إلى نصب وصي آخر فجعل من اختاره الميت وصياً ببعض أموره وصياً في كلها فالوصي أولى من جعل غيره وصياً لأن الموصي لم يرض بتصرف غيره في شيء من الأمور ورضي في بعض الأمور لأنها استصلحه واستصوبه في الوصاية فكون هذا وصياً على العموم أولى
And if he appointed a man as executor over his wealth, he is executor over it and over his children. And if he appointed him over types and was silent about a type, he is executor over all types according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i, because if his executorship did not encompass, there would be need to appoint another executor. So making the one chosen by the deceased as executor for some of his affairs an executor for all is better because the testator was not pleased with the disposal of someone else in any matters, and was pleased [with this one] in some matters because he deemed him suitable and proper for executorship, so making this one an executor generally is better.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage addresses the scope of an executor's authority. The Ḥanafī school holds that if a testator appoints an executor for his wealth or for specific types of matters, the executor's authority covers all matters of the estate and the children (if there are minor children). This is based on the principle that the testator's choice indicates trust, and limiting the authority would require additional appointments, which is less desirable. Imam al-Shāfi‘ī restricts the authority to the explicitly mentioned matters. The Ḥanafīs argue that since the testator did not want anyone else to act, the chosen executor should have comprehensive authority to avoid needless complications.

Key Terms
الوصاية (al-waṣāyah): The office or authority of an executor (waṣī).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Ḥanafī school, what is the scope of an executor appointed to handle 'the wealth' of the testator?
A) Only the wealth, not the children.
B) Only what is explicitly mentioned in the will.
C) Both the wealth and the children.
D) Only the debts and funeral expenses.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, appointing an executor for some matters makes him executor for all matters, unless the testator explicitly restricts his authority.

Conditional and Time-Limited Executorship
Ruling on conditional and time-limited executorship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional executorship and identify when the first executor is dismissed upon the fulfillment of a condition or time limit.

Core Idea
Executorship can be conditioned or time-limited like agency.

In Hanafi fiqh, a testator may appoint an executor for a limited time or until a specified event occurs. This lesson explains the validity of such appointments, drawing an analogy to agency (wakālah). The key rule: upon the arrival of the condition or time limit, the first executor is automatically dismissed and the named successor takes over, provided the wording clearly indicates a condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Recognize that conditional and time-limited executorship is valid (sahīḥ) in the Hanafi school. The testator's words are effective as spoken.
E.g., 'You are executor until Zayd arrives.'
2
Understand the Analogy to Agency
Executorship is likened to agency (wakālah) or appointment of guardianship. Both are inherently susceptible to time restrictions and conditions. This analogy justifies the ruling.
Just as an agent can be appointed 'until a certain date,' so can an executor.
3
Apply the Rule to Different Wordings
If the testator says 'until Zayd arrives,' the first executor is dismissed when Zayd arrives, and Zayd becomes executor. If the testator instead says 'I appoint 'Umar, then when Zayd arrives I appoint Zayd,' 'Umar is also dismissed, because the condition is implicit.
Al-Quduri mentioned that in some formulations, the first remains joint executor, but the dominant narrations support dismissal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال لفلان وصي إلى أن يقدم فلان فهو كما قال، وذكر القدوري الأول وصي مع الثاني ولا يصح تخصيصه بزمان دون زمان وجه ظاهر الرواية أن الإيصاء قابل للتوقيت لأنه توكيل أو إثبات ولاية وكلا الأمرين قابل للتوقيت فيتوقت وصاية الأول بقدوم فلان، فإذا قدم فلان انعزل الأول كما لو وكل وكيلاً إلى أن يقدم فلان وصار الثاني وصياً لأنه علق وصية الأول بالشرط وتعليق الأيصاء بالشرط جائز لأنها وكالة، وتعليق الوكالة والنيابة بالشرط جائز كما لو قال إن سافرت فأنت وكيلي في أمري كما لو قال أوصيت صح إلى عمر وما لم يقدم زيد وسكت فقدم زيد كان عمرو وصياً بعد قدوم زيد وكان أقام عمراً وصياً لأنه مختار الميت ووصيه أولى من إقامة غيره بخلاف ما لو قال أوصيت إلى عمر يقدم زيد فإذا قدم زيد فقد أوصيت إلى زيد كان كما قال لأنه لم يبق عمرو وصياً معه بعد قدوم زيد
And if he said: 'So-and-so is executor until So-and-so arrives,' it is as he said. Al-Quduri mentioned that the first [executor] is [joint] executor with the second, and it is not valid to specify him for a time without a time. The apparent meaning of the narration is that appointment is subject to timing because it is agency or establishment of guardianship, and both are subject to timing. So the executorship of the first is terminated by the arrival of So-and-so. When So-and-so arrives, the first is dismissed, just as if he appointed an agent until So-and-so arrives, and the second becomes executor because he conditioned the executorship of the first upon a condition, and conditioning executorship upon a condition is permissible because it is agency. And conditioning agency and deputyship upon a condition is permissible, as if he said: 'If you travel, you are my agent in my affairs,' just as if he said: 'I appoint you executor,' it is valid for 'Umar until Zayd arrives, and if he remains silent and Zayd arrives, 'Amr is executor after Zayd's arrival, and 'Amr was appointed executor because he is the deceased's chosen one, and his executor is better than appointing someone else, unlike if he said: 'I appoint 'Umar as executor, then Zayd arrives, then I have appointed Zayd as executor,' it is as he said because 'Umar does not remain executor with him after Zayd's arrival.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage clarifies that executorship (waṣāyah) can be limited by time or conditioned on a future event, as it is a form of agency (wakālah) or appointment of guardianship (ithbāt wilāyah). Both are inherently susceptible to time restrictions and conditions. The example given: if the testator says, 'So-and-so is executor until Zayd arrives,' then upon Zayd's arrival, the first executor is automatically dismissed and Zayd becomes the executor. This is analogous to agency where an agent can be appointed for a specific period. The passage also discusses a nuance: if the testator says, 'I appoint 'Umar as executor, then [later] Zayd arrives, I have appointed Zayd,' then 'Umar is not dismissed because the testator did not condition his dismissal on Zayd's arrival; rather he appointed both. But if the wording is 'until Zayd arrives,' then 'Umar's term ends. Al-Quduri's mention that 'the first is executor with the second' suggests a joint executorship in some formulations.

Key Terms
التوقيت (al-tawqīt): Limiting a legal arrangement to a specific time period or condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the first executor if the testator says 'You are executor until Zayd arrives' and Zayd arrives?
A) The first executor remains executor alongside Zayd.
B) The first executor is automatically dismissed.
C) The first executor remains, and Zayd is not executor.
D) The testator must make a new appointment.

Key Takeaway
Executorship can be validly conditioned on a future event or limited to a time, with automatic dismissal upon fulfillment.

Acceptance and Binding Nature of Executorship
Necessity of acceptance and effect of rejection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on acceptance and rejection of executorship, and distinguish when rejection is valid and when it is not.

Core Idea
Once an executor accepts, he cannot reject because the testator relied on him — harm to the testator is prevented.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq explains that executorship (waṣāyah) is a voluntary undertaking that becomes binding once accepted, to protect the testator’s trust and prevent harm. The ruling balances the executor’s freedom to decline initially with the binding nature after commitment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act
The act is accepting the role of executor (waṣī). The passage establishes that acceptance is necessary because the executor is volunteering for a task with potential liability (al-'uhdah).
Acceptance can be by word or by action (e.g., paying a debt).
2
Understand the necessity of acceptance
Since the executor is undertaking a voluntary responsibility that may cause him harm (liability), his consent is required. Without acceptance, he cannot be forced into the role.
This respects the principle that no one can be compelled to take on a liability.
3
Determine the effect of acceptance after death
Once the executor accepts (either before or after the testator's death), the executorship becomes binding. He cannot subsequently reject it, because that would harm the testator who relied on his commitment.
The testator appointed someone he trusted; allowing rejection after death would defeat the purpose.
4
Recognize the exception for rejection during life
If the executor accepted during the testator’s life, he may reject it face-to-face with the testator. This is valid because the testator can then appoint someone else without harm.
Rejection must be in the testator’s presence or with his knowledge to avoid deception (ghurūr).
5
Note the option when no acceptance during life
If the executor did not accept during the testator’s life, he has a choice after death: he may accept or reject. Here the testator is considered negligent for not securing acceptance, so no harm is caused.
The testator could have asked the executor to accept during life; failing that, he bears the risk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بد من قبول الموصى له لأنه متبرع بالعمل له ويلحقه ضرر العهدة فلا بد من قبوله والتزامه. وإذا أوصى إليه فقبل قبل موته أو بعده ثم رد لم يخرج لأن الموصي ما أوصى إلا إلى من يعتمد عليه من والأمناء، فلو اعتبر القبول بعد الموت فربما لا يقبل فلا يحصل غرضه وهو الوصي الذي اختاره. وقيل : لو صح رده بعد الموت تضرر به وصار مغروراً من جهته لأنه اعتمد على قبوله بأن يقوم بجميع التصرفات بعد وفاته والوصي بقبول الوصاية التزم ذلك بمحضر منه، فلو صح رده وقع الموصي في ضرر ويصير مغروراً من جهة الوصي فصارت الوصاية لازمة عليه شرعاً بالتزامه نظراً للموصي دفعاً للضرر عنه بخلاف الوصية بالمال لأن ثمة لو لم يصح رده بعد موته لا يتضرر الميت لأنه يعود الثلث إلى الورثة بل الضرر على الموصى له. ولو قبل في حياة الموصي ثم رده في حياته مواجهة يصح ولا يصح بدون محضر الموصي أو علمه لما فيه من الغرور كما في الوكيل لأن الموصي طلب منه الإلتزام بعد الوفاة لا حالة الحياة ولا يمكنه في الأخيرة أن يوصي إلى غيره فتضرر به، ولو لم يقبل في حياته فهو بالخيار بعد موته إن شاء قبل، وإن شاء رد لأن هناك الميت مغرور وهنا ليس كذلك لأنه يمكنه أن يسأل أن يقبله أو لا يقبله، فإذا لم يفعل واعتمد على أنه يقبله بعد موته ولم يوص إلى غيره فقد قصر في أمره فصار مغتراً من جهة نفسه لا مغروراً من جهة الوصي والقبول تارة يكون بالقبول وتارة الفعل، فالقبول بالفعل كتنفيذ في وصيته أو شراء شيء للورثة أو قضاء دين كقبوله بالقول إذا الوصاية قد تمت وتقررت بموت الموصي شرعاً فإنها لا تقبل البطلان من جهة الموصي إلا أن للموصى له ولاية الرد حتى لا يلزمه ضرر الوصاية بغير رضاه وليس من صيرورته وصياً
And acceptance by the legatee [executor] is necessary because he is volunteering to work and is subject to the harm of liability; so acceptance and commitment are necessary. And if the testator appointed him and he accepted before his death or after, then rejected, it does not remove him because the testator only appointed someone he relied upon from among the trustworthy. If acceptance were considered after death, perhaps he would not accept, so the testator's purpose would not be achieved, and he is the executor he chose. Some said: if his rejection after death were valid, the testator would be harmed and become deceived by him because he relied on his acceptance to carry out all dispositions after his death, and the executive by accepting the executorship committed to that in his presence. So if his rejection were valid, the testator would fall into harm and become deceived by the executor; so executorship becomes binding upon him legally by his commitment, out of consideration for the testator to repel harm, unlike a bequest of property because there if rejection after death were not valid, the deceased would not be harmed since the third returns to the heirs; rather the harm is on the legatee. And if he accepted during the testator's life then rejected it face-to-face during his life, it is valid, and it is not valid without the testator's presence or knowledge because of the deception involved, as with an agent, because the testator asked him to commit after death, not during life, and in the latter case he can appoint someone else, so he would be harmed. And if he did not accept during his life, he has choice after his death: if he wishes he accepts, and if he wishes he rejects, because there the deceased is deceived, and here it is not so because he could have asked him to accept or not. So if he did not act and relied on his accepting after death and did not appoint someone else, he has been negligent; thus he becomes self-deceived, not deceived by the executor. And acceptance is sometimes by speech and sometimes by action; acceptance by action is like executing his will or buying something for the heirs or paying a debt, like acceptance by speech when the executorship has been completed and established legally by the testator's death, for it does not admit annulment from the testator's side, but the legatee has authority to reject so that he is not forced into the liability of executorship unwillingly, and it is not like his becoming executor.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage explains that acceptance (qabūl) by the appointed executor is a necessary condition for the executorship to take effect. The executor is undertaking a voluntary responsibility with potential liability (al-'uhdah), so his consent is required. Once he accepts (either before or after the testator's death), he cannot subsequently reject it unilaterally, because that would harm the testator who relied on him. However, if he accepts during the testator's life and then rejects face-to-face with the testator, it is valid, provided it is in the testator's presence or with his knowledge, to avoid deception. If he never accepted during life, he has the option after death to accept or reject. The passage also notes that acceptance can be by action, such as carrying out some duties of executorship (e.g., paying debts), which is equivalent to verbal acceptance. The ruling distinguishes between executorship (waṣāyah) and a mere bequest of property (wasiyyah bil-māl): in the latter, rejection after death by the legatee is valid because it does not harm the deceased (the property reverts to heirs), whereas in executorship, harm would be caused.

Key Terms
العهدة (al-'uhdah): Legal liability and responsibility that the executor assumes for the proper management of the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if an executor accepts the executorship before the testator's death but then wishes to reject it after the testator's death?
A) He may reject it freely because he is not bound until after death.
B) He cannot reject because it would harm the testator who relied on his acceptance.
C) He can reject only if he returns any compensation received.
D) He must seek court permission to reject.

Key Takeaway
Once an executor accepts the role (by word or action), he is bound to fulfill it; rejection after death is invalid to protect the testator's reliance and prevent harm.

Priority of General Executor and Validity of Time-Limited Executorship
Ruling on priority and conditional executorship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that a general executor takes precedence over a specific executor, and explain why a time-limited executorship is valid in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A general executor is preferred; appointments can be conditional like agency.

When a testator appoints multiple executors, the one appointed for all affairs (general) takes priority over one appointed for specific tasks. Additionally, the testator may set a time limit or condition for the executorship, such as 'until so-and-so arrives.' This lesson explains these rules and their legal basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage covers two scenarios: (1) a general executor vs a specific executor, and (2) a time-limited appointment. Identify each and note the ruling.
The general executor is 'first' in priority.
2
Analyze the reasoning for priority
Why does a general executor take precedence? The testator's trust is broader. The passage says the testator 'was pleased' with the general executor for all matters, whereas the specific executor was approved only for some.
The basis is the testator's intention and trust.
3
Examine the time-limited appointment
The passage gives examples: 'Zaid is my executor until Amr arrives.' Upon Amr's arrival, Zaid's executorship ends and Amr becomes executor. This is valid because executorship is like agency (wakalah) or guardianship (wilayah), both of which can be conditioned.
Al-Quduri is cited to support this.
4
Understand the condition of acceptance
The appointed executor must accept the role because it involves work and potential liability. Without acceptance, the appointment is not binding.
Acceptance (qubul) is a necessary condition.
5
Contrast with other formulations
The passage distinguishes between 'I bequeath to Amr as long as Zaid has not arrived' (Amr remains executor after Zaid arrives) and other phrasings that automatically transfer executorship.
The exact wording matters for the legal outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولى من جعل غيره وصياً لأن الموصي لم يرض بتصرف غيره في شيء من الأمور ورضي في بعض الأمور لأنها استصلحه واستصوبه في الوصاية فكون هذا وصياً على العموم أولى ولو قال لفلان وصي إلى أن يقدم فلان فهو كما قال، وذكر القدوري الأول وصي مع الثاني ولا يصح تخصيصه بزمان دون زمان وجه ظاهر الرواية أن الإيصاء قابل للتوقيت لأنه توكيل أو إثبات ولاية وكلا الأمرين قابل للتوقيت فيتوقت وصاية الأول بقدوم فلان، فإذا قدم فلان انعزل الأول كما لو وكل وكيلاً إلى أن يقدم فلان وصار الثاني وصياً لأنه علق وصية الأول بالشرط وتعليق الإيصاء بالشرط جائز لأنها وكالة، وتعليق الوكالة والنيابة بالشرط جائز كما لو قال إن سافرت فأنت وكيلي في أمري كما لو قال أوصيت صح إلى عمر وما لم يقدم زيد وسكت فقدم زيد كان عمرو وصياً بعد قدوم زيد وكان أقام عمراً وصياً لأنه مختار الميت ووصيه أولى من إقامة غيره بخلاف ما لو قال أوصيت إلى عمر يقدم زيد فإذا قدم زيد فقد أوصيت إلى زيد كان كما قال لأنه لم يبق عمرو وصياً معه بعد قدوم زيد فإنه لا يحتاج إلى إقامة من ليس بمختار الميت مقام عمرو، ولا بد من قبول الموصى له لأنه متبرع بالعمل له ويلحقه ضرر العهدة فلا بد من قبوله والتزامه.
It is more proper that one who has been made an executor generally is more entitled than one who has been made an executor for specific matters, because the testator was not pleased with the disposal of someone else in any of the matters, and he was pleased in some matters because he considered him suitable and approved him for executorship. So if he said, 'So-and-so is my executor until so-and-so arrives,' it is as he said. Al-Quduri mentions: the first is an executor along with the second, and it is not valid to restrict him to a time without a time. The apparent reason of the narration is that bequest (al-isa') is subject to time specification because it is a deputyship or proof of guardianship, and both are subject to time specification. So the executorship of the first is timed with the arrival of so-and-so. When so-and-so arrives, the first is dismissed, just as if he appointed an agent until so-and-so arrives. The second becomes an executor because he made the executorship of the first conditional upon a condition, and conditioning the bequest upon a condition is permissible because it is a deputyship. Conditioning deputyship and representation upon a condition is permissible, as if he said, 'If you travel, then you are my agent in my affairs.' As if he said, 'I have bequeathed to Amr as long as Zaid has not arrived,' and he remained silent. Then Zaid arrived; Amr is an executor after Zaid's arrival, and he (the testator) had appointed Amr as an executor because he is the chosen one of the deceased, and his executor is more entitled than the appointment of another. Unlike if he said, 'I have bequeathed to Amr; Zaid has arrived; so I have bequeathed to Zaid' – it is as he said because Amr does not remain an executor with him after Zaid's arrival. So there is no need to appoint someone who is not the chosen one of the deceased in place of Amr. And the acceptance of the legatee is necessary because he is donating his work, and the liability of responsibility affects him, so his acceptance and commitment are necessary.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explaining the rules of appointing an executor. It covers two main points. First, a general executor (appointed for all affairs) takes precedence over a specific executor (appointed for certain matters). The reason is that the testator's trust in the general executor is more comprehensive. Second, an appointment can be made conditional upon a time or event. For example, if the testator says 'Zaid is my executor until Amr arrives,' upon Amr's arrival, Zaid's executorship ends and Amr becomes the executor. The author cites Al-Quduri to affirm that the first remains an executor alongside the second until the condition is fulfilled. The legal reasoning (wajh al-dalalah) is that executorship (isa') is akin to agency (wakalah) or guardianship (wilayah), both of which can be time-bound by conditions. The passage also emphasizes that the nominated executor must accept the appointment, because it entails work and potential liability. Without acceptance, the appointment is not effective.

Key Terms
وصي (wasi): A person appointed by the testator to execute his will and manage his affairs after death.
إيصاء (isa'): The act of making a bequest or appointing an executor by the testator.
توقيت (tawqit): Specifying a time limit or condition for the validity of the executorship.
قبول (qubul): The acceptance of executorship by the nominated person, which is necessary for it to take effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if a testator says 'Zaid is my executor until Amr arrives' and then Amr arrives?
A) Zaid remains executor, and Amr is not appointed.
B) Zaid's executorship ends, and Amr becomes executor.
C) Both Zaid and Amr are executors jointly.
D) The appointment becomes invalid.

Key Takeaway
A general executor takes precedence, and executorship can be conditional (like agency) and requires acceptance.
