6 min
Acceptance and Rejection of Executorship in Hanafi Law
Explanation of when rejection binds and when it's valid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when an executor's acceptance becomes binding and identify when rejection is valid or invalid.

Core Idea
Once accepted, executorship binds after death; rejection only valid face-to-face during lifetime.

The following passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the binding nature of executorship. The testator relies on the executor's acceptance, so rejecting after death would harm the testator. However, if the executor never accepted during the testator's life, he retains the option after death. This lesson walks through the scenarios and the effect of actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline rule
Read the first part: if the executor accepts before or after death and then rejects, he does not get out. Note that rejection after death is generally invalid due to harm to the testator.
The testator's trust is the basis.
2
Examine the exception: rejection during lifetime
Find where the text says rejection face-to-face during the testator's life is valid. Understand why: the testator can then appoint another, avoiding deception (ghurur).
The testator's presence is necessary.
3
Distinguish acceptance by action
Look for the statement that acceptance can be by action (e.g., paying debts, selling estate goods). Once such actions occur, rejection becomes impossible because the actions cannot be undone.
Action implies consent beyond words.
4
Contrast with a bequest of property
Note the comparison: in a bequest, rejection does not harm the testator because the property returns to heirs. In executorship, rejection harms the testator's plans.
This reinforces the special binding nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أوصى إليه فقبل قبل موته أو بعده ثم رد لم يخرج لأن الموصي ما أوصى إلا إلى من يعتمد عليه من والأمناء، فلو اعتبر القبول بعد الموت فربما لا يقبل فلا يحصل غرضه وهو الوصي الذي اختاره. ما لم الأصدقاء وقيل : لو صح رده بعد الموت تضرر به وصار مغروراً من جهته لأنه اعتمد على قبوله بأن يقوم بجميع التصرفات بعد وفاته والوصي بقبول الوصاية التزم ذلك بمحضر منه، فلو صح رده وقع الموصي في ضرر ويصير مغروراً من جهة الوصي فصارت الوصاية لازمة عليه شرعاً بالتزامه نظراً للموصي دفعاً للضرر عنه بخلاف الوصية بالمال لأن ثمة لو لم يصح رده بعد موته لا يتضرر الميت لأنه يعود الثلث إلى الورثة بل الضرر على الموصى له. ولو قبل في حياة الموصي ثم رده في حياته مواجهة يصح ولا يصح بدون محضر الموصي أو علمه لما فيه من الغرور كما في الوكيل لأن الموصي طلب منه الإلتزام بعد الوفاة لا حالة الحياة ولا يمكنه في الأخيرة أن يوصي إلى غيره فتضرر به، ولو لم يقبل في حياته فهو بالخيار بعد موته إن شاء قبل، وإن شاء رد لأن هناك الميت مغرور وهنا ليس كذلك لأنه يمكنه أن يسأل أن يقبله أو لا يقبله، فإذا لم يفعل واعتمد على أنه يقبله بعد موته ولم يوص إلى غيره فقد قصر في أمره فصار مغتراً من جهة نفسه لا مغروراً من جهة الوصي والقبول تارة يكون بالقبول وتارة الفعل، فالقبول بالفعل كتنفيذ في وصيته أو شراء شيء للورثة أو قضاء دين كقبوله بالقول إذا الوصاية قد تمت وتقررت بموت الموصي شرعاً فإنها لا تقبل البطلان من جهة الموصي إلا أن للموصى له ولاية الرد حتى لا يلزمه ضرر الوصاية بغير رضاه وليس من صيرورته وصياً بغير علمه ضرر على الوصي إذا كانت له ولاية الرد والإبطال كمن أقر لغيره بمال يثبت حكمه حتى لو مات المقر قبل القبول توقف على قبول المقر له، فإذا تصرف الوصي في التركة تصرفاً يدل على قبوله تلزمه الوصاية لأنه لا يقدر على الرد إلا برد التصرف ولا يمكنه رد التصرف فلا يبقى له الرد لزمته الوصاية ضرورة.
If he (the testator) appointed him, and he accepted before his death or after, then he rejected, he does not get out because the testator only appointed someone he trusts. If acceptance were considered after death, perhaps he would not accept and the testator's purpose—the executor he chose—would not be achieved. It is said: If his rejection after death were valid, the testator would be harmed and would be deceived by him because he relied on his acceptance to carry out all transactions after his death, and the executor by accepting executorship committed to that in his presence. If his rejection were valid, the testator would fall into harm and become deceived by the executor. So executorship becomes binding upon him legally by his commitment, out of consideration for the testator and to repel harm from him. Unlike a bequest of property, because there if his rejection after death were not valid, the deceased would not be harmed because the one-third returns to the heirs; rather the harm is on the legatee. If he accepted during the testator's life, then rejected it face-to-face during his life, it is valid, but it is not valid without the testator's presence or knowledge because of the deception involved, as in the case of an agent. Because the testator requested his commitment after death, not during life, and he cannot in the latter case appoint another, thus he is harmed by it. If he did not accept during his life, he has the option after his death: if he wishes, he may accept; if he wishes, he may reject. Because there the deceased is deceived, and here it is not so, because the testator could ask him to accept or not. If he did not do so and relied on him accepting after death and did not appoint another, he has fallen short in his matter, so he is deceived from his own side, not by the executor. Acceptance is sometimes by verbal acceptance and sometimes by action. Acceptance by action is like implementing his will, buying something for the heirs, or paying a debt, like acceptance by words. When executorship is complete and established by the testator's death legally, it does not accept annulment from the testator's side, but the appointee has the right of rejection so that the harm of executorship does not befall him without his consent. And him becoming an executor without his knowledge does not harm the executor if he has the right of rejection and annulment, like one who admits to another's property; its ruling is established. If the executor acts in the estate in a way that indicates his acceptance, the executorship becomes binding on him because he cannot reject except by reversing the action, and he cannot reverse the action, so he no longer has the right of rejection, and it becomes binding of necessity.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling statement on the acceptance and rejection of executorship. It begins by stating that if a person accepts executorship before or after the testator's death, then later tries to reject it, the rejection is not effective. The reason is that the testator relied on his acceptance; allowing rejection after death would harm the testator's interest and deceive him. However, if the rejection is made face-to-face with the testator during his lifetime, it is valid. But if done without the testator's knowledge, it is invalid due to deception. If the nominee did not accept during the testator's lifetime, he has the option after death to accept or reject. Once he performs an action indicating acceptance (e.g., selling estate property, paying a debt), the executorship becomes binding and he cannot reject. This is because he cannot undo those actions. The text also compares executorship to a bequest of property: in the latter, rejection does not harm the testator because the property returns to the heirs, but in executorship, rejection would harm the testator's plans.

Key Terms
رد (radd): Rejection of executorship after acceptance. It is generally not valid after the testator's death.
غرور (ghurur): Deception or delusion; here it refers to the testator being misled by the executor's acceptance.
قبول بالفعل (qubul bi-l-fi'l): Acceptance by action, such as selling estate goods or paying debts, which makes the executorship binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does an executor lose the right to reject executorship?
A) When he verbally accepts before the testator's death.
B) When he performs an action indicating acceptance, like paying a debt from the estate.
C) When the testator dies.
D) When he rejects face-to-face during the testator's life.

Key Takeaway
An executor's acceptance becomes binding after the testator's death, and rejection is only valid if done face-to-face during the testator's lifetime.

Three Categories of Executors and Judge's Authority
Classification of executors and judge's authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of executors in Hanafi law and state the judge's authority regarding each type.

Core Idea
A judge may remove a corrupt executor but must retain a trustworthy one, adding a co-executor if the executor is incapable.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq classifies executors into three categories and clarifies the judge's power to remove or add to them. Understanding these categories helps protect the deceased's estate and the rights of heirs.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the three-fold classification
Identify the three types: trustworthy and capable, trustworthy but incapable, corrupt and feared for property.
These are based on the executor's character and ability.
2
Understand the ruling for each type
For trustworthy and capable: judge does not remove. For trustworthy but incapable: judge adds a co-executor but does not remove. For corrupt: judge removes and appoints another.
The judge's goal is to preserve the estate and the testator's intent.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There is a dispute on whether the judge's removal of a trustworthy executor is effective. The author of Al-Fusulayn says it is not; others say it is.
The relied-upon view is that removal is effective.
4
Apply the rulings to scenarios
Given a description of an executor, decide what the judge should do according to the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن أبي يوسف في المنتقى : الدخول في الوصية أول مرة غلط، والثاني خيانة والثالث سرقة فإذا ظهرت من الوصي خيانة عزله القاضي ونصب آخر لأن الأمانة في الإيصاء أصل لأن منفعة الإيصاء وفائدتها تحصل بها. ثم الأوصياء ثلاثة : عدل كاف وغير عدل كاف، وفاسق مخوف على ماله . فالعدل الكافي لا يعزله القاضي وإن عزله ينعزل وصار حائراً لأن للقاضي سطوة يد وولاية شاملة على الكافة خصوصاً على مال الميت والصغار فيكون عزل القاضي كعزل الميت لو كان حياً. قال صاحب الفصولين : المختار عندي أنه لا ينعزل ولو لم يعلم القاضي أن للميت وصياً والوصي غائب فأوصى إلى رجل فالوصي هو وصي الميت دون وصي القاضي لأنه اتصل به اختيار الميت دون وصي القاضي كما إذا كان القاضي عالماً والعدل الذي ليس بكاف أو ضعيف لا يقدر على التصرف وحفظ التركة بنفسه يضم إليه غيره ولا يعزله لاعتماد الموصى عليه لأمانته وصيانته حتى لا ينقطع عن الميت منفعة عدالته ويضم إليه آخر حتى يزول ضرر عدم كفايته وهدايته. والفاسق المخوف على ماله بعزله القاضي ونصب آخر مكانه لأن في إبقائه على الوصية إضراراً بالميت والميت لا يقدر على عزله فقام القاضي مقامه في العزل. وفي الفتاوى : ولو قال الوصي لي على الميت دين ولا بينة له قيل بأن للقاضي أن يخرجه من الوصاية لأنه يستحل الأخذ من مال الميت، وقيل لا يخرجه إلا إذا ادعى شيئاً بعينه أخرجه من يده، والمختار أن القاضي يقول للموصى له إما أن تقيم البينة عليه حتى تستوفي، وإما أن تبرئه من الدين، وأما إن أخرجك من الوصاية فإن أبرأه وإلا أخرجه وذكر الخصاف في آداب القاضي أن للقاضي أن يجعل للميت وصياً أخر في مقدار ذلك الدين خاصة حتى يقيم الأول البينة على الوصي لأن البينة لا تقبل إلا على الخصم ولا يخرجه من الوصاية.
And from Abu Yusuf in Al-Muntaqa: Entering into executorship the first time is a mistake, the second is betrayal, and the third is theft. If betrayal appears from the executor, the judge dismisses him and appoints another because trustworthiness in executorship is the foundation, as the benefit and utility of executorship are achieved through it. Then executors are of three types: a trustworthy and capable one, a trustworthy but not capable one, and a corrupt person who is feared for his property. As for the trustworthy and capable, the judge does not dismiss him, and if he dismisses him, he is dismissed and becomes confused because the judge has a powerful hand and comprehensive authority over all, especially over the property of the deceased and minors. So the judge's dismissal is like the dismissal of the deceased if he were alive. The author of Al-Fusulayn said: What I choose is that he is not dismissed. And if the judge does not know that the deceased has an executor, and the executor is absent, and he appoints a man, then that man is the executor of the deceased, not the executor of the judge, because the choice of the deceased has reached him, not the judge's choice, as if the judge were aware. As for the trustworthy but not capable, or weak, who cannot handle transactions and preserve the estate by himself, the judge adds another to him and does not dismiss him, because the testator relied on his trustworthiness and probity, so that the benefit of his probity does not cease from the deceased. He adds another so that the harm of his incapacity and lack of guidance is removed. As for the corrupt person feared for his property, the judge dismisses him and appoints another in his place, because keeping him in executorship harms the deceased, and the deceased cannot dismiss him, so the judge stands in his place in dismissal. And in Al-Fatawa: If the executor says, 'I have a debt against the deceased,' and he has no evidence, it is said that the judge may remove him from executorship because he takes from the deceased's property by legal trick. And it is said: He does not remove him unless he claims a specific thing that he has taken from his hand. The chosen view is that the judge says to the legatee: 'Either you produce evidence against him to collect, or you release him from the debt. As for removing you from executorship, if he releases him, otherwise he removes him.' And Al-Khassaf mentioned in Adab al-Qadi that the judge may appoint for the deceased another executor for the amount of that debt specifically, until the first executor produces evidence against that executor, because evidence is only accepted against an opponent, and he does not remove him from executorship.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings regarding executors and the judge's authority. It begins with a quote from Abu Yusuf's Al-Muntaqa: the first instance of mismanagement is a mistake, the second is betrayal, the third is theft; at the point of betrayal, the judge may remove him. Then it categorizes executors into three types: (1) trustworthy and capable – the judge should not remove him, and if the judge does, the removal is effective because the judge has authority; however, the author of Al-Fusulayn opines that the judge's removal does not actually remove him. (2) trustworthy but not capable – the judge does not remove him but adds a co-executor to assist. (3) corrupt and unreliable – the judge removes him and appoints another. The passage also addresses a scenario where the executor claims the deceased owed him a debt without evidence. The preferred view is that the judge gives the beneficiary an option: provide proof or release the executor; if the beneficiary does not release, the judge removes the executor. Al-Khassaf suggests appointing a separate executor for that debt matter only.

Key Terms
عدل كاف (adl kafi): A trustworthy and capable executor who can manage the estate properly.
فاسق مخوف على ماله (fasiq makhuf 'ala malihi): A corrupt executor whose financial integrity is doubtful; the judge should remove him.
عزل (azl): Removal of an executor by the judge due to unfitness or betrayal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should a judge do with a trustworthy but incapable executor?
A) Remove him immediately.
B) Add another executor to assist him.
C) Ignore his incapacity.
D) Appoint a new executor in his place.

Key Takeaway
A judge must preserve the estate by removing corrupt executors and supporting trustworthy but incapable ones.

Executor's Debt Claim Without Evidence: Judge's Role
Procedure for executor's unsubstantiated debt claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi procedure when an executor claims a debt against the deceased without evidence.

Core Idea
An executor claiming unpaid debt without proof may be removed unless the beneficiary releases him or proves a specific claim.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a delicate conflict of interest: an executor alleges the deceased owed him money. Hanafi jurists provide a balanced procedure to protect both the estate and the executor's rights. Understanding this ruling prevents abuse of executorship.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conflict of interest
The executor is both the claimant and the manager of the estate, creating a potential conflict.
The deceased cannot defend himself.
2
Recognize the two opinions
One opinion: the judge should remove the executor because he is taking from the estate by legal trick. Another: do not remove unless he claims a specific item taken from his possession.
These appear in Al-Fatawa.
3
Analyze the preferred view
The judge gives the beneficiary an option: produce evidence to collect the debt, or release the executor from the claim. If the beneficiary does not release, the judge removes the executor.
This balances the rights of both parties.
4
Note Al-Khassaf's alternative
Al-Khassaf suggests appointing a separate executor specifically to handle the debt claim, allowing the original executor to present evidence against that representative without being his own opponent.
This avoids removing the executor entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن أبي يوسف في المنتقى : الدخول في الوصية أول مرة غلط، والثاني خيانة والثالث سرقة فإذا ظهرت من الوصي خيانة عزله القاضي ونصب آخر لأن الأمانة في الإيصاء أصل لأن منفعة الإيصاء وفائدتها تحصل بها. ثم الأوصياء ثلاثة : عدل كاف وغير عدل كاف، وفاسق مخوف على ماله . فالعدل الكافي لا يعزله القاضي وإن عزله ينعزل وصار حائراً لأن للقاضي سطوة يد وولاية شاملة على الكافة خصوصاً على مال الميت والصغار فيكون عزل القاضي كعزل الميت لو كان حياً. قال صاحب الفصولين : المختار عندي أنه لا ينعزل ولو لم يعلم القاضي أن للميت وصياً والوصي غائب فأوصى إلى رجل فالوصي هو وصي الميت دون وصي القاضي لأنه اتصل به اختيار الميت دون وصي القاضي كما إذا كان القاضي عالماً والعدل الذي ليس بكاف أو ضعيف لا يقدر على التصرف وحفظ التركة بنفسه يضم إليه غيره ولا يعزله لاعتماد الموصى عليه لأمانته وصيانته حتى لا ينقطع عن الميت منفعة عدالته ويضم إليه آخر حتى يزول ضرر عدم كفايته وهدايته. والفاسق المخوف على ماله بعزله القاضي ونصب آخر مكانه لأن في إبقائه على الوصية إضراراً بالميت والميت لا يقدر على عزله فقام القاضي مقامه في العزل. وفي الفتاوى : ولو قال الوصي لي على الميت دين ولا بينة له قيل بأن للقاضي أن يخرجه من الوصاية لأنه يستحل الأخذ من مال الميت، وقيل لا يخرجه إلا إذا ادعى شيئاً بعينه أخرجه من يده، والمختار أن القاضي يقول للموصى له إما أن تقيم البينة عليه حتى تستوفي، وإما أن تبرئه من الدين، وأما إن أخرجك من الوصاية فإن أبرأه وإلا أخرجه وذكر الخصاف في آداب القاضي أن للقاضي أن يجعل للميت وصياً أخر في مقدار ذلك الدين خاصة حتى يقيم الأول البينة على الوصي لأن البينة لا تقبل إلا على الخصم ولا يخرجه من الوصاية.

If betrayal appears from the executor, the judge dismisses him and appoints another because trustworthiness in executorship is the foundation, as the benefit and utility of executorship are achieved through it. Commentary
This passage covers several rulings regarding executors and the judge's authority. It begins with a quote from Abu Yusuf's Al-Muntaqa: the first instance of mismanagement is a mistake, the second is betrayal, the third is theft; at the point of betrayal, the judge may remove him. Al-Khassaf suggests appointing a separate executor for that debt matter only.

Key Terms
وصي (wasi): Executor, the person appointed to manage the deceased's estate.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically two male witnesses.
موصى له (musa lahu): Beneficiary or legatee, the person to whom the bequest is made.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (mukhtar), what happens if the beneficiary refuses to release the executor from the debt claim?
A) The executor is kept in office and the claim is ignored.
B) The judge removes the executor from executorship.
C) The judge appoints a separate executor for the entire estate.
D) The beneficiary must pay the debt.

Key Takeaway
When an executor claims a debt without evidence, the judge should not immediately remove him but give the beneficiary the option to prove or release; removal follows only if the beneficiary refuses to release.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Does a Command to Pay Debts Create Executorship?
Contrasts Abu Hanifa's and Muhammad al-Shaybani's views on executorship via debt payment command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani on whether a command to pay debts constitutes executorship, and explain the practical difference between the two views.

Core Idea
A command to pay debts alone makes the addressee an executor according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to Muhammad al-Shaybani.

This lesson examines a concise intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani concerning the scope of executorship (wasiyyah). The key question: does a sick person's instruction to 'pay my debts' automatically appoint the addressee as an executor over the entire estate, or does it grant only a limited role? The difference turns on how broadly one interprets the testator's intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Ruling
What is the act? A sick person tells someone 'Pay my debts.' Identify the legal effect according to each imam.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position
He becomes an executor. Note the reasoning: implied authority from the importance of debt payment.
3
State Muhammad's Position
He does not become an executor unless explicitly told to execute bequests as well.
4
Analyze the Dispute
How does each imam interpret the testator's intention? What is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)?
5
Determine the Preferred View
Which view is mufta bihi? The text does not specify, but the matn presents Abu Hanifa's view first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مريض قال لآخر اقض ديوني صار وصياً في قول أبي حنيفة. وقال محمد ما لم يقل أقض ديوني ونفذ وصاياي لا يصير وصياً.
A sick person said to another, 'Pay my debts.' He becomes an executor according to Abu Hanifa. And Muhammad said: As long as he did not say 'Pay my debts and execute my bequests,' he does not become an executor.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the scope of executorship. Abu Hanifa holds that if a sick person (on his deathbed) instructs someone to pay his debts, that person becomes an executor (wasi) by implication, because paying debts is part of the testator's affairs. Imam Muhammad disagrees, stating that the person does not become an executor unless he is also told to execute the bequests (wasiyyah). The difference hinges on whether the command to pay debts alone is sufficient to create an executorship. For Abu Hanifa, it is; for Muhammad, executorship requires a broader mandate. This shows that the precise wording matters in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
مريض (marid): A sick person, especially one in a terminal condition (marad al-mawt).
اقض ديوني (iqdi duyuni): Command to 'pay my debts' – according to Abu Hanifa, this constitutes appointment as executor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the effect of a dying person saying to someone 'Pay my debts'?
A) The person becomes an executor only for paying debts.
B) The person becomes a full executor.
C) The person does not become an executor.
D) The person becomes an agent but not an executor.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the scope of executorship can hinge on the precise wording of the testator's instructions, leading to a disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Legal Stratagem for Executor to Pay Unproven Debts
Provides a legal stratagem for an executor to pay known debts without formal proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi legal stratagem for an executor to satisfy a known debt when the creditor lacks formal evidence, and apply it to a scenario.

Core Idea
An executor may sell estate goods to the creditor who then denies the price — a permissible hila to pay a known debt without formal proof.

This passage presents a practical legal stratagem (hila) from the Hanafi school, addressing a common dilemma: an executor knows a debt is genuine, but the creditor has no evidence. How can the executor fulfill the deceased's obligation without violating the rights of the beneficiaries? The solution involves a sale with denial, effectively transferring ownership without actual payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dilemma
The executor knows a debt is owed but there is no evidence. The creditor cannot prove the debt in court.
The executor's knowledge is certain, but formal proof is missing.
2
State the first stratagem from Nasir ibn Yahya
The executor sells estate goods to the creditor; the creditor then denies having paid the price. This way the creditor gets the goods as compensation for the debt.
The denial is a key element; it makes the sale effectively a transfer without payment.
3
Differentiate for movables
If the estate consists of movable goods, the executor deposits them with the creditor, who then denies receipt.
The same principle applies: the creditor retains the goods by denying receipt.
4
Examine the second opinion from Nasir ibn Abi Sulayman
If a just witness testifies, the executor may pay if he trusts the witness, but can withhold if afraid of liability from beneficiaries.
This opinion gives discretion to the executor based on witness credibility.
5
Apply the rule for specific identifiable property
If the property is known to belong to the claimant or was usurped by the deceased, the executor must return it directly.
No stratagem is needed; the property is clearly identifiable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سأل نصير بن يحيى عن قوم ادعوا على الميت ديناً ولا بينة لهم والوصي يعلم ذلك قال : يبيع الوصي بعض التركة من الغريم ثم يجحد الغريم الثمن فيصير عن ماله، وإن كانت التركة متاعاً أودعهم ثم يجحدون. وقال نصير بن أبي سليمان : وصي شهد عنده عدل أن لهذا على الميت ألف درهم قال يسعه أن يعطيه بقوله، وإن خاف الضمان وسعه أن لا يعطيه فإن كان هذا شيئاً بعينه كجارية ونحوها فعلم الوصي أنها لهذه أو كان الميت غصبها قال : هذا يدفعها إلى المغصوب منه . قصاصاً. قال رحمه الله : وإلا) (لا أي إن لم يرد عنه بل ردها في غير وجهه لا ترتد لأن الوصي
Nasir ibn Yahya was asked about people who claimed a debt against the deceased without evidence, while the executor knows it. He said: The executor sells some of the estate to the debtor, then the debtor denies the price, and it becomes from his wealth. If the estate consists of goods, he deposits them with them, then they deny. And Nasir ibn Abi Sulayman said: An executor; a just person testified before him that this person has a thousand dirhams against the deceased. He said: It is within his capacity to give it to him based on his word, and if he fears liability, it is within his capacity not to give it. If this is a specific thing, like a slave girl and the like, and the executor knows that it belongs to this claimant, or the deceased had usurped it, he said: He returns it to the person from whom it was usurped, as a retaliation (qisas?). He (the author) said: Otherwise, i.e., if he did not return it from him, but returned it in an improper way, it is not accepted because the executor... [incomplete]
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents legal stratagems (hiyal) for an executor to fulfill debts without formal proof. The first stratagem is from Nasir ibn Yahya: if the executor knows the debt is genuine but the creditor lacks evidence, the executor can sell some estate goods to the creditor, and then the creditor denies having paid the price. This way, the creditor gets the goods without actually paying, effectively satisfying the debt. For movables, the executor can entrust them to the creditor and then the creditor denies receiving them, again getting the goods. The second opinion from Nasir ibn Abi Sulayman addresses the case where a just witness testifies to a debt. The executor may pay if he trusts the witness, but if he fears being held liable by the beneficiaries, he may not. For specific identifiable property belonging to the claimant or known to have been usurped, the executor must return it. The last part is incomplete but seems to discuss the executor's liability if he returns property in an improper manner.

Key Terms
حيلة (hila): A legal stratagem to achieve a permissible end; here, paying a known debt without formal evidence.
الغريم (gharim): The debtor/creditor (here, the person claiming the debt against the deceased).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Nasir ibn Yahya, how can an executor pay a known creditor who lacks evidence?
A) By simply giving the creditor money from the estate.
B) By selling estate goods to the creditor, and the creditor denies the price.
C) By asking the judge for permission.
D) By making the creditor take an oath.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a legal stratagem where an executor sells goods to a creditor who then denies payment, allowing payment of a known debt without formal proof.

Ruling on Acceptance After Rejection of Executorship
Explains that executor may accept after initial rejection unless qadi removed him

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which an executor's later acceptance is valid and explain the distinction between executor and legatee regarding acceptance.

Core Idea
An executor's initial rejection does not void the appointment unless the qadi removes him, because preserving the deceased's harm outweighs the executor's choice.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a common scenario: a person nominated as executor (waṣī) initially declines but later changes his mind. The ruling hinges on whether the qadi has intervened. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper estate management and reflects the Hanafi principle of preventing harm (ḍarar) to the deceased.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The core ruling is that an executor's later acceptance after initial rejection is valid, but only if the qadi has not removed him. Note the condition: the qadi's intervention invalidates the later acceptance.
The condition is key: qadi's removal is the only event that makes rejection final.
2
Trace the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The author explains that rejecting the executor entirely would harm the deceased (by leaving the estate without management). The harm to the legatee in keeping the executor is offset by the executor's hope for reward. Thus, preventing harm to the deceased takes priority.
This is an example of istihsan based on harm prevention.
3
Distinguish between executor and legatee
Unlike the executor, a legatee's acceptance during the testator's life is not binding; he may reject after death. This is because the legatee receives a benefit, whereas the executor bears a responsibility. Rejecting executorship without the deceased's knowledge causes harm, so it is restricted.
The legatee's acceptance does not harm the deceased; only the executor's does.
4
Compare with the agent (wakil) case
The passage concludes by comparing the executor to an agent appointed to purchase an unspecified item. Such an agent may resign without the principal's knowledge because no specific harm results, unlike the executor whose resignation harms the deceased.
This distinction is based on the presence or absence of specific reliance (ta'ayyun).
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some Hanafi scholars hold that an agent cannot resign without knowledge even for unspecified items. The author sides with the view allowing it, citing al-Hidayah and al-'Inayah. This shows that even within the madhhab, there is room for ijtihad.
The mufta bihi is the permissive view for unspecified items.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن مات الموصي فقال لا أقبل ثم قيل صح إن لم يخرجه قاض منذ قال لا أقبل أي الموصى إليه إن لم يقبل حتى مات الموصي فقال لا أقبل ثم قال أقبل فله ذلك إن لم يكن أخرجه من الوصية حين قال لا أقبل لأن مجرد قوله لا أقبل لا يبطل الإيصاء لأن فيه ضرراً بالميت وضرر الموصى له في الإبقاء مجبور بالثواب ودفع الضرر الأول أولى إلا أن القاضي إذا أخرجه عن الوصية يصح ذلك لأنه مجتهد فيه فكان له إخراجه بعد قوله لا أقبل كما أن له إخراجه بعد قبوله أولاً لأنه نصب ناظراً فإذا رأى غيره أصلح منه كان له عزله ونصب غيره، وربما يعجز هو عن ذلك فيتضرر بالوصية فيدفع القاضي الضرر وينصب حافظاً لمال الميت متصرفاً فيه فيدفع الضرر من الجانبين. ولو قال أقبل بعدما أخرجه القاضي لا يلتفت إليه لأنه قبل بعدما بطلت الوصية بإخراج القاضي إياه. قال في العناية : وطولب بالفرق بين الموصى له والموصى إليه فإن قبول الأول في الحال غير معتبر حتى لو قبل حال حياة الموصي ثم رده بعد وفاته كان صحيحاً بخلاف الثاني فإنه إذا قبله في حال الحياة ثم رده بعد الموت لا يصح، وفي أن قبوله حال حياته معتبر وقبول الأول في حال الحياة غير معتبر . وأجيب بأن الإيصاء يقع للميت فكان ردها بغير علمه إضراراً به فلا يجوز بخلاف الأول. وقوله بخلاف الوكيل بشراء عبده بغير عينه أو ببيع ماله حيث يصح رده في غيبته وبغير علمه لأنه لا ضرر .
And if the testator dies and he (the proposed executor) says 'I do not accept', then later it is said (by the author) that it is valid if the qadi has not removed him since he said 'I do not accept' – that is, the proposed executor, if he had not accepted until the testator died and then said 'I do not accept' and then said 'I accept', then he has that right as long as the qadi had not removed him from the executorship when he said 'I do not accept', because his mere saying 'I do not accept' does not invalidate the bequest, because that would contain harm to the deceased, and the harm to the legatee (the orphan/beneficiary) in keeping it is compensated by reward, and repelling the first harm is more appropriate. But if the qadi removes him from the executorship, that is valid because it is a matter of ijtihad, so he may remove him after he said 'I do not accept' just as he may remove him after he first accepted, because he appointed a supervisor, and if he sees another more suitable than him, he may dismiss him and appoint another. And perhaps he (the executor) is incapable of that and would be harmed by the executorship, so the qadi repels the harm and appoints a custodian for the deceased's property to act in it, thus repelling harm from both sides. And if he says 'I accept' after the qadi has removed him, it is not heeded because he accepted after the executorship was invalidated by the qadi's removal. He said in al-'Inayah: And a distinction was requested between the legatee (the one given something in the will) and the executor: the acceptance of the former (legatee) at the time (of the testator's life) is not considered, so if he accepts during the testator's life and then rejects after his death, it is valid (i.e., the rejection is effective), contrary to the latter (executor), because if he accepts during life and then rejects after death, it is not valid. And that his acceptance during his life is considered, while the acceptance of the former during life is not considered. And it was answered that the bequest (of executorship) falls to the deceased, so its rejection without his knowledge is harmful to him, so it is not permissible, unlike the former. And his statement: 'Contrary to the agent appointed to purchase a slave not specifically identified, or to sell his property, where his rejection in his absence and without his knowledge is valid, because there is no harm.'
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on executorship. It addresses the scenario where a person is nominated as executor (waṣī) but does not accept until after the testator's death. He initially says 'I do not accept' but later changes his mind and accepts. The ruling is that this later acceptance is valid provided the qadi has not already removed him from the role after his initial rejection. The reasoning (tālīl) is that the initial rejection does not automatically void the executorship because that would harm the deceased (by leaving the estate without a manager) and the harm to the legatee (the one entitled to the will's benefit) in keeping the executor is compensated by the reward the executor would hope for. Since repelling harm to the deceased is prioritized, the original appointment remains effective until the qadi intervenes. However, if the qadi, acting on his ijtihād, removes the executor after the rejection, the later acceptance is void. The passage then distinguishes the executor from a legatee (mūṣā lahu) who receives a gift in the will; the legatee's acceptance during the testator's life is not binding, and he may later reject, whereas the executor's acceptance during life is binding and later rejection is invalid. Finally, a comparison is made with a wakīl (agent) appointed to purchase an unspecified slave or sell property; such an agent can resign without the principal's knowledge because no specific harm (ḍarar) results, unlike the executor whose resignation harms the deceased.

Key Terms
المُوصَى إِلَيْهِ (al-mūṣā ilayhi): The person appointed as executor (waṣī) to carry out the testator's will after death.
المُوصَى لَهُ (al-mūṣā lahu): The beneficiary who receives a bequest (wasiyyah) from the testator's estate.
وَصِيَّة (wasiyyah): A bequest or will, including the appointment of an executor and the transfer of property after death.
قَبُول (qabūl): Acceptance of the executorship; can be verbal or by conduct (e.g., selling the estate).
ضَرَر (ḍarar): Harm that justifies legal rulings, used here to argue that rejection by the waṣī without knowledge of the testator harms the deceased.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An executor nominated by the testator says 'I do not accept' after the testator's death. Later he says 'I accept'. The qadi has not removed him. What is the ruling?
A) His acceptance is invalid because he already rejected.
B) His acceptance is valid because the qadi did not remove him.
C) His acceptance is valid only if the beneficiaries agree.
D) His acceptance is invalid because the testator is dead.

Key Takeaway
An executor's initial rejection does not permanently void the appointment; later acceptance is valid unless the qadi has removed him, based on preventing harm to the deceased.

Agent's Self-Revocation Without Principal's Knowledge: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Al-Nihaya criticizes the author's view on agent's revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two positions in the Hanafi dispute regarding an agent's self-revocation without the principal's knowledge, and identify the key sources cited (al-Dhakhirah, Adab al-Qadi, al-Jami' al-Saghir) and the reasoning of later scholars (al-Sarakhsi, al-Halwani).

Core Idea
An agent cannot exit agency without the principal's knowledge according to earlier Hanafi texts, but later scholars allowed it in some ijtihad-based cases.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the validity of an agent's self-revocation during the principal's absence. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a critique from al-Nihayah, which points out a contradiction with established transmissions in earlier works such as al-Dhakhirah and Adab al-Qadi. We will analyze the arguments on both sides and understand the practical implications of this disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Critique from al-Nihayah
Read the passage and note that al-Nihayah accuses the author of al-Bahr of contradicting the general riwayat found in three specific early Hanafi works: al-Dhakhirah, Adab al-Qadi by al-Sadr al-Shahid, and al-Jami' al-Saghir by al-Mahbubi. These works indicate that the agent does not leave the agency when he dismisses himself without the principal's knowledge during the principal's absence.
2
Understand the Author's Position and the Qadi's Role
The author of al-Bahr refers to the qadi's removal (ikhraj) of the executor. He reports that the shuyukh differed: some (including al-Sarakhsi and the author of al-Hidayah) held that the qadi's ruling in a matter of ijtihad is valid and executes the removal. Al-Halwani agreed. This view validates the agent's self-revocation indirectly by allowing qadi intervention.
The dispute is about whether the agent's self-revocation is effective on its own or only through qadi's action.
3
Analyze the Evidence from Earlier Texts
The earlier texts (al-Dhakhirah, Adab al-Qadi, al-Jami' al-Saghir) contain explicit indications that the agent does not exit agency without the principal's knowledge. This is strong evidence because these texts are part of the zahir al-riwayah or widely transmitted works.
These sources are authoritative within the Hanafi school.
4
Discover the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Difference)
The practical consequence: if the agent sells property after self-revocation without informing the principal, some scholars (following the earlier texts) would consider the sale invalid (since the agency is still in effect), while others (following al-Sarakhsi) would validate the sale if the qadi had removed the agent or if the agency was for unspecified items.
This affects legal certainty in transactions during the principal's absence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال صاحب النهاية : هذا الذي ذكره مخالف لعامة روايات الكتب من الذخيرة وأدب القاضي للصدر الشهيد والجامع الصغير للمحبوبي، وفي كل واحد منهما ما يدل على أن الوكيل إذا عزل نفسه من غير علم الموكل لم يخرج عن الوكالة حال غيبة الموكل وقول المؤلف «إن لم يخرجه قاض إلى آخره اختلف المشايخ في هذا الإخراج قال في العناية : فمنهم من قال حكم في فصل مجتهد فيه فينفذ وإليه ذهب الإمام السرخسي واختاره المصنف، قال إنما صح لأنها لو صحت بقبوله كان للقاضي أن يخرج ويصح الإخراج فهذا أولى وإليه ذهب الحلواني .
The author of al-Nihayah said: 'What he (the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) mentioned contradicts the general transmissions of the books: al-Dhakhirah, Adab al-Qadi by al-Sadr al-Shahid, and al-Jami' al-Saghir by al-Mahbubi. In each of them there is evidence that the agent, if he dismisses himself without the knowledge of the principal, does not exit from the agency during the absence of the principal.' And the statement of the author (of al-Bahr) 'if the qadi has not removed him' etc. – the shuyukh differed concerning this removal. He said in al-'Inayah: 'Some of them said: (the qadi's) ruling is in a matter of ijtihad, so it is executed. This is the view of Imam al-Sarakhsi, and the author (of al-Hidayah) chose it. He said: It is valid because if the executorship becomes valid by his acceptance, the qadi has the authority to remove him, and the removal is valid, so this is more appropriate. And al-Halwani held this view.'
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This is a critical refinement (hashiya) from al-Nihayah pointing out that the ruling given earlier (that an agent can revoke without knowledge for unspecified items) contradicts the well-known transmissions (riwayat) in earlier Hanafi works such as al-Dhakhirah, Adab al-Qadi of al-Sadr al-Shahid, and al-Jami' al-Saghir of al-Mahbubi. Those works indicate that an agent who dismisses himself without the knowledge of the principal does not actually exit from the agency while the principal is absent. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq then reports that the shuyukh differed regarding the qadi's power to remove an executor after the executor's rejection. According to al-'Inayah, one group (including al-Sarakhsi and the author of al-Hidayah) held that the qadi's ruling is based on ijtihad and thus valid; if the executorship could become valid by acceptance, then the qadi's removal is even more appropriate. Another group, represented by al-Halwani, also agreed with this. This passage thus clarifies the level of authority and the intra-madhhab disagreement.

Key Terms
النهاية (al-Nihayah): A commentary by a later Hanafi scholar that critiques the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on this point.
إخراج (ikhraj): The qadi's removal of the executor from his position, which is a ruling based on ijtihad.
الذخيرة (al-Dhakhirah): A Hanafi fiqh work by al-Sarakhsi? Actually al-Dhakhirah al-Burhaniyyah by al-Burhan al-Marghinani? Here it is cited as a source for the earlier position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Nihayah, what do earlier Hanafi books like al-Dhakhirah and Adab al-Qadi indicate about an agent who dismisses himself without the principal's knowledge?
A) The agent validly exits the agency.
B) The agent does not exit the agency.
C) The agent's dismissal is valid if the principal is absent.
D) The agent's dismissal is valid only for unspecified items.

Key Takeaway
An agent does not exit the agency without the principal's knowledge according to earlier Hanafi texts, but later scholars allowed qadi's intervention based on ijtihad, creating a legitimate intra-school difference.

Ruling on Will to a Slave, Disbeliever, or Sinner
Explains that will to these persons is valid but qadi replaces them with others

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on appointing a slave, disbeliever, or fasiq as executor, and explain the disagreement between Imam Muhammad and the later view.

Core Idea
A will to a slave, disbeliever, or fasiq is valid initially, but the qadi must replace them due to defective supervision.

When a testator appoints an executor, certain conditions must be met: freedom, Islam, and probity. But what happens if he appoints someone lacking these? The Hanafi school holds that the appointment is valid in itself, yet the qadi is obliged to remove the executor and appoint another. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this ruling and the internal disagreement within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three disqualifying traits
List the three types of persons mentioned: slave (not free), disbeliever (lacks Islam), fasiq (lacks probity). These violate the conditions for executorship.
Freedom, Islam, and probity are conditions (shurut) for the executor's capacity.
2
Understand initial validity vs. eventual removal
The appointment is valid (sahih) ab initio because the person has the basic capacity for supervision, but the qadi must remove them and substitute another because supervision is defective.
Al-Quduri's statement that the qadi removes them implies entry into the role, hence validity.
3
Analyze the proof from al-Quduri
Al-Quduri's ruling that the qadi has authority to remove them indicates the appointment was initially valid—removal can only occur after entry.
4
Examine Imam Muhammad's opposing view
Imam Muhammad in al-Asl said the will is batil (void). Interpretations: 'will become void' (sabatulu) or for the slave void due to lack of authority over himself.
The later view (sahih) reconciles by saying it is valid but subject to removal.
5
Assess the ikhtilaf on actions before removal
Some shuyukh held the executor's actions before qadi's removal are effective; the correct view (sahih) is they are not effective.
The author states 'wa huwa al-sahih' (that is the correct view).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (والى عبد وكافر وفاسق بدل بغيرهم إذا أوصى إلى هؤلاء المذكورين أخرجهم القاضي ويستبدل غيرهم مكانهم . وأشار المصنف إلى شروط الولاية : فالأول الحرية، والثاني الإسلام، والثالث العدالة. فلو ولي من ذكر صح ويستبدل غيره، وذكر القدوري أن للقاضي أن يخرجهم عن الوصية وهذا يدل على أن الولاية صحيحة لأن الإخراج يكون بعد الدخول. وذكر محمد في الأصل أن الوصية باطلة قيل معناه ستبطل، وقيل في العبد باطلة لعدم الولاية على نفسه وفي غيره معناه ستبطل، وقيل في الكافر باطلة أيضاً لعدم ولايته على المسلم ووجه الصحة ثم الإخراج أن أصل النظر ثابت لقدرة العبد حقيقة وولاية الفاسق على نفسه وعلى غيره على ما عرف من أصلنا، وولاية الكافر تتم في الجملة إلا أنه لم يتم النظر لتوقف ولاية العبد على إجازة مولاه وتمكنه من الحجر بعدها والمعادة الدينية دالة على ترك النظر في حق المسلم واتهام الفاسق بالخيانة فيخرجهم القاضي عن الوصية ويقيم غيرهم مقامهم إتماماً للنظر . وشرط في الأصل أن يكون الفاسق مخوفاً منه على المال لأنه يكون عذراً في إخراجه وتبديله بغيره بخلاف ما إذا أوصى إلى مكاتبه أو مكاتب غيره حيث يجوز لأن المكاتب في منافعه كالحر وإن رد بعد ذلك فالجواب فيه كالجواب في القن، والصبي كالقن لو بلغ الصبي وعتق العبد وأسلم الكافر لم يخرجهم القاضي عن الوصية. وإذا تصرف الصبي أو العبد أو الذمي قبل أن يخرجهم القاضي من الوصاية هل ينفذ تصرفهم؟ اختلف فيه المشايخ ؛ فمنهم من قال ينفذ، ومنهم من قال لا ينفذ وهو الصحيح.
He (the author) said: 'And to a slave, disbeliever, and fasiq (sinner) – they are replaced by others – that is, if he bequeaths (appoints) to these mentioned, the qadi removes them and substitutes others in their place. And the author (of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) pointed to the conditions of guardianship: first, freedom; second, Islam; third, probity ('adalah). So if he appoints one of those mentioned, it is valid, and another is substituted. Al-Quduri mentioned that the qadi has the authority to remove them from the executorship, and this indicates that the guardianship is valid, because removal comes after entry (into the role). Muhammad mentioned in al-Asl that the will is void; it was said its meaning is that it will become void; and it was said concerning the slave it is void for lack of guardianship over himself, and concerning others it means it will become void; and it was said concerning the disbeliever it is also void for lack of his authority over a Muslim. The aspect of validity and then removal is that the basis of supervision (nazar) is established due to the slave's capability in reality, and the fasiq's authority over himself and others according to what is known from our usul, and the disbeliever's authority is established in general, except that supervision is not complete because the slave's authority depends on the permission of his master and his ability to place him under interdiction after that, and the religious excuse (al-mu'adha al-diniyya) indicates abandoning supervision in the matter of the Muslim, and the fasiq is suspected of betrayal. So the qadi removes them from the executorship and appoints others in their place to complete supervision. And it is conditioned in al-Asl that the fasiq be one from whom harm to the property is feared, because that is an excuse for his removal and replacement with another, unlike the case where he bequeaths to his mukatab (slave contracted for manumission) or another's mukatab, for that is permissible because the mukatab, in his benefits, is like a free person. But if he rejects after that, the answer regarding him is like the answer regarding the qinn (full slave). And the minor is like the qinn. If the minor reaches puberty, the slave becomes free, and the disbeliever becomes Muslim, the qadi does not remove them from the executorship. And if the minor, slave, or dhimmi acts before the qadi removes them from the executorship, is their action effective? The shuyukh differed: some said it is effective; some said it is not, and that is the correct (opinion).
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the validity of a will appointing a slave, a disbeliever, or a fasiq (immoral person) as executor. The matn states that such a person is replaced by others (i.e., by the qadi). The commentator explains that the author (of Kanz) indicates three conditions for guardianship: freedom, Islam, and probity (justice). If the testator appoints a person lacking these, the appointment is valid ab initio (sahih), but the qadi must remove that person and appoint someone else. This is based on the statement of al-Quduri that the qadi has the power to remove them, which logically implies that the appointment was initially valid (since removal can only occur after entry). However, Imam Muhammad in al-Asl is reported to have said the will is void (batil). The commentators reconcile this by interpreting 'batil' as 'will become void soon' (sabatulu). For the slave, some said it is void because he lacks authority over himself; for the disbeliever, because he has no authority over a Muslim. The correct view (as held by the author) is that the appointment is valid but subject to removal. The reasoning is that the slave has the actual capacity to manage (nazar), the fasiq has general authority over himself and others in Hanafi usul, and the disbeliever's authority is valid in general. However, supervision (nazar) is not fully realized because of constraints: the slave's authority depends on his master's permission and possible interdiction; the disbeliever's religious excuse prevents proper supervision over Muslim interests; the fasiq is suspected of betrayal. Thus, the qadi removes them. But if the deficiency is removed (slave freed, Muslim converts, etc.), the qadi does not remove them. The passage ends with an ikhtilaf (dispute) among shuyukh about the validity of actions performed by such a defective executor before the qadi's removal: the correct view is that their actions do not take effect (la yanfudh).

Key Terms
الوَصِيَّة (al-wasiyyah): The appointment of an executor (waṣī) by a testator; also the bequest of property.
النَّظَر (al-nazar): The supervisory role of the executor over the estate; 'supervision' required for validity.
الفَاسِق (al-fāsiq): A person who commits major sins openly, lacking probity ('adalah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A testator appoints a slave as executor. What is the initial ruling according to al-Quduri?
A) The appointment is void because a slave cannot own property.
B) The appointment is valid, but the qadi will remove him and appoint someone else.
C) The appointment is valid only if the master agrees.
D) The appointment is valid and the slave remains executor forever.

Key Takeaway
A will to a slave, disbeliever, or fasiq is initially valid, but the qadi must replace them due to defective supervision; actions before removal are ineffective.

The Effect of Permanent Insanity on an Executor's Appointment
Intra-Hanafi ruling on insanity's effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's ruling on permanent insanity of an executor and explain the condition under which the original executor is reinstated.

Core Idea
Permanent insanity of an executor requires qadi intervention, but recovery before removal restores his role automatically.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq examines an intra-Hanafi ruling regarding an executor who becomes permanently insane. Abu Hanifa clarifies that while such insanity disqualifies the executor from managing the estate, his executorship is not definitively voided; it revives if he recovers before the qadi appoints a replacement. The text also hints at a variant in the Nawadir, though the passage is truncated.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
Identify the core ruling: the qadi should (yambaghi) appoint a replacement executor for the deceased when the appointed executor becomes permanently insane.
The term 'yambaghi' indicates a recommendatory duty, not a strict obligation (fard).
2
Identify the decisive condition
Note that the determinative factor is the permanence of the insanity (junun mutbiq), not mere temporary episodes. The ruling applies only to continuous insanity.
Intermittent insanity is treated differently and may not require replacement.
3
Analyse the two scenarios
First scenario: qadi appoints a replacement. Second scenario: qadi delays and the executor recovers before any replacement. In the latter, the original executor is reinstated automatically.
This shows that insanity does not permanently destroy the executorship; it only suspends it until recovery or replacement.
4
Recognise source hierarchy
Abu Hanifa's ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah. The text then references Nawadir Ibrahim from Muhammad, which may contain a conditional variant. The Nawadir carries less authority but is still considered.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) remains Abu Hanifa's view as presented.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أوصى إلى عاقل فجن جنوناً مطبقاً قال أبو حنيفة : ينبغي للقاضي أن يجعل مكانه وصياً للميت فإن لم يفعل القاضي حتى أفاق الوصي كان وصياً على حاله. وفي نوادر إبراهيم عن محمد: إذا أوصى إلى رجل فقال إن مت أنت فالوصي بعدك
And if he bequeaths (appoints) a sane person as executor and he (the executor) becomes afflicted with continuous insanity, Abu Hanifa said: It is incumbent upon the qadi to place in his stead an executor for the deceased. If the qadi does not do so until the executor regains consciousness, he (the original executor) remains the executor as before. And in the Nawadir of Ibrahim from Muhammad: If he bequeaths to a man and says, 'If you die, then the executor after you...'
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of permanent insanity (junun mutbiq) on an executor. Abu Hanifa's position is that the qadi must appoint a replacement executor for the deceased. However, if the qadi does not do so and the executor recovers his sanity before removal, the original executor resumes his role automatically; his executorship is not permanently destroyed by the insanity. The passage then references Nawadir Ibrahim (rare transmissions from Ibrahim al-Halabi?) from Muhammad al-Shaybani, but the text is cut off. It seems to discuss a conditional appointment where the testator makes one executor for himself and another after his death. The core ruling from Abu Hanifa is clear: permanent insanity creates a need for qadi intervention, but recovery before intervention restores the original status.

Key Terms
جُنُون مُطْبِق (junun mutbiq): Continuous or permanent insanity, as opposed to intermittent madness.
نَوَادِر إِبْرَاهِيم (Nawadir Ibrahim): Rare transmissions from Imam Muhammad via Ibrahim al-Halabi or another Ibrahim, containing supplementary rulings outside the six major books.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The qadi must wait for the executor to recover.
B) The qadi should appoint a replacement executor for the deceased.
C) The executorship is automatically void.
D) The qadi can do nothing; the estate is managed by the heirs.

Key Takeaway
Permanent insanity of an executor triggers a duty for the qadi to appoint a replacement, but recovery before that replacement reinstates the original executor.

Rejection of Executorship After Death vs. During Life
Ruling on the validity of rejecting executorship after death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on rejection of executorship after death and after acceptance during life, and explain the underlying proof based on harm to the deceased.

Core Idea
Rejecting executorship after death is valid; rejecting after accepting during life harms the deceased and is invalid.

This passage clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi law of executorship (wasiyyah): the timing of acceptance matters. You will see how the principle of harm (darar) determines validity, and how the executor differs from a regular agent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (1) rejecting executorship after the testator's death, (2) accepting during life then rejecting after death.
2
State the Ruling for Each
Rejection after death is valid (sahih); rejection after acceptance during life is invalid (la yasihh).
The ruling depends on when acceptance occurred.
3
Identify the Proof (Dalil)
The proof is rational: executorship belongs to the deceased; rejecting it without his knowledge harms him, so it is impermissible after acceptance.
The text contrasts with an agent (wakil) where no such harm exists.
4
Examine the Distinction from Agency
An agent may validly reject his agency in the principal's absence because there is no harm, but an executor causes harm to the deceased.
This distinction highlights the unique nature of wasiyyah.
5
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Judge's Removal
The passage notes a difference among later scholars: al-Sarakhsi holds that a judge can remove an executor; al-Halwani disagrees. The author inclines to al-Sarakhsi.
This shows how later Hanafi jurists resolve conflicts within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حياة الموصي ثم رده بعد وفاته كان صحيحاً بخلاف الثاني فإنه إذا قبله في حال الحياة ثم رده بعد الموت لا يصح، وفي أن قبوله حال حياته معتبر وقبول الأول في حال الحياة غير معتبر . وأجيب بأن الإيصاء يقع للميت فكان ردها بغير علمه إضراراً به فلا يجوز بخلاف الأول. وقوله بخلاف الوكيل بشراء عبده بغير عينه أو ببيع ماله حيث يصح رده في غيبته وبغير علمه لأنه لا ضرر . قال صاحب النهاية : هذا الذي ذكره مخالف لعامة روايات الكتب من الذخيرة وأدب القاضي للصدر الشهيد والجامع الصغير للمحبوبي، وفي كل واحد منهما ما يدل على أن الوكيل إذا عزل نفسه من غير علم الموكل لم يخرج عن الوكالة حال غيبة الموكل وقول المؤلف «إن لم يخرجه قاض إلى آخره اختلف المشايخ في هذا الإخراج قال في العناية : فمنهم من قال حكم في فصل مجتهد فيه فينفذ وإليه ذهب الإمام السرخسي واختاره المصنف، قال إنما صح لأنها لو صحت بقبوله كان للقاضي أن يخرج ويصح الإخراج فهذا ومنهم من أولى وإليه ذهب الحلواني .
If he rejects it after the death of the testator, it is valid, unlike the second case: if he accepts it during the testator's life then rejects it after death, it is not valid. And (it is said) that his acceptance during his life is considered, while the acceptance of the first (i.e., the one who rejects during life) is not considered during life. It was answered: because the executorship belongs to the deceased, so rejecting it without his knowledge is harmful to him, thus it is not permissible, unlike the first. And his statement: unlike the agent buying his slave without specification or selling his property, where his rejection is valid in his absence and without his knowledge because there is no harm. The author of al-Nihaya said: what he mentioned contradicts the general narrations of the books such as al-Dhakhira, Adab al-Qadi by al-Sadr al-Shahid, and al-Jami' al-Saghir by al-Mahbubi, and in each of them there is evidence that if the agent removes himself without the knowledge of the principal, he does not exit the agency during the principal's absence. And the author's statement: 'if the judge does not remove him, etc.' The scholars differed regarding this removal. In al-Inaya: some said it is a ruling in an issue of ijtihad, so it is effective; this is the view of Imam al-Sarakhsi and chosen by the author. He said: it is only valid because if it were valid by his acceptance, the judge could remove him and the removal would be valid. Others said it is more appropriate; this is the view of al-Halwani.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the rejection of executorship. The author discusses two scenarios: (1) rejecting executorship after the testator's death is valid; (2) accepting during life and then rejecting after death is invalid. The rationale is that executorship vests in the deceased; rejecting it without his knowledge harms him, so it is impermissible. The author draws a distinction with an agent (wakil) who can validly reject his agency in the principal's absence because there is no similar harm. However, the author of al-Nihaya criticizes this view, citing majority narrations that an agent who removes himself without the principal's knowledge does not exit the agency. This leads to a discussion among later scholars about the judge's removal of an executor, with Imam al-Sarakhsi and al-Halwani holding differing views. The passage illustrates how later Hanafi scholars debate and sometimes resolve apparent conflicts within the school.

Key Terms
الوصية (al-wasiyyah): The legal designation of a person to execute the testator's wishes after death; the executorship.
الوكيل (al-wakil): An agent appointed by a principal to act on his behalf in a specific matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the testator's knowledge is required at the time of rejection, and after death he cannot know.

Key Takeaway
Rejection of executorship after death is valid; but if accepted during the testator's life, later rejection is invalid because it harms the deceased.

8 min
Appointing a Slave, Disbeliever, or Sinner as Executor
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on appointing a slave, disbeliever, or sinner as executor and explain the judge's replacement and the disagreement over actions before removal.

Core Idea
An invalid executor appointment is valid but the judge replaces them; actions before removal are ineffective.

The Hanafi school lays down specific conditions for who may serve as an executor: freedom, Islam, and justice. But what if a testator appoints someone who lacks one of these? The ruling is surprising: the appointment is valid, yet the judge must remove the executor and appoint another. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this apparent paradox and explores the key intra-Hanafi disagreement about the validity of actions taken before the judge's removal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The appointment (tawliyah) of a slave, disbeliever, or sinner as executor (wasi). The ruling: the appointment is valid (sahih) but the judge replaces them.
The distinction between validity and effectiveness is critical here.
2
Locate the reasoning for validity then removal
Why is the appointment valid? Because the appointee has basic capacity: a slave has real ability, a sinner has authority over himself, and a disbeliever's authority is complete in general. But removal is justified because oversight is imperfect: the slave's authority depends on his master, the disbeliever is untrustworthy due to enmity, and the sinner may betray.
The author uses 'talil' (reasoning) to justify both aspects.
3
Analyse the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Quduri states the judge 'may remove them', implying validity. Muhammad in al-Asl says the will is 'void' (batila), but this is interpreted as 'will become void' after removal. Also, scholars differ on whether actions by the appointee before removal are effective.
Distinguish between the initial validity and the final effect.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The correct view (as stated by the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq) is that actions before removal are not effective. If the appointee becomes qualified later (e.g., slave freed), the judge does not remove them.
The tarjih is based on the need to protect the estate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (والى عبد وكافر وفاسق بدل بغيرهم أي إذا أوصى إلى هؤلاء المذكورين أخرجهم القاضي ويستبدل غيرهم مكانهم . وأشار المصنف إلى شروط الولاية : فالأول الحرية، والثاني الإسلام، والثاني العدالة. فلو ولي من ذكر صح ويستبدل غيره، وذكر القدوري أن للقاضي أن يخرجهم عن الوصية وهذا يدل على أن الولاية صحيحة لأن الإخراج يكون بعد الدخول. وذكر محمد في الأصل أن الوصية باطلة قيل معناه ستبطل، وقيل في العبد باطلة لعدم الولاية على نفسه وفي غيره معناه ستبطل، وقيل في الكافر باطلة أيضاً لعدم ولايته على المسلم ووجه الصحة ثم الإخراج أن أصل النظر ثابت لقدرة العبد حقيقة وولاية الفاسق على نفسه وعلى غيره على ما عرف من أصلنا، وولاية الكافر تتم في الجملة إلا أنه لم يتم النظر لتوقف ولاية العبد على إجازة مولاه وتمكنه من الحجر بعدها والمعادة الدينية دالة على ترك النظر في حق المسلم واتهام الفاسق بالخيانة فيخرجهم القاضي عن الوصية ويقيم غيرهم مقامهم إتماماً للنظر . وشرط في الأصل أن يكون الفاسق مخوفاً منه على المال لأنه يكون عذراً في إخراجه وتبديله بغيره بخلاف ما إذا أوصى إلى مكاتبه أو مكاتب غيره حيث يجوز لأن المكاتب في منافعه كالحر وإن رد بعد ذلك فالجواب فيه كالجواب في القن، والصبي كالقن لو بلغ الصبي وعتق العبد وأسلم الكافر لم يخرجهم القاضي عن الوصية. وإذا تصرف الصبي أو العبد أو الذمي قبل أن يخرجهم القاضي من الوصاية هل ينفذ تصرفهم؟ اختلف فيه المشايخ ؛ فمنهم من قال ينفذ، ومنهم من قال لا ينفذ وهو الصحيح.
He (the author) said: 'and to a slave, disbeliever, and sinner, the judge replaces them with others' i.e., if one appoints these mentioned, the judge removes them and puts others in their place. The author indicated the conditions of guardianship: first, freedom; second, Islam; third, justice. So if one appoints such a person, it is valid, but the judge replaces him. Al-Quduri mentioned that the judge may remove them from the executorship, which indicates that the appointment is valid because removal occurs after entry. Muhammad mentioned in al-Asl that the will is void; it is said meaning it will become void. It is said regarding the slave: it is void due to lack of authority over himself; regarding others, it means it will become void. It is said regarding the disbeliever: it is void also due to lack of authority over a Muslim. The face of validity and then removal is that the basis of oversight is established due to the real ability of the slave and the authority of the sinner over himself and others as known from our principle. The authority of the disbeliever is complete in general, but the oversight is not complete because the slave's authority depends on his master's permission and the possibility of restriction thereafter, and religious enmity indicates abandoning oversight over a Muslim, and the sinner is suspected of treachery; so the judge removes them from the executorship and puts others in their place to complete oversight. It is conditioned in al-Asl that the sinner be feared for the wealth because that is an excuse for his removal and replacement, unlike if he appoints his mukatab or someone else's mukatab, where it is permissible because the mukatab is like a free man regarding his benefits. If he returns thereafter, the answer is like the answer regarding the slave. The minor is like the slave. If the minor reaches puberty, the slave is freed, or the disbeliever converts to Islam, the judge does not remove them from the executorship. And if the minor, slave, or dhimmi acts before the judge removes them from the executorship, is their act effective? The scholars differed: some said it is effective; some said it is not effective, and that is correct.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on appointing three categories of persons as executor: a slave, a disbeliever, and a sinful person (fasiq). The base ruling from the matn is that such an appointment is valid but the judge replaces them with others. The author explains the conditions of guardianship: freedom, Islam, and justice. The validity of appointment is supported by al-Quduri's statement that the judge can remove them, implying the appointment was initially valid. However, Muhammad in al-Asl states the will is void, which is interpreted to mean it will become void (i.e., the appointment is initially valid but then nullified by the judge's removal). The rationale for removal: the slave's authority depends on his master, the disbeliever's oversight is compromised by enmity, and the sinner is unreliable. If the appointee becomes qualified later (e.g., slave freed, disbeliever converts), the judge does not remove them. There is a disagreement among scholars about the validity of actions performed before the judge's removal: the correct view is that they are not valid.

Key Terms
الولاية (al-wilayah): Guardianship or authority to act on behalf of another; here it means the legal capacity to be an executor.
الإخراج (al-ikhraj): The judge's removal of the appointed executor and replacement with another.
الفاسق (al-fasiq): A person who commits major sins or persists in minor sins; one who lacks justice (adalah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on the appointment of a slave as executor according to the correct Hanafi view?
A) It is void because a slave has no legal capacity.
B) It is valid, but the judge must replace the slave with a qualified executor.
C) It is valid and the slave remains executor unless the heirs object.
D) It is suspended until the slave is freed.

Key Takeaway
The appointment of an unqualified executor is valid but the judge must replace them; actions before removal are ineffective.

Executor's Persistent Insanity and Judge's Duty
Ruling when a sane executor becomes persistently insane

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the replacement of an executor who becomes persistently insane and apply it to cases of temporary incapacity and stipulated successors.

Core Idea
A persistently insane executor must be replaced by the judge; recovery before replacement restores his role.

This lesson examines a scenario where an executor loses legal capacity due to persistent insanity. The Hanafi school outlines clear duties for the judge and clarifies the fate of the original appointment, including edge cases from the Nawadir literature.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Ruling
State Abu Hanifa's ruling that the judge should appoint a replacement executor when the original executor becomes persistently insane.
The ruling is wajib on the judge to protect the estate.
2
Understand the Condition of Insanity
Recognize that the insanity must be 'mutbiq' (persistent and continuous without lucid intervals) to trigger replacement.
Temporary insanity does not remove the executor permanently.
3
Analyze the Temporal Element
If the judge delays and the executor recovers before removal, the original executor resumes his role.
The judge's failure to act does not automatically terminate the appointment.
4
Examine the Stipulated Successor Case
When the testator names a successor in case the first executor dies, but the first becomes insane, the judge appoints a temporary executor until the insane one dies, then the successor takes over.
Insanity is not death; the temporary executor fills the gap.
5
Apply to the Minor Executor Case
If a testator appoints his minor son as executor, the judge appoints an interim executor; when the son reaches puberty, he can be made executor and the interim removed only by explicit act.
Removal is not automatic upon majority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أوصى إلى عاقل فجن جنوناً مطبقاً قال أبو حنيفة : ينبغي للقاضي أن يجعل مكانه وصياً للميت فإن لم يفعل القاضي حتى أفاق الوصي كان وصياً على حاله. وفي نوادر إبراهيم عن محمد: إذا أوصى إلى رجل فقال إن مت أنت فالوصي بعدك أن فلان فجن الأول جنوناً مطبقاً فالقاضي يجعل مكانه وصياً حتى يموت الذي جن فيكون الذي سماه الموصى وصياً فقد ذكر ابن سماعة عن محمد رحمه الله في نوادره فيمن أوصى إلى ابن صغير له قال ؛ يجعل القاضي له وصياً يجوز أمره، وإذا بلغ ابنه جعله وصياً وأخرج الأول إن شاء ولا يخرج إلا بالإخراج.
If one appoints a sane person who then becomes persistently insane, Abu Hanifa said: the judge should appoint an executor for the deceased in his place. If the judge does not do so until the executor regains sanity, he remains executor. And in Nawadir Ibrahim from Muhammad: if a person appoints a man as executor and says 'if you die, the executor after you is so-and-so', then the first one becomes persistently insane; the judge appoints an executor in his place until the one who became insane dies, then the one named by the testator becomes executor. Ibn Sama'a reported from Muhammad in his Nawadir regarding one who appointed his minor son as executor: he said the judge appoints an executor for him (the minor) to manage his affairs; when his son reaches puberty, he makes him the executor and removes the first one if he wishes, and he is not removed except by removal.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where an initially qualified executor (sane, adult) becomes persistently insane after appointment. Abu Hanifa's ruling is that the judge should appoint a replacement executor for the deceased. However, if the judge delays and the executor recovers his sanity before removal, he resumes his role as executor from that point. The text then quotes a narration from Muhammad in Nawadir: if the testator stipulates a successor executor in case the first dies, but the first becomes insane (not dead), the judge appoints a temporary executor until the insane one dies, after which the stipulated successor takes over. Another case: if the testator appoints his minor son as executor, the judge appoints an interim executor; when the son reaches puberty, he can be made executor and the interim removed, but removal must be explicit. These examples illustrate how the school handles situations when the executor loses capacity due to insanity or minority.

Key Terms
الجنون المطبق (al-junun al-mutbiq): Persistent, continuous insanity without lucid intervals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what should a judge do when an executor becomes persistently insane?
A) Wait for the executor to recover before taking any action.
B) Appoint a replacement executor for the deceased immediately.
C) Ask the heirs to choose a new executor.
D) Declare the will invalid and let the estate be divided by inheritance.

Key Takeaway
The judge must replace a persistently insane executor, but if the executor recovers before replacement, he remains executor.
