Appointing One's Own Slave as Executor with Minor Heirs: Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on slave as executor with minor heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa permits appointing a slave as executor when heirs are minors and distinguish his reasoning from Abu Yusuf's qiyas-based objection.

Core Idea
A slave can be executor over minor heirs because he is responsible and minors lack authority.

This passage from *al-Bahr al-Ra'iq* presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: can a man appoint his own slave as executor (wasi) over his minor children? While Abu Hanifa holds it valid, his leading student Abu Yusuf argues by qiyas that it is invalid. We will dissect both positions, their proofs, and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi majority position
Identify Abu Hanifa's ruling: the appointment is valid (ja'iz). Note that this is the position supported by the matn and author.
The text says 'sahh' (valid) at the start.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Abu Yusuf holds it is invalid (la yajuz) by qiyas. Recognize that this is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not an external school.
The passage calls it 'al-qiyas' – the analogical reasoning.
3
Follow Abu Yusuf's qiyas
His qiyas: slavery negates guardianship (wilayah); executorship is a form of guardianship; thus a slave cannot be an executor. Also, it inverts the natural order of master-slave relationship.
He also argues that the father's guardianship is indivisible.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa counters that the slave is a responsible person (mukhatab mustanid bil-tasarruf) and thus fit. Minor heirs have no authority to object. The judge's power to sell the slave is removed once executorship is established.
He analogizes to a mukatab (slave with contract of manumission).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the appointment stands and the slave manages the estate; under Abu Yusuf, it is invalid and the court appoints another executor.
This affects the validity of the slave's subsequent actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( والى عبده وورثته صغار صح) أي إذا أوصى إلى عبد نفسه وورثته صغار جاز الإيصاء إليه، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقال أبو يوسف : لا يجوز وهو القياس لأن الولاية منعدمة لما أن الرق ينافيها ولان فيه الولاية للمولك على المالك وفي هذا قلب المشروع، ولأن الولاية الصادرة من الأب لا تتجزئ في اعتبار هذه الولاية تجزؤها لا يملك بيع رقبته وهذا خلاف الموضوع. ولأبي حنيفة أنه مخاطب مستند بالتصرف فيكون أهلاً للوصاية وليس لأحد عليه الولاية فإن الصغار وإن كانوا ملاكاً فليس لهم ولاية التصرف فلا منافاة فإن قيل : إن لم يكن لهم ذلك فللقاضي يبيعه فيتحقق المنع والمنافاة أجيب بأنه إذا ثبت الإيصاء لم يبق للقاضي ولاية بخلاف ما إذا كان في الورثة كبار أو أوصى إلى عبد الغير لأنه لا يستبد إذا كان للمولى منعه بخلاف الأول لأنه ليس له بيعه وإيصاء المولى إليه يؤذن بكونه ناظراً لهم فصار كالمكاتب والوصايا قد تجزأ على ما رواه الحسن عن أبي حنيفة كما إذا أوصى لرجلين أحدهما يكون في الدين والآخر في العين فيكون كل واحد منهما وصياً فيما أوصى إليه خاصة أو نقول يصار إليه كيلا يؤدي إلى إبطال أصله وتعيين الوصف بإبطال عموم الولاية أولى من إبطال أصل الإيصاء، وقول محمد فيه مضطرب ويروى مع أبي حنيفة ويروى مع أبي يوسف. قال رحمه الله : (وإلا لا يعني إن لم تكن الورثة صغاراً بأن كانوا كلهم أو بعضهم كباراً لا يجوز الإيصاء لأن الكبير له أن يمنعه أو يبيع نصيبه فيمنعه المشتري فيعجز عن الوفاء بما التزم فلا يفسد.
He (the author) said: 'And to his slave while his heirs are minors is valid' i.e., if one appoints his own slave as executor while his heirs are minors, the appointment is permissible according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: it is not permissible, and that is by qiyas because guardianship is nonexistent since slavery negates it, and because it involves the master having authority over the master, and this inverts the prescribed order, and because the guardianship emanating from the father is not divisible; considering this guardianship as divisible would mean the father cannot sell his [the slave's] person, which contradicts the subject. For Abu Hanifa: he is a responsible person capable of acting, thus he is fit for executorship, and no one has guardianship over him. Minors, even if they are owners, do not have the authority to act, so there is no conflict. If it is said: if they do not have that, the judge could sell him, so the prevention and conflict occur. It is answered: if the executorship is established, the judge no longer has authority, unlike the case where the heirs include adults or if he appointed another's slave, because he is not independent if his master can prevent him, unlike the first case because he (the master) cannot sell him and the master's appointment indicates that he is overseeing them, so he becomes like a mukatab. Executorships may be divided as related by al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa: if one appoints two persons, one for debts and the other for cash, each is an executor for his specific area. Or we say it is upheld to avoid nullifying its foundation; specifying the description by nullifying general guardianship is better than nullifying the original appointment. Muhammad's statement is contradictory: sometimes narrated with Abu Hanifa, sometimes with Abu Yusuf. He (the author) said: 'otherwise no' meaning if the heirs are not minors, i.e., if all or some are adults, the appointment is not permissible because the adult can prevent him or sell his share, and the buyer would prevent him, making him unable to fulfill his commitment, so it is not valid.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of appointing one's own slave as executor when the heirs are minors. Abu Hanifa holds it valid, while Abu Yusuf argues by qiyas that it is invalid because slavery negates the capacity for guardianship, and it would create an inversion of authority (the master becomes subordinate to his slave in some sense). Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the slave is a responsible person (mukallaf) capable of acting, and the minor heirs do not have the authority to act, so there is no conflict. The objection that the judge could sell the slave is rebutted: once executorship is established, the judge loses that authority. The ruling distinguishes from the case of adult heirs or another's slave, where the master's right to prevent or sell his share would cause conflict. Muhammad's position is reported as inconsistent. The passage concludes that if heirs include adults, the appointment is invalid because the adult heir can prevent or sell, leading to incapacity of the slave to fulfill duties. This illustrates how Hanafi imams used textual reasoning (nass) and analogy (qiyas) to derive rulings.

Key Terms
الرق (al-riqq): Slavery, a legal status that negates full legal capacity for certain actions, particularly guardianship.
وصاية (wisayah): Executorship; the role of managing a deceased's estate for heirs, especially minors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Abu Hanifa consider appointing one's own slave as executor valid when heirs are minors?
A) Because the slave is not a responsible person.
B) Because minors have authority over the slave.
C) Because the slave is a responsible person and minors do not have authority to act.
D) Because the judge has no authority over the estate.

Key Takeaway
The dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on whether a slave can be executor for minor heirs turns on whether executorship requires full guardianship (negated by slavery) or simply the capacity to act as a responsible person.

Judge's Role When an Executor Becomes Incapacitated
Ruling on executor incapacity and judge's options

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a judge may add or replace an executor and apply the Hanafi principle of preserving both the executor's and heirs' rights.

Core Idea
A judge may only remove a capable, trustworthy executor upon proven treachery, but may add or replace upon verified incapacity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a judge's role when an executor becomes unable to fulfill his duties. The author explains the principles of 'dual observance' (مراعاة الحقين) that balance the testator's choice and the heirs' interests. Understanding this ruling is essential for judges and legal practitioners dealing with estate administration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is the judge's decision regarding an executor who cannot carry out duties. The ruling is that it is permissible (jaiz) for the judge to either appoint a co-executor (ضم) or replace the executor (استبدال) depending on the degree of incapacity.
The text uses 'ضم' for adding and 'استبدل' for replacing.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the rationale (talil) provided: 'because in joining there is observance of both rights' (لأن في الضم رعاية الحقين). The principle is that the judge must act to protect both the executor's right (to remain as per testator's choice) and the heirs' right (to proper management).
This is a maslaha-based argument, not a textual scriptural proof.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the rationale yield the ruling? By adding a co-executor, the original executor is not removed (preserving his right), but the oversight is improved (preserving heirs' right). Total replacement occurs only when incapacity is total (عجز أصلاً), because then the executor's right is lost.
The judge must first verify incapacity before acting (لا يجيبه حتى يعرف ذلك حقيقة).
4
Note the condition for removal
If the executor is capable and trustworthy, the judge may not remove him. The testator's choice is paramount, even over the testator's father. Removal is only allowed if treachery (خيانة) is proven, because that voids the trust.
The text gives an analogy: the testator would have removed the executor if alive, so the judge stands in his place.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider an executor who becomes physically disabled but is honest. The judge should first verify. If the incapacity is partial, appoint a co-executor; if total, replace. If the executor is capable but a heir complains, the judge must not remove until treachery is proven.
The key is distinguishing between incapacity (physical/mental) and breach of trust (treachery).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ومن عجز عن القيام ضم إليه غيره) لأن في الضم رعاية الحقين حق الوصي وحق الورثة لأن تكميل النظر يحصل به لأن النظر يتم بإعانة غيره، ولو شكى الوصي إليه ذلك فلا يجيبه حتى يعرف ذلك حقيقة لأن الشاكي قد يكون كاذباً على نفسه، ولو ظهر للقاضي عجزه أصلاً استبدل به غيره رعاية للنظر من الجانبين، ولو كان قادراً على التصرف وهو أمين فيه ليس للقاضي أن يخرجه لأن مختار الميت، ولو اختار غيره كان دونه فكان إبقاؤه أولى ألا ترى أنه قدم على أب الميت مع وفور شفقته فأولى أن يقدم على غيره، وكذا إذا شك الورثة أو بعضهم الوصي إليه لا ينبغي له أن يعزله حتى تبدو له منه خيانة لأنه استفاد الولاية من الميت غير أنه إذا ظهرت الخيانة فاتت الأمانة، والميت إنما اختاره لأجلها وليس من النظر ابقاؤه بعد فواتها ولو كان حياً لأخرجه منها فينوب القاضي منابه عند عجزه ويقيم غيره مقامه كأنه مات ولا وصي له .
He (the author) said: 'And whoever is incapable of carrying out (the duties), the judge appoints another alongside him' because in joining there is observance of both rights: the right of the executor and the right of the heirs, because complete oversight is achieved thereby, as oversight is perfected by assistance from another. If the executor complains to him (the judge) about that, he should not respond until he knows it truly, because the complainant may be lying about himself. If incapacity becomes evident to the judge completely, he replaces him with another, observing oversight from both sides. If he is capable of acting and is trustworthy, the judge may not remove him because he is the testator's choice; if he had chosen another, it would be inferior, so keeping him is better. Do you not see that he was preferred over the testator's father, despite his abundant affection, so it is more appropriate that he be preferred over others. Similarly, if the heirs or some of them complain about the executor, the judge should not remove him until evidence of treachery appears from him, because he acquired the authority from the testator. However, if treachery appears, the trust is lost; the testator only chose him for that trust, and it is not in the interest to keep him after its loss. If he were alive, he would have removed him, so the judge substitutes for him in case of his incapacity and puts another in his place, as if he died and had no executor.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on dealing with an executor who becomes incapable of fulfilling his duties. The judge has the option to appoint a co-executor (ضم) rather than full replacement, achieving both the executor's right (to be retained) and the heirs' right (to proper management). The judge must verify claims of incapacity, as the executor might falsely claim it. If total incapacity is proven, the judge replaces him entirely. A capable and trustworthy executor cannot be removed by the judge because he was the testator's choice, and the testator's preference is respected even over the next of kin. The judge can only remove if treachery (خيانة) is proven, as the testator chose the executor for trustworthiness. In case of incapacity (mental or physical), the judge acts as the testator's substitute and appoints a replacement, as if the executor died without appointing a successor.

Key Terms
العجز (al-ajz): Incapacity to fulfill the duties of executorship, whether physical, mental, or due to external constraints.
الخيانة (al-khiyanah): Breach of trust by the executor, such as embezzlement or negligence, which justifies removal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition can the judge remove a capable and trustworthy executor?
A) If the heirs complain.
B) If the executor requests to be relieved.
C) Only if treachery becomes evident.
D) If the testator's father objects.

Key Takeaway
A judge may add or replace an executor only upon proven incapacity, and remove only upon proven treachery, respecting the testator's choice and the heirs' interests.

Appointing a Slave as Executor: The Hanafi Dispute
Explains the ruling of appointing a slave as executor and the condition of heirs being minors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on appointing a slave as executor, explain the proofs for each, and identify the practical consequence of their disagreement.

Core Idea
A slave can be appointed executor if heirs are minors, per Abu Hanifa; Abu Yusuf rejects it because slavery negates guardianship.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a testator can appoint his own slave as executor (wasi) when the heirs are minors. Abu Hanifa rules it valid, while Abu Yusuf argues it is invalid based on qiyas. The commentary explains both positions and provides Abu Hanifa's rebuttals, highlighting key principles of legal capacity and guardianship.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its condition
Identify the ruling according to Abu Hanifa: the appointment of one's own slave as executor is valid when the heirs are minors. Note that this is the position advocated in the matn.
The condition 'heirs are minors' is crucial; if any heir is adult, the ruling changes.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's objection and his proof
Abu Yusuf holds the appointment invalid. His proof (qiyas) is that slavery negates guardianship (wilayah), it creates an inversion of authority (owned over owner), and the father's guardianship is indivisible.
Abu Yusuf's argument relies on the general principle that a slave lacks legal capacity for guardianship.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa argues that a slave is a responsible person (mukhatab) and thus fit for executorship. The minors have no authority over him, and the testator's appointment removes any intervention by the judge. The slave is analogous to a mukatab.
Abu Hanifa also cites that executorship can be divided, as narrated by Hasan ibn Ziyad.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the ruling is valid, the slave manages the minors' property; if invalid, the judge appoints another executor.
This shows that even a theoretical dispute has real-world implications for estate administration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( والى عبده وورثته صغار صح) أي إذا أوصى إلى عبد نفسه وورثته صغار جاز الإيصاء إليه، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقال أبو يوسف : لا يجوز وهو القياس لأن الولاية منعدمة لما أن الرق ينافيها ولان فيه الولاية للمولك على المالك وفي هذا قلب المشروع، ولأن الولاية الصادرة من الأب لا تتجزئ في اعتبار هذه الولاية تجزؤها لا يملك بيع رقبته وهذا خلاف الموضوع. ولأبي حنيفة أنه مخاطب مستند بالتصرف فيكون أهلاً للوصاية وليس لأحد عليه الولاية فإن الصغار وإن كانوا ملاكاً فليس لهم ولاية التصرف فلا منافاة فإن قيل : إن لم يكن لهم ذلك فللقاضي يبيعه فيتحقق المنع والمنافاة أجيب بأنه إذا ثبت الإيصاء لم يبق للقاضي ولاية بخلاف ما إذا كان في الورثة كبار أو أوصى إلى عبد الغير لأنه لا يستبد إذا كان للمولى منعه بخلاف الأول لأنه ليس له بيعه وإيصاء المولى إليه يؤذن بكونه ناظراً لهم فصار كالمكاتب والوصايا قد تجزأ على ما رواه الحسن عن أبي حنيفة كما إذا أوصى لرجلين أحدهما يكون في الدين والآخر في العين فيكون كل واحد منهما وصياً فيما أوصى إليه خاصة أو نقول يصار إليه كيلا يؤدي إلى إبطال أصله وتعيين الوصف بإبطال عموم الولاية أولى من إبطال أصل الإيصاء، وقول محمد فيه مضطرب ويروى مع أبي حنيفة ويروى مع أبي يوسف. قال رحمه الله : (وإلا لا يعني إن لم تكن الورثة صغاراً بأن كانوا كلهم أو بعضهم كباراً لا يجوز الإيصاء لأن الكبير له أن يمنعه أو يبيع نصيبه فيمنعه المشتري فيعجز عن الوفاء بما التزم فلا يفسد.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And to his slave, and his heirs are minors, it is valid) meaning if he appoints his own slave as executor and his heirs are minors, the appointment is permissible. This is according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: It is not permissible, and that is the analogy (qiyas) because guardianship is absent since slavery negates it, and because it involves giving authority to the owned over the owner, which overturns the legislation, and because the guardianship issued by the father is indivisible, and in considering this guardianship, its division leads to the slave not being able to sell his own person, which contradicts the subject. For Abu Hanifa, he is a responsible person capable of acting, so he is fit for executorship, and no one has guardianship over him. The minors, though they are owners, have no right to dispose, so there is no contradiction. If it is said: If they do not have that, then the judge can sell him, so prevention and contradiction occur, the answer is that when executorship is established, the judge no longer has authority, unlike when there are adult heirs or when he appoints a slave belonging to another, because he is not independent if the master can prevent him, unlike the first case because the master cannot sell him. The master's appointment indicates that he is a guardian for them, so he becomes like a mukatab (slave under contract of manumission). Executorships can be divided according to what Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa, such as if he appoints two men, one for debts and the other for tangible property, so each becomes an executor for what he was appointed to specifically, or we say it is resorted to so as not to lead to nullifying its origin, and specifying the attribute by nullifying general guardianship is better than nullifying the origin of the executorship. Muhammad's statement is contradictory; it is narrated both with Abu Hanifa and with Abu Yusuf. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if not, meaning if the heirs are not minors—that is, all or some of them are adults—the appointment is not valid because the adult can prevent him or sell his share, so the buyer prevents him, and he becomes unable to fulfill what he undertook, so it becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of appointing a slave as executor when the deceased's heirs are minors. The matn states that it is valid (sahih) according to Abu Hanifa, while Abu Yusuf considers it invalid based on qiyas (analogy). The commentary first presents Abu Yusuf's reasons: a slave lacks guardianship (wilayah) due to the deficiency of slavery, it creates a reversal where the owned has authority over the owner, and the guardianship from the father (the testator) cannot be divided. Abu Hanifa counters that a slave is a responsible being (mukhatab) capable of transactions and therefore eligible for executorship; the minors have no guardianship over their own property, so there is no conflict. An objection that the judge could sell the slave if needed is answered: once executorship is established, the judge loses that authority, unlike if the heirs were adults or the slave belonged to another. The testator's appointment implies the slave is a guardian for the minors, analogous to a mukatab. The commentary also notes that executorship can be divided, citing a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa. Muhammad al-Shaybani's position is uncertain, as he is reported with both Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. The second part explains the exception: if heirs are not minors, the appointment is invalid because an adult heir has the right to prevent the slave or sell his share, leading to incapacitation.

Key Terms
وصي (wasi): Executor appointed in a will to manage the estate.
ولاية (wilayah): Guardianship or authority over another's affairs.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave under a contract of manumission (kitaba), who has partial legal capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when is it valid to appoint a slave as executor?
A) Only if the heirs are all adults.
B) If the heirs are minors and the slave is the testator's own slave.
C) Always, regardless of the heirs.
D) If the slave belongs to someone else.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits appointing a slave as executor when heirs are minors, overriding the general rule that slavery negates guardianship, due to the testator's intent and the slave's capacity.

Joining Another Person to an Incapable Executor
Explains procedure when executor is incapable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a judge may join another person to an executor or replace him, and explain the Hanafi reasoning for preserving both rights.

Core Idea
The judge cannot remove a capable and trustworthy executor chosen by the deceased; only verified incapability or betrayal justifies intervention.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the judge's role regarding an executor who is unable to fulfill duties. The matn states that another person is joined to him, and the commentary unfolds the legal reasoning based on preserving the rights of both the executor and the heirs. The judge must verify the executor's condition before acting, distinguishing between incapability and mere complaint.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The issue concerns an executor who is incapable of performing duties. The passage discusses how to balance preserving the executor's right (chosen by the deceased) and the heirs' right (proper estate management).
The core principle is 'ri'ayat al-haqqayn' (preserving both rights).
2
Determine the judge's duty of verification
The judge must not act on mere complaints; he must verify the executor's incapability because the executor may be falsely claiming inability.
This safeguard prevents unjust removal based on false claims.
3
Analyze the judge's options
If the executor is absolutely unable, the judge may replace him entirely. If capable and trustworthy, the judge must keep him. The judge can also appoint a co-executor to assist.
The default is to preserve the original executor's position.
4
Understand the limits on removing a trustworthy executor
Even if heirs suspect the executor, the judge cannot remove him without clear evidence of betrayal (khiyanah). The executor is preferred even over the deceased's father, so his removal requires strong justification.
The analogy with the father underscores the high bar for removal.
5
Recognize the judge's role as substitute for the deceased
When betrayal is proven, the judge removes the executor because trust is lost. The judge acts as the deceased's substitute, just as if no executor existed.
This ensures the estate is managed properly despite the failure of the original appointment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ومن عجز عن القيام ضم إليه غيره) لأن في الضم رعاية الحقين حق الوصي وحق الورثة لأن تكميل النظر يحصل به لأن النظر يتم بإعانة غيره، ولو شكى الوصي إليه ذلك فلا يجيبه حتى يعرف ذلك حقيقة لأن الشاكي قد يكون كاذباً على نفسه، ولو ظهر للقاضي عجزه أصلاً استبدل به غيره رعاية للنظر من الجانبين، ولو كان قادراً على التصرف وهو أمين فيه ليس للقاضي أن يخرجه لأن مختار الميت، ولو اختار غيره كان دونه فكان إبقاؤه أولى ألا ترى أنه قدم على أب الميت مع وفور شفقته فأولى أن يقدم على غيره، وكذا إذا شك الورثة أو بعضهم الوصي إليه لا ينبغي له أن يعزله حتى تبدو له منه خيانة لأنه استفاد الولاية من الميت غير أنه إذا ظهرت الخيانة فاتت الأمانة، والميت إنما اختاره لأجلها وليس من النظر ابقاؤه بعد فواتها ولو كان حياً لأخرجه منها فينوب القاضي منابه عند عجزه ويقيم غيره مقامه كأنه مات ولا وصي له .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And whoever is unable to carry out [the duties], another is joined to him) because in joining there is preservation of both rights: the right of the executor and the right of the heirs, since complete supervision is achieved by the assistance of another. If the executor complains about his inability, the judge should not respond until he knows the truth of the matter, because the complainant may be lying about himself. If the judge sees that he is absolutely unable, he may replace him completely, preserving supervision from both sides. If he is capable of acting and trustworthy, the judge should not remove him because he is the choice of the deceased. Had the deceased chosen another, that other would have been inferior to him, so keeping him is preferable. Do you not see that he was preferred over the father of the deceased despite his abundant compassion, so he is even more preferable over others? Similarly, if the heirs or some of them suspect the executor, the judge should not dismiss him until clear betrayal appears, because the executor derives his authority from the deceased. However, if betrayal appears, trust is gone, and the deceased only chose him for that trust. It is not in the interest to keep him after losing it. If the deceased were alive, he would have removed him. So the judge acts as his substitute when he is incapacitated and appoints another in his place, as if he died and left no executor.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage details the ruling on when an executor is incapable of performing his duties. The matn states that a co-executor (or a replacement) should be appointed. The commentary explains that the judge should first verify the incapability, because the executor might be falsely claiming inability. If the judge determines the executor is genuinely unable, he may add another person or replace him entirely. However, if the executor is capable and trustworthy, the judge cannot remove him because the deceased chose him; the judge's role is to preserve the deceased's choice. The executor is preferred even over the father of the deceased, so keeping him is more important. Even if heirs suspect the executor, the judge must not dismiss him without clear evidence of betrayal, because the authority comes from the deceased. Only when betrayal is proven does the executor lose trust, and the judge may replace him, acting as the deceased's substitute as if no executor existed.

Key Terms
ضم (damm): Joining or appointing an additional person to assist or replace the executor.
عجز (ajz): Incapacity or inability to perform the duties of executorship.
خيانة (khiyanah): Betrayal of trust, e.g., misappropriation of estate property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can the judge remove an executor and appoint a replacement?
A) Immediately upon the request of the heirs.
B) When the executor claims he is unable, without verification.
C) When the executor becomes incapable or shows clear betrayal.
D) Only if all heirs agree.

Key Takeaway
The judge must preserve the deceased's choice of executor unless verified incapability or betrayal justifies intervention.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Can One Executor Act Alone?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on one executor acting alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the invalidity of one executor acting alone, explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement with Abu Yusuf, and apply the ruling to distinguish executors from agents.

Core Idea
One of two executors cannot act alone; action is suspended pending the other's approval, except for necessities.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether one of two appointed executors can independently manage the estate. The matn declares such action invalid, following Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, but Abu Yusuf dissents. We will explore the reasoning behind each position and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The matn states: 'The action of one of the two executors is invalid.' Determine what 'invalid' means — it is not absolutely void but suspended (mawquf) pending the other's approval.
The exception for necessities like shrouding and minor's needs is noted.
2
State the Intra-Hanafi Disagreement
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that one executor cannot act alone; Abu Yusuf holds each can act independently. Note the two scenarios: appointment by separate contracts vs. single contract.
There is a further sub-disagreement on which scenario is contested.
3
Analyze the Arguments
The Hanafi majority argue that joining a second executor implies the first's inability; the testator intended joint action, so each is like half a cause. Abu Yusuf analogizes to guardianship in marriage, which is indivisible.
Compare the response that marriage guardianship arises from kinship, not appointment.
4
Understand the Exception
Urgent matters (tajhiz, shrouding, minor's needs) allow one executor to act alone out of necessity, not because of inherent authority.
This exception is always applicable, not a case of authority.
5
Distinguish from Agency
In agency (wakalah), if two are appointed separately, each can act alone by consensus. The difference is the testator's intent and the principal's presence.
The agent's independence is presumed unless specified otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( ويبطل فعل أحد الوصيين) أي إذا أوصى إلى اثنين لم يكن لأحدهما أن يتصرف في مال الميت فإن تصرف فيه فهو باطل وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد. وقال أبو يوسف : ينفرد كل واحد منهما بالتصرف. ثم قيل : الخلاف فيما إذا أوصى إلى كل واحد منهما بعقد، وأما إذا أوصى إليهما معاً أو أوصى إليهما بعقد على حدة ومحل الخلاف إذا كان ذلك في عقدين، وأما إذا كان في عقد واحد فلا ينفرد أحدهما بالإجماع فكذا ذكره الكيساني. وقيل : : الخلاف في الفصلين جميعاً ذكره أبو بكر الإسكاف. وقال في المبسوط : وهو الأصح. ولا يخفى أن المراد من البطلان التوقف على إجازة الآخر أو رده بخلاف الوكيلين إذا وكلهما متفرقاً حيث ينفرد كل واحد منهما بالتصرف بالإجماع والفرق أن ضم الثاني في الأيصاء دليل على عجز الأول عن المباشرة وحده وهذا لأن ضم الإيصاء إلى الثاني يقصد به الاشتراك الأول مع وهو يملك الرجوع عن الوصية للأول فيملك اشتراك الثاني معه، وقد يوصي الإنسان إلى غيره على أنه يتمكن من اتمام مقصوده وحده ثم يتبين له عجزه عن ذلك فيضم إليه غيره فصار بمنزلة الإيصاء إليهما معاً ولا كذلك الوكالة فإن رأى الموكل قائم. ولو كان الوكيل عاجزاً لباشر بنفسه لتمكنه من ذلك، ولما وكل علم أن مراده أن ينفرد كل واحد منهما بالتصرف، ولأن وجوب الوصية عند الموت فيثبت لهما معاً بخلاف الوكالة المتعاقبة، فإذا ثبت أن الخلاف فيهما معاً فأبو يوسف يقول : إن الوصايا سبيلها الولاية وهي وصف شرعي لا يتجزئ فيثبت كل واحد كاملاً كولاية الإنكاح للأخوين وهذه لأن الوصايا خلافة وإنما تتحقق الخلافة إذا انتقلت إليه كذلك فلأن اختيار الموصي أيهما يؤذن باختصاص كل واحد منهما بالشفقة إليه ولهما أن الولاية تنبت عند الموت فيراعى وصف ذلك وهو وصف الاجتماع لأنه شرط مفيد لأن رأي الواحد لا يكون كرأي الاثنين ولم يرض الموصي إلا بالأثنين فصار كل واحد في هذا السبب بمنزلة شطر العلة وهو لا يثبت به لحكم فكان باطلاً بخلاف الأخوين في النكاح لأن السبب هناك القرابة وقد قامت بكل واحد منهما كملاً، ولأن الإنكاح حق مستحق لها على الولي حتى لو طالبته بإنكاحها من كفء يخطبها يجب عليه وههنا حق التصرف للوصي ولهذا بقي مخيراً في التصرف ففي الوليين أولى حتماً على صاحبه وفي الوصيين استوفى حقاً لصاحبه فلا يصح نظير الأول إيفاء دين عليهما. ونظير الثاني استيفاء دين لهما حيث يجوز في الأول دون الثاني بخلاف مواضع الاستثناء لأنها من باب الضرورة لا من باب الولاية على ما نبينه ومواضع الضرورة مستثناة دائماً أبداً وهو استثناه في الكتاب وأخواتها. وفي التتارخانية : رجل أوصى إلى رجلين فمات أحدهما وأوصى إلى صاحبه جاز ويكون لصاحبه أن يتصرف، وروي أنه لا يجوز والصحيح الأول. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث : إذا أوصى إلى رجلين فقبل أحدهما وسكت الآخر فقال الذي قبل ما
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the action of one of the two executors is invalid) meaning if he appoints two executors, neither of them may act in the property of the deceased; if one acts, it is invalid. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: Each of them may act independently. Then it is said: The disagreement is when he appointed each of them by a separate contract. As for when he appointed both together or appointed them by a single contract, the disagreement is regarding two contracts. As for a single contract, neither acts alone by consensus. Al-Kasani mentioned this. It is also said: The disagreement applies to both cases, as mentioned by Abu Bakr al-Iskaf. In al-Mabsut, he said this is the more correct. It is not hidden that the meaning of invalidity is that it is suspended upon the approval or rejection of the other, unlike the case of two agents if they are appointed separately, where each acts alone by consensus. The difference is that joining the second in the executorship indicates the first's inability to act alone, because joining the second intends partnership with the first, and the testator has the right to revoke the executorship of the first, so he can include the second with him. A man may appoint another thinking he can accomplish his purpose alone, then realizes his inability, so he joins another, making it like appointing both together. This is not the case with agency, for the principal is present; if the agent were unable, the principal would act himself. When he appointed, he knew his intention that each would act independently. And because executorship becomes obligatory at death, so it is established for both together, unlike subsequent agency. When it is established that the disagreement applies to both cases, Abu Yusuf says: Executorships are a form of guardianship, a legal attribute that is indivisible, so each is established fully like the guardianship of marriage for two brothers. Because executorship is a deputyship, and deputyship is realized only when it transfers to him like that, because the testator's choice of each indicates his special affection for each. And they also say: Guardianship arises at death, so its attribute (social aspect) is considered, which is beneficial, because one person's opinion is not like two, and the testator was only satisfied with two, so each one in this cause is like half a cause, which does not establish the ruling, so it is invalid. Unlike the case of two brothers in marriage, because the cause there is kinship, which is complete in each. And because the marriage is a right due to her from the guardian, such that if she demands marriage to a suitable suitor, he must do it. Here, the right to act belongs to the executor, so he remains free to act. For the two guardians, the duty is binding on one's companion; for two executors, one collects a right from his companion, so it is not valid. The first is like paying a debt on them; the second is like collecting a debt for them. The first is allowed, the second is not. Except in cases of necessity, which are always exempt, as he will explain, and the book and its counterparts make an exception. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: A man appointed two executors; one died and appointed his companion, it is valid, and the companion may act. It is also narrated that it is not valid, but the first is correct. In Fatawa of Abu al-Layth: If a man appoints two executors, one accepted and the other remained silent, then the one who accepted said...
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage covers an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether one of two appointed executors can act alone. The matn states that the action of one alone is invalid, which is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding that each can act independently. The commentary explores the disagreement in detail. Two scenarios are discussed: whether the testator appointed each executor by a separate contract or by a single contract. In the case of a single contract, all agree that one cannot act alone. The disagreement is when the appointment is by two separate contracts. According to al-Kasani, the dispute is limited to that; al-Iskaf says it applies to both. Al-Sarakhsi in al-Mabsut considers the latter correct. The term 'invalid' (batil) here means the action is suspended pending the other's approval, unlike agency where two agents appointed separately can act independently. The reasoning: joining a second executor implies the first is incapable alone. The testator has the right to revoke the first, so he can make them partners. In agency, the principal is alive and could act if needed, so independence is the norm. Abu Yusuf argues that executorship is a form of guardianship (wilayah), which is indivisible, so each executor has full authority, analogous to two brothers each having full authority to give a woman in marriage. The counter-argument: guardianship in marriage arises from kinship (complete in each), while executorship arises from the testator's choice which implies joint action. The testator was satisfied only with the collective opinion of two. Thus each is like half a cause, insufficient for a full ruling. The exception is for necessary items like shrouding and purchasing the shroud and needs of minors, where one can act alone out of necessity. The passage ends with a quote from al-Tatarkhaniyya: if one of two executors dies and appoints the other as his own executor, the surviving one can act alone. And from Fatawa of Abu al-Layth: if one accepts and the other remains silent, the one who accepted... (cut off).

Key Terms
وصيان (wasiyyan): Two executors appointed jointly in a will.
تفرد بالتصرف (tafarrud bi al-tasarruf): Acting independently without the other executor's involvement.
تجهيز (tajhiz): Preparing the deceased for burial, including shrouding and related expenses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is valid and binding if the other executor later approves.
C) It is valid immediately because each executor has full authority.
D) It is valid only for necessary expenses like shrouding.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's relied-upon position: one of two executors cannot act alone; any such action is suspended pending the other's approval, except for urgent necessities.

Silence as Acceptance of Executorship
Ruling on silent person accepting executorship by saying yes to buying for deceased

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that a silent person accepts executorship by performing a positive action, such as agreeing to buy for the deceased after the testator's death.

Core Idea
Silence alone does not accept executorship; a positive act like saying 'yes' to a purchase does.

In Hanafi fiqh, acceptance of an executorship (wasiyyah) is not automatic. A person who remains silent when informed of the appointment is not considered an executor. However, if after the testator's death, the silent person takes a positive step—like agreeing to purchase something for the deceased—that action is legally deemed acceptance of the role. This lesson explores this specific rule from the Hanafi school, which clarifies the boundary between inaction and implied consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage addresses a person who was silent when offered executorship. After the testator's death, someone asks him to buy something for the deceased.
The person had not explicitly accepted or rejected the role earlier.
2
Identify the trigger action
The request 'Buy this for the deceased' is made to the silent person.
The request must be after the testator's death.
3
Identify the response
The person responds with 'yes' (نعم).
This is a verbal or explicit positive response.
4
Determine the ruling
The passage rules that this response constitutes acceptance (قبول) of the executorship.
The ruling is specific to this set of circumstances.
5
Understand the underlying principle
Silence is not a valid form of acceptance in Hanafi law; a positive action indicating consent is required.
This principle prevents unintended liabilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للساكت بعد موت الموصي اشتر هذا للميت فقال نعم كان قبولاً للوصية
For the silent one after the death of the testator: 'Buy this for the deceased' and he said 'yes' – it is acceptance of the executorship.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the Hanafi school. The passage states that if a person remains silent regarding an offer of executorship, but later after the testator's death, someone asks him to buy something for the deceased and he agrees, that agreement constitutes acceptance of the executorship. The ruling is based on the principle that silence is not acceptance, but a positive action (saying 'yes' to a request) indicates consent. This is a straightforward ruling.

Key Terms
وصي (waṣī): Executor appointed to carry out the testator's wishes.
قبول (qabūl): Acceptance of the role of executor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What constitutes acceptance of executorship for a silent person after the testator's death?
A) Remaining silent
B) Saying 'yes' to a request to buy for the deceased
C) Proposing to buy something for the deceased
D) Informing someone of the executorship

Key Takeaway
A silent person accepts executorship by a positive action—such as agreeing to buy for the deceased—after the testator's death.

Death of One Executor Before Disposal: General vs Specific Bequest
Ruling on validity of one-third disposal when one of two executors dies before acting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a bequest when one of two appointed executors dies before acting, and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
The death of one executor before disposal invalidates a general bequest, but a specific designation allows the judge to appoint a replacement or permit the survivor to act; Abu Yusuf allows the survivor to act alone.

When a testator appoints two executors to dispose of one-third of the estate, the death of one before any action raises a question: does the bequest fall, or can the survivor proceed? The Hanafi school has nuanced answers depending on the wording used. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the ruling and an intra-madhhab difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two wordings
The text presents two scenarios: (1) the testator says 'Place a third of my wealth wherever you wish' (general authority), (2) the testator says 'I made a third of my wealth for the poor' (specific purpose).
The wording determines the outcome.
2
Determine the ruling for general wording
If one executor dies before any disposal, the bequest becomes invalid and the third returns to the heirs. The joint authority is essential; the survivor cannot act alone.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
3
Determine the ruling for specific wording
If the testator said 'for the poor', the judge appoints another executor or, if he wishes, tells the survivor to act alone. The bequest does not automatically fail.
The specific purpose preserves the bequest.
4
Note Abu Yusuf's alternative opinion
Abu Yusuf has another view: in the case of a specific purpose, the surviving executor may give charity alone without needing the judge's permission.
This is a lenient position within the Hanafi school.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa/Muhammad, the survivor cannot act alone even for a specific purpose without judge's appointment; under Abu Yusuf, he can act alone for a specific purpose.
The general wording case is agreed upon: the bequest fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أوصى إلى رجلين وقال لهما ضعا ثلث مالي حيث شئتما فمات أحدهما قبل أن يفعلا ذلك بطلت الثلث لورثة الميت ولو قال جعلت ثلث مالي للمساكين والمسألة بحالها قال : يجعل القاضي وصياً آخر، وإن شاء يقول للثاني منهما اقسم أنت وحدك. وعلى قول أبي يوسف الآخر له أن يتصدق وحده
And if he appoints two men as executors and says to them: 'Place a third of my wealth wherever you wish', then one of them dies before they do that, the third becomes invalid for the heirs of the deceased. And if he said: 'I made a third of my wealth for the poor' and the case is the same, he said: The judge appoints another executor, and if he wishes, he says to the remaining one: 'You divide it alone.' And according to the other opinion of Abu Yusuf, he may give charity alone.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding two executors appointed to dispose of one-third of the estate. If one dies before acting, the ruling differs based on the wording. If the testator said 'make a third for the poor', the judge appoints a replacement or allows the remaining executor to act alone. According to Abu Yusuf's other opinion, the survivor can act alone. This shows the importance of the testator's exact wording and the emphasis on collective authority initially.

Key Terms
وصيان (waṣiyyān): Two executors jointly appointed.
الثُلُث (ath-thuluth): One-third of the estate, the maximum for bequests.
القاضي (al-qāḍī): The judge who oversees estate matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The survivor may act alone.

C) The bequest becomes invalid for the heirs.
D) The bequest is divided between the heirs of the two executors.

Key Takeaway
The fate of a bequest when one of two executors dies depends critically on the testator's wording: general authority leads to invalidation, while a specific purpose preserves the bequest with judicial oversight, with an intra-Hanafi lenient option from Abu Yusuf.

Executor Buying from Co-Executor at Testator's Price
Ruling on purchase between executors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which an executor may purchase property from a co-executor at the price set by the testator.

Core Idea
An executor may buy from a co-executor at the testator's price only if each was delegated to act alone.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from Hanafi wasiyyah law: two executors, one owns a slave worth more than the testator's specified price. Can the other executor buy it at that price? The ruling hinges on whether the testator granted each executor independent authority. Understanding this distinction clarifies the boundaries of executor powers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the parties
The act is one executor buying a slave from his co-executor; the price is set by the testator. The parties are two executors, one of whom owns the property.
The testator is deceased; the executors act on behalf of the estate.
2
State the default ruling
Without explicit delegation, such a purchase is generally not permissible as it may involve a conflict of interest.
3
Identify the condition
The condition is tafwid: the testator delegated to each executor the authority to act independently in the matter.
The Arabic verb 'fawwada' is the root of tafwid.
4
Apply the condition to the scenario
If tafwid is present, the purchase is permissible (ja'iz).
The text emphasizes this condition explicitly.
5
Note the additional nuance
If the co-executor sells the item to a stranger (ajnabi), the executors cannot later buy it for the estate.
This prevents circumvention of the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه أيضاً سئل أبو القاسم عمن أوصى إلى رجلين بأن ماله عبداً بكذا درهماً ولأحد الوصيين عبد قيمته أكثر مما سماه الموصي هل للوصي الآخر أن يشتري العبد بما نص الموصي ؟ قال : إن فوض الموصي إلى كل واحد أن ينفرد في ذلك فشراؤه من صاحبه جائز، ولو باع ذلك صاحب العبد من أجنبي وسلمه إليه لم يشتريا جميعاً للميت. وفي الخانية فهذا أصوب
And in it also: Abu al-Qasim was asked about a man who appointed two men as executors regarding his property: a slave for such-and-such dirhams, and one of the executors has a slave whose value is more than what the testator specified. Is it permissible for the other executor to buy the slave for the price specified by the testator? He said: If the testator delegated to each one to act alone in that, then his purchase from his co-executor is permissible. And if the owner of the slave sold it to a stranger and handed it over to him, they do not both buy for the deceased. And in al-Khaniyya, this is more correct.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This is a ruling on a specific scenario: two executors, one owns a slave worth more than the testator's specified price. The question is whether the other executor can buy that slave at the testator's price. The answer: if the testator authorized each to act alone, the purchase is valid. However, if the slave-owner sells to a stranger, then the executors cannot buy it back for the deceased later. This highlights the importance of delegation and the limits of executor authority.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwīḍ): Delegation of authority to act independently.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Never

Key Takeaway
An executor may purchase from a co-executor at the testator's price only if the testator delegated independent authority to each.

The Legal Effect of 'Act on His Opinion' in Wills
Dispute over interpretation of testator's phrase 'act on his opinion'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule distinguishing permissive from restrictive wording to determine whether a named advisor becomes a co-executor.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, 'act on his opinion' is advice, not authority; only 'do not act except on his opinion' makes him a co-executor.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a common scenario in Islamic estate planning: a testator appoints an executor but also names another person whose opinion is to be followed. The question is whether that other person becomes a co-executor or merely an advisor. The Hanafi school, through a careful analogy to agency law, distinguishes between two phrasings with very different legal effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed scenario
A testator says to the appointed executor: 'Act on the opinion of so-and-so.' What legal question arises? Does this make the named person a co-executor or just an advisor?
2
State the opposing views
Some scholars, like Abu al-Layth, hold that both are co-executors in any case. Others, like Abu Bakr (according to the text), say the appointed one is sole executor. Abu Nasr introduces a key distinction.
Abu Nasr's view is considered more akin to the Hanafi school's position.
3
Analyse Abu Nasr's distinction
If the testator says 'act on his opinion' (اعمل برأي فلان), the advisor is not a co-executor. If he says 'do not act except on his opinion' (لا تعمل إلا برأي فلان), the advisor becomes a necessary co-executor.
The phrasing 'except' (إلا) creates a condition.
4
Follow the analogy to agency
The Hanafi school analogizes this to a principal appointing an agent to sell a slave. Saying 'sell with witnesses' allows selling without witnesses; saying 'do not sell except with witnesses' makes witnesses a condition. The same logic applies to the will.
The key is whether the wording is permissive or restrictive.
5
Apply the distinction
Given a new will clause, classify it as permissive or restrictive, then determine who is the executor and whether the executor may act without the other person's consent.
This step tests your understanding of the legal effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه أيضاً سأل أبو بكر عمن أوصى إلى رجل وقال اعمل فيه برأي فلان قال : هو وصي تام وله أن يعمل بغير رأي فلان، وفي قول آخر الثاني هو الوصي التام والأول هو وصي ناقص . قال الفقيه أبو الليث وبعضهم قالوا كلاهما وصيان في الوجهين جميعاً. وقال بعضهم : الأول هو الوصي وبه قال نصير. وقال أبو نصر : إن قال اعمل فيه برأي فلان فهو خاصة، وإن قال لا تعمل إلا برأي فلان فهما وصيان وهو أشبه بقول أصحابنا فإنهم قالوا فيمن وكل آخر ببيع عبده وقال بالشهود فباعه الوكيل بغير شهود جاز، وكذلك لو قال بعه بمحضر فلان فباعه بغير محضر فلان يجوز ولو قال لا تبع إلا بالشهود أو قال لا تبع إلا بمحضر من فلان فباع بغير شهود أو بغير محضر فلان لا يجوز، وعلى هذا إذا قال الموصي بعلم فلان أو قال إلا بعلم فلان
And in it also: Abu Bakr asked about a man who appointed a man as executor and said: 'Act in it according to the opinion of so-and-so.' He said: 'He is a complete executor and he may act without the opinion of so-and-so.' In another opinion, the second is the complete executor and the first is a deficient executor. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: Some of them said both are executors in both aspects. Some said the first is the executor, and Nasir held that. Abu Nasr said: If he said 'act on the opinion of so-and-so', then he is alone; if he said 'do not act except on the opinion of so-and-so', then they are both executors. This is more akin to the statement of our companions, for they said regarding one who appointed an agent to sell his slave and said 'with witnesses', and the agent sold without witnesses, it is permissible. Similarly, if he said 'sell it in the presence of so-and-so' and he sold without his presence, it is permissible. But if he said 'do not sell except with witnesses' or 'do not sell except in the presence of so-and-so', and he sold without witnesses or presence, it is not permissible. And this applies when the testator says 'with the knowledge of so-and-so' or 'except with his knowledge'.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal interpretation of a testator's statement 'act on the opinion of so-and-so' when appointing an executor. There is a dispute: some say the appointed person is the sole executor and may ignore the advisor's opinion; others say the advisor becomes a co-executor. Abu Nasr's distinction is preferred: if the testator says 'act on his opinion', it is merely advice; but if he says 'do not act except on his opinion', then the advisor becomes a necessary co-executor. This is analogized to agency where a restriction using 'except' (لا تبع إلا) imposes a condition.

Key Terms
رأي (ra'y): Opinion or advice of a person named by the testator.
وكيل (wakīl): Agent; used in analogy for executor.
وصي (waṣī): Executor appointed in a will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Nasr's opinion, what wording makes the advisor a co-executor?
A) 'Act on his opinion'
B) 'Do not act except on his opinion'
C) 'Consult him'
D) 'Inform him'

Key Takeaway
The distinction between 'act on his opinion' and 'do not act except on his opinion' determines whether the named person is a co-executor or merely an advisor.

Joint Agreement Required for Executors
Intra-Hanafi dispute over executors' authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require joint agreement of executors and identify the practical consequence of the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
Joint executors must agree; a single executor cannot act without consensus.

When a testator appoints two executors and gives them discretion to distribute one-third of the estate, what happens if they disagree on the recipient? The Hanafi school has an internal difference: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the disagreement nullifies individual authority, requiring either agreement or judicial intervention, while Abu Yusuf permits each to act alone. This lesson unpacks that dispute and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Dispute
First, clarify the case: a testator appoints two executors and tells them to give one-third of his wealth to whomever they wish. The executors then disagree, each proposing a different recipient.
2
Analyze the Two Hanafi Positions
Examine the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: they rule that neither executor has the authority to act alone. Then examine Abu Yusuf's view: he allows each to act independently.
The source does not provide explicit proofs, but the majority view rests on the principle that joint appointment implies joint authority.
3
Identify the Mufta Bihi and Practical Effect
The relied-upon view in the school is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. The practical effect is that if the executors cannot agree, they must either come to a consensus or refer the matter to a judge—neither can unilaterally decide.
This protects the testator's intent and prevents unjust distribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أوصى الرجل إلى رجلين وقال لهما ضعا ثلث مالي حيث شئتما أو قال أعطياه ممن شئتما ثم اختلفا في ذلك فقال أحدهما أعطيه فلاناً وقال الآخر أعطيه فلاناً آخر لم يكن لواحد منهما ذلك عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد خلافاً لأبي يوسف
And if a man appoints two men as executors and says to them: 'Place a third of my wealth wherever you wish' or says: 'Give it to whomever you wish', then they differ on that; one says 'give it to so-and-so' and the other says 'give it to so-and-so else' – neither of them has that authority according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the authority of two executors when they disagree on the recipient of a bequest. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the disagreement nullifies the authority of each individually; they must either agree or refer to the judge. Abu Yusuf, however, holds that each may act according to his own judgment, presumably because the wording 'to whomever you wish' gives each discretion. The majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) emphasizes that joint authority requires consensus.

Key Terms
اختلاف (ikhtilāf): Disagreement between executors that prevents unilateral action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Each may give to his chosen recipient
B) Neither has authority to act alone
C) The judge decides
D) The bequest becomes invalid

Key Takeaway
Joint executors must agree; a single executor cannot act without consensus—the majority Hanafi view prevails.

Separate Executors: Joint or Separate Authority in Hanafi Law
Intra-Hanafi dispute on scope of executor authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the scope of authority when separate executors are appointed, and identify which view is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Appointing separate executors makes them partners over the entire estate unless the testator stipulates otherwise.

When a testator appoints different executors for different portions of his wealth or for different tasks, a question arises: do they become joint executors over everything, or each confined to his assigned matter? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the intra-Hanafi dispute and the preferred position for fatwa. Understanding this dispute clarifies how Hanafi jurists balance the testator's intent with the practical need for unified administration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
Read the first part of the passage: a man appoints one executor for the share of some children and another for the share of others. Also consider variants: executor for a debt vs. executor to free a slave, or executors in different cities.
The key is that separate executors are appointed for distinct matters.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa
According to Abu Hanifa, both executors are joint executors over the entire estate ('فهما يشتركان في الكل' and 'فهما وصيان في كل شيء').
This is the relied-upon view for fatwa.
3
State the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
They hold that each executor is restricted to his assigned matter; the other does not enter with him ('كل واحد منهما وصي على ما له لا يدخل الآخر معه').
This is a legitimate opposing view within the school.
4
Understand the shaykh's refinement
Shaykh Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl explains that if the testator stipulates that each executor should not interfere in the other's domain, that condition is respected by all. If no stipulation, the dispute remains, and the fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's view.
The stipulation overrides the default joint authority.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Consider a testator who appoints one executor to manage his business in Country A and another for his property in Country B, without any condition. According to the mufta bihi view, both executors are joint over all assets.
Practical consequence: they must act together unless the will separates them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية : رجل أوصى بنصيب بعض ولده إلى رجل وبنصيب البعض إلى رجل آخر فهما يشتركان في الكل، ولو أوصى إلى رجل بدين وإلى آخريان يعتق عبده أو ينفذ وصيته فهما وصيان في كل شيء في قول أبي حنيفة. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد كل واحد منهما وصي على ما له لا يدخل الآخر معه، وكذا لو أوصى بميراثه في بلد كذا إلى رجل وبميراثه في بلد كذا إلى رجل وبميراثه في بلد أخرى إلى آخر. وقال الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل : إذا جعل الرجل رجلاً وصياً على ابنه وجعل رجلاً آخر وصياً على ابنه أو جعل أحدهما وصياً في ماله الحاضر وجعل الآخر وصياً في ماله الحاضر وجعل الآخر وصياً في ماله الغائب، فإن كان شرط أن لا يكون كل واحد منهما وصياً فيما أوصى إلى الآخر يكون الأمر على ما شرط عند الكل، وإن لم يكن شرط ذلك فحينئذ تكون المسألة على الاختلاف، والفتوى على قول أبي حنيفة
And in al-Khaniyya: A man appointed one man as executor for the share of some of his children and another man for the share of others; then they both share in the whole. And if he appointed one man as executor for a debt and another to free his slave or execute his will, then they are both executors for everything according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: Each of them is an executor over what he has authority over, the other does not enter with him. Similarly, if he appointed one for his inheritance in one city and another for his inheritance in another city and another for a third city. And the shaykh al-imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl said: If a man appoints one man as executor over his son and another as executor over his son, or makes one executor for his present wealth and another for his present wealth and another for his absent wealth, then if he stipulated that each should not be an executor over what he appointed to the other, then it proceeds according to the stipulation according to all. If he did not stipulate that, then the matter rests on the difference, and the fatwa is on the statement of Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage addresses whether appointing separate executors for different parts of the estate makes them joint executors over everything or each restricted to his assigned part. According to Abu Hanifa, they become joint executors for all matters. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that each is limited to his designated area. The final view of Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl states that if the testator explicitly states that each should not interfere in the other's domain, then that condition is respected; otherwise, Abu Hanifa's view (joint authority) applies, and this is the preferred position for fatwa.

Key Terms
اشتراك (ishtirāk): Joint authority of executors over the whole estate.
وصي (waṣī): Executor of a will; a person appointed to carry out the testator's wishes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view for fatwa in this passage, what is the ruling when separate executors are appointed for different matters without a stipulation?
A) Each executor is restricted to his appointed matter
B) They become joint executors over everything
C) The bequest is invalid
D) The judge appoints a single executor

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that separate executors become joint executors over the entire estate unless a condition separates them.

Priority of Guardians: Fathers vs. Executor for Child of Joint Slave-Girl
Ruling on custody and execution for a child born to a joint slave-girl when parents and executors are involved

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse the hierarchical rules of guardianship for a child born to a joint slave-girl, distinguishing between the authority of fathers and mother's executor, and identify the intra-Hanafi difference when one father is present.

Core Idea
Fathers, as natural guardians, supersede the mother's executor in managing the child's property, except in their absence.

This passage from al-Ziyadat presents a complex hierarchy of legal authority over the property of a minor child born to a joint slave-girl. The rulings address who has the right to dispose of and preserve the child's wealth when fathers, mother, and executors are involved. Understanding these rules requires careful parsing of conditional scenarios and awareness of an intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the role of a single present father.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
The passage begins by stating that when both fathers are alive and present, they have full authority over the child's property, not the mother's executor.
This is the baseline: parents have priority.
2
Define the mother's executor's limited role
When both fathers are absent, the mother's executor gains only authority of preservation (wilayat al-hifz), not full disposal, and only for property the child inherited from the mother or owned before her death.
The executor cannot sell property that is not perishable; only sell what decays quickly.
3
Analyze the one-father-present scenario
Here, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the mother's executor still has a role (preservation) as in the case of both absent, while Abu Yusuf grants the present father exclusive authority.
This is the ikhtilaf: does the single father have full or shared authority?
4
Trace the hierarchy after deaths and executors
When fathers die, their executors and then grandfathers take priority, with the last deceased's executor having precedence over earlier ones.
The order: present father > executor of last deceased father > executor of earlier deceased father > grandfathers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الزيادات : جارية بين رجلين جاءت بولد فادعياه جميعاً حتى ثبت النسب منهما وصارت الجارية أم ولد لهما على ما عرف ثم إنهما أعتقا الجارية واكتسب اكتساباً ثم ماتت وأوصت إلى رجل ولم تدع وارثاً غير ابنها هذا وهو صغير لم يبلغ كان ولاية التصرف في مال الولد وحفظه للولدين لا لوصي الأم، فإن غاب الوالدان تظهر ولاية وصي الأم فتثبت له ولاية الحفظ ولكن إنما تثبت الولاية فيما ورث الصغير من الإمام وفيما كان للصغير قبل موت الأم لا فيما ورث الصغير بعد ذلك، وكما ثبت له ولاية الحفظ ثبت له ولاية كل تصرف هو من باب الحفظ كبيع المنقول وبيع ما يتسارع إليه الفساد وإن غاب أحد الوالدين والآخر حاضر فكذلك الجواب عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وعند أبي يوسف أحد الأبوين ينفرد بالتصرف في مال الصغير فولاية التصرف في مال الصغير وحفظه للوالد دون وصي الأم. ولو مات أحد الأبوين بعد موت الأم ولم يدع وارثاً غير هذا الصغير وأوصى إلى رجل والوالد الآخر حاضر فالميراث كله للصغير وولاية التصرف في التركتين للأب الثاني لا لوصي. وإن كان الوالد الثاني غائباً فلوصي الأم حفظ ما تركت الأم فيما كان من باب الحفظ. وإن مات الوارث الثاني بعد ذلك وأوصى إلى رجل فوصيه يكون أولى من وصي الأب الذي مات قبله وأولى من وصي الأم، فإن كان للأب الذي مات أولاً أب وهو جد هذا الغلام وباقي المسألة بحالها فوصي الأب الذي مات آخراً أولى بالتصرف في مال الصغير، وكذلك لو كان الأب الذي مات آخراً أباً وهو جد الغلام كانت وصيته أولى من أبيه . وإن مات ووصي الأب الذي مات آخراً ولم يوص إلى أحد ومات الأب الذي مات آخراً ولم يوص إلى أحد وقد ترك الأب الذي مات أولاً أبا جد هذا الغلام ووصياً فإن وصي الأب الذي مات أولاً أولى من وصيه، فإن كان مات الوالدان أحدهما قبل الآخر ولكل واحد منهما أب وأوصى كل واحد إلى رجل إن عرف الذي مات أولاً من الذي مات آخراً فولاية التصرف في المال لوصي الذي مات آخراً، وإن مات هذا الموصي ولم يوص إلى أحد ومات الأب الذي عرف موته آخراً ولم يوص إلى أحد وباقي المسألة بحالها فولاية التصرف في المال للجدين لا ينفرد أحدهما به
And in al-Ziyadat: A slave-girl belonging to two men gave birth to a child, and both claimed him until lineage was established from both, and the slave-girl became umm walad for both, as known. Then they both freed the slave-girl, and she earned some earnings, then she died and appointed a man as executor, and she left no heir other than this son of hers, who is a minor not yet mature. The authority to dispose of the child's wealth and its preservation belongs to the two fathers (the owners), not to the mother's executor. If the two fathers are absent, then the authority of the mother's executor appears and he has authority of preservation, but only for what the minor inherited from the mother and what the minor owned before the mother's death, not for what the minor inherits afterwards. And just as he has authority of preservation, he has authority over any disposition that falls under preservation, such as selling movable property and selling that which is subject to rapid decay. And if one of the fathers is absent and the other is present, then the same answer according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but according to Abu Yusuf, one of the fathers alone has authority to dispose of the minor's wealth; thus the authority to dispose and preserve is for the father, not the mother's executor. And if one of the fathers dies after the mother's death and leaves no heir other than this minor and appoints an executor, and the other father is present, then the entire inheritance is for the minor and the authority to dispose of the two estates (from the mother and the deceased father) is for the second father, not the executor. If the second father is absent, then the mother's executor has preservation of what the mother left, in matters of preservation. And if the second heir (the second father) then dies and appoints an executor, then his executor has priority over the executor of the father who died earlier and over the mother's executor. If the first father to die had a father (the minor's grandfather) and the rest of the case remains the same, then the executor of the father who died last has priority in disposing of the minor's wealth. Similarly, if the father who died last is a father (grandfather of the boy), his executor has priority over his (the other) father. And if the executor of the father who died last dies without appointing an executor, and the father who died last also died without appointing an executor, and the first father to die left a father (the minor's grandfather) and an executor, then the executor of the first father has priority over his (the grandfather's) executor. If one of the fathers dies before the other and each has a father and each appointed an executor, if it is known which died first and which died last, then the authority to dispose of the wealth is for the executor of the one who died last. And if this testator (the last to die) dies without appointing an executor, and the father known to have died last dies without appointing an executor, and the rest of the case remains the same, then the authority to dispose of the wealth is for the two grandfathers, and one of them does not have exclusive authority.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This is a complex ruling from al-Ziyadat involving a joint slave-girl, her child, and multiple executors. The key principle is that the biological father(s) have priority over the mother's executor in managing the child's property. When a parent is present, the parent's authority supersedes the executor's. The passage systematically outlines various scenarios: presence/absence of fathers, death of one or both, appointment of executors by the deceased, and interactions with grandfathers. The overall rule is that the nearest male guardian (father, then his executor, then grandfather) has priority over more distant ones, with the mother's executor only having limited authority in the absence of both parents.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who has borne a child to her master and thus becomes free upon his death.
ولاية الحفظ (wilāyat al-ḥifẓ): Authority of preservation over a minor's property, as opposed to full disposal.
ولاية التصرف (wilāyat al-taṣarruf): Authority to dispose of property, including sale and management.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, when one father is present and the other absent, who has authority over the child's property?
A) The present father exclusively
B) The mother's executor exclusively
C) The present father, but the mother's executor still has preservation authority
D) The judge

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the biological fathers have primary authority over their child's property, and the mother's executor steps in only when both fathers are absent, and even then only for preservation.

Exceptions Where One Executor May Act Alone
Listing exceptions with rationale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the exceptions where one executor may act alone and explain the rationale based on the distinction between wilaya and i'ana.

Core Idea
One executor may act alone for urgent needs and assistance, not only guardianship.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq outlines specific cases where a single executor can act without the consent of co-executors. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for proper estate administration, ensuring that urgent matters and simple assistance tasks are not delayed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Normally, co-executors must act jointly. Recognize this default before learning exceptions.
2
List the exceptions from the text
Read the passage and enumerate the six exceptions: funeral preparation, minor's needs, returning specific deposit, paying debt, returning usurped property, returning goods from void sale, plus preserving wealth.
3
Understand the rationale
These actions are matters of assistance (i'ana) rather than guardianship (wilaya). Assistance does not require joint authority.
4
Apply the test
For a new scenario, ask: Is this action a matter of simple assistance or does it involve discretionary guardianship? Only assistance allows unilateral action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (إلا في التجهيز وشراء الكفن لأن في التأخير فساد الميت ولهذا يملكه الجيران أيضاً في الحضر والرفقة في السفر. قال رحمه الله : ( وحاجة الصغار والاتهاب لهم لأنه يخاف هلاكهم من الجوع والعري وانفراد أحدهما بذلك خير ولهذا يملكه كل من هو في يده . قال رحمه الله : (ورد) وديعة عين وقضاء دين لأنه ليس هو من باب الولاية وإنما هو من باب الإعانة ألا ترى أن صاحب الحق يملكه إذا ظفر به بخلاف اقتضاء دین الميت لأنه رضي بأمانتهما جميعاً في القبض، ولأن فيه معنى المبادلة وعند اختلاف الجنس حقيقة المبادلة ورد المغصوب ورد المبيع في البيع هذا القبيل، وكذا حفظ المال فلذلك ينفرد به أحدهما دون صاحبه وما استثناه القدوري في الفاسد من
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'Except in preparing the body and buying the shroud, because delay causes harm to the deceased; therefore, even neighbors own that in the town and travel companions in travel.' He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'And the need of minors and giving gifts to them, because it is feared they will perish from hunger and nakedness, and one of them acting alone is better; therefore, everyone who is in charge of them owns that.' He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'And returning a specific deposit and paying a debt, because it is not from the category of guardianship but from the category of assistance; don't you see that the owner of the right owns it if he obtains it, unlike collecting a debt of the deceased, because he consented to both of their trustworthiness in collection. And because it contains the meaning of exchange, and when the type differs, the reality of exchange, and returning usurped property and returning goods in a void sale are of this nature. Likewise, preserving wealth; therefore, one of them acts alone without his companion. And what al-Quduri excepted in the corrupt from...'
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage lists exceptions to the general rule that co-executors must act jointly. The exceptions are: (1) funeral arrangements and buying a shroud, because delay harms the deceased; (2) urgent needs of minors (food, clothing), because delay could cause their destruction; (3) returning a specific deposit; (4) paying a debt (but not collecting a debt of the deceased, because the debtor trusted both executors); (5) returning usurped property; (6) returning goods sold in a void sale. Also, preserving wealth is included. The reasoning is that these actions are matters of assistance (i'ana) rather than guardianship (wilaya), so a single executor can act.

Key Terms
تجهيز (tajhīz): Preparing the deceased for burial (washing, shrouding, etc.).
إعانة (i'āna): Assistance; a category of action that does not require joint guardianship.
ولاية (wilāya): Guardianship; authoritative control requiring joint execution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following actions is explicitly mentioned as an exception where one executor may act alone?
A) Collecting a debt owed to the deceased
B) Selling property of the estate
C) Returning a specific deposit
D) Hiring a lawyer for litigation

Key Takeaway
One executor may act alone in cases of necessity or assistance (i'ana), such as funeral preparation, minor's needs, returning deposits, and paying debts.
