Authority over Orphan's Property: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Mother's Executor
Hypothetical scenario illustrating who has authority over a minor's property after the mother's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi positions on guardianship of a minor's property when the mother dies and appoints an executor, and distinguish between the views of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
The father's authority over the minor's property overrides the mother's executor, but the exact scope varies between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a detailed hypothetical case involving a female slave, her owners, her child, and various deaths and executors. The core question is: who manages the minor's property when the mother dies and appoints a waṣī? The Hanafi imams differ on the extent of the father's exclusive authority versus the executor's limited preservation rights. This lesson walks you through the layers of priority.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
A female slave between two men gives birth; both claim paternity, so she becomes umm walad for both. She is freed, earns, dies, and appoints an executor. She leaves only a minor son.
The child's lineage is established to both fathers, so they are his legal fathers.
2
Identify the primary rule
The two fathers have exclusive authority over the child's property and its preservation. The mother's executor has no authority.
This is the default position when both fathers are alive and present.
3
Note the exception for absent fathers
If both fathers are absent, the mother's executor gains limited authority for preservation only (e.g., selling movable goods). This authority does not extend to all types of disposal.
The executor cannot make full disposition, only acts of preservation.
4
Pinpoint the intra-madhhab dispute
When one father is present and the other absent, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the present father has exclusive authority. Abu Yusuf holds that even if both are present, one father can act alone.
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the present father must act alone; under Abu Yusuf, either father may act.
5
Extend through multiple deaths
The passage layers more deaths and executors. Priority goes to the surviving parent, then to the executor of the last-dying parent, then to grandfathers jointly.
The hierarchy: present father > absent father's executor? The passage specifies: if one father dies first, the other father takes precedence over the first father's executor. If both die, the executor of the last-dying father is preferred. If no executor, grandfathers act jointly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الزيادات : جارية بين رجلين جاءت بولد فادعياه جميعاً حتى ثبت النسب منهما وصارت الجارية أم ولد لهما على ما عرف ثم إنهما أعتقا الجارية واكتسب اكتساباً ثم ماتت وأوصت إلى رجل ولم تدع وارثاً غير ابنها هذا وهو صغير لم يبلغ كان ولاية التصرف في مال الولد وحفظه للولدين لا لوصي الأم، فإن غاب الوالدان تظهر ولاية وصي الأم فتثبت له ولاية الحفظ ولكن إنما تثبت الولاية فيما ورث الصغير من الإمام وفيما كان للصغير قبل موت الأم لا فيما ورث الصغير بعد ذلك، وكما ثبت له ولاية الحفظ ثبت له ولاية كل تصرف هو من باب الحفظ كبيع المنقول وبيع ما يتسارع إليه الفساد وإن غاب أحد الوالدين والآخر حاضر فكذلك الجواب عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وعند أبي يوسف أحد الأبوين ينفرد بالتصرف في مال الصغير فولاية التصرف في مال الصغير وحفظه للوالد دون وصي الأم. ولو مات أحد الأبوين بعد موت الأم ولم يدع وارثاً غير هذا الصغير وأوصى إلى رجل والوالد الآخر حاضر فالميراث كله للصغير وولاية التصرف في التركتين للأب الثاني لا لوصي. وإن كان الوالد الثاني غائباً فلوصي الأم حفظ ما تركت الأم فيما كان من باب الحفظ. وإن مات الوارث الثاني بعد ذلك وأوصى إلى رجل فوصيه يكون أولى من وصي الأب الذي مات قبله وأولى من وصي الأم، فإن كان للأب الذي مات أولاً أب وهو جد هذا الغلام وباقي المسألة بحالها فوصي الأب الذي مات آخراً أولى بالتصرف في مال الصغير، وكذلك لو كان الأب الذي مات آخراً أباً وهو جد الغلام كانت وصيته أولى من أبيه . وإن مات ووصي الأب الذي مات آخراً ولم يوص إلى أحد ومات الأب الذي مات آخراً ولم يوص إلى أحد وقد ترك الأب الذي مات أولاً أبا جد هذا الغلام ووصياً فإن وصي الأب الذي مات أولاً أولى من وصيه، فإن كان مات الوالدان أحدهما قبل الآخر ولكل واحد منهما أب وأوصى كل واحد إلى رجل إن عرف الذي مات أولاً من الذي مات آخراً فولاية التصرف في المال لوصي الذي مات آخراً، وإن مات هذا الموصي ولم يوص إلى أحد ومات الأب الذي عرف موته آخراً ولم يوص إلى أحد وباقي المسألة بحالها فولاية التصرف في المال للجدين لا ينفرد أحدهما به
Additional comments: A female slave between two men gave birth to a child, and both claimed paternity until lineage was established from both, and the female slave became umm walad for both as known. Then both manumitted the female slave, and she earned earnings. Then she died and appointed a man as executor, and she left no heir except this son of hers, who is a minor who has not reached puberty. The authority to dispose of the child's property and its preservation belongs to the two fathers, not to the mother's executor. If both fathers are absent, the authority of the mother's executor appears, so he is given the authority of preservation, but this authority is established only regarding what the child inherited from the mother and what the child had before the mother's death, not regarding what the child inherits after that. And just as he is given the authority of preservation, he is given the authority of every disposal that is from the category of preservation, like selling movable goods and selling what quickly deteriorates. If one of the two fathers is absent and the other is present, the answer is the same according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but according to Abu Yusuf, one of the two fathers is exclusively entitled to dispose of the minor's property, so the authority to dispose of the minor's property and its preservation is for the father, not the mother's executor. If one of the fathers dies after the mother's death and leaves no heir other than this minor, and appoints a man as executor, and the other father is present, then all inheritance is for the minor, and the authority to dispose of the two estates is for the second father, not for the executor. If the second father is absent, then the mother's executor has preservation of what the mother left in matters of preservation. If the second heir dies after that and appoints a man as executor, then his executor has more right than the executor of the father who died before and more right than the mother's executor. If the father who died first has a father, who is the grandfather of this boy, and the rest of the case remains the same, then the executor of the father who died last has more right to dispose of the minor's property. Similarly, if the father who died last was a father and he is the grandfather of the boy, his executorship has more right than his father. If the executor of the father who died last dies without appointing an executor, and the father who died last dies without appointing an executor, and the first father left a father, the grandfather of this boy, and an executor, then the executor of the first father has more right than his executor. If the two fathers died, one before the other, and each of them has a father, and each appointed a man as executor, and it is known which died first and which died last, then the authority to dispose of the property is for the executor of the one who died last. If this testator died without appointing an executor, and the father known to have died last died without appointing an executor, and the rest of the case remains the same, then the authority to dispose of the property is for the two grandfathers, and neither exclusively takes it.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is an intra-madhhab dispute from the commentary of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It presents a detailed hypothetical scenario regarding a female slave (jariyah) jointly owned by two men, who becomes umm walad for both after both claim paternity of her child. After she is manumitted, earns, and dies, she appoints an executor (wasi). The question is: who has wilayah (guardianship/authority) over the property of her minor son? The passage first states the general rule: the two fathers (the men who were her owners) have the authority over the child's property, not the mother's executor. If both fathers are absent, the mother's executor gains a limited authority, but only for preservation and actions akin to preservation (e.g., selling movable or perishable goods). However, if one father is present, the ruling differs. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), the present father has exclusive authority; according to Abu Yusuf, even if both are present, one father can act alone. The passage then extends the scenario through multiple deaths and appointments of executors, establishing a hierarchy of priority: the surviving parent takes precedence; if both parents die, the executor of the last-dying parent has priority; if no executor, the grandfathers act jointly. The commentary follows the order of the hypothetical, building each layer of heirs and executors to demonstrate the rules of precedence in guardianship. It emphasizes that the authority of the mother's executor is limited to preservation and cannot extend to full disposition. The dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf is clearly delineated, though the passage does not explicitly state the mufta bihi (operative position).

Key Terms
ولاية (wilāyah): Legal guardianship or authority to manage the property of a minor.
وصي (waṣī): An executor appointed by a person to manage their affairs after death, here specifically for the minor child.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, thereby gaining certain rights including manumission upon her master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if one of the two fathers of a minor is present after the mother's death, who has authority to dispose of the minor's property?
A) The mother's executor
B) The present father
C) Both fathers jointly
D) The grandfather

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the father's guardianship over a minor's property takes precedence over the mother's executor; when one father is present, he has exclusive authority, but Abu Yusuf allows either present father to act alone.

The Core Exceptions for a Single Executor's Independent Action
Listing the first set of exceptions for a single executor's independent action, with rationales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven core exceptions where a single executor may act alone and explain the rationale for each, distinguishing between acts of guardianship and acts of assistance.

Core Idea
Necessity and assistance, not guardianship, allow a single executor to act independently.

When two executors are appointed, the default rule is that they must act jointly in managing the estate. However, the Hanafi school recognizes several exceptions where urgency or the nature of the act permits one executor to proceed without the other. This lesson focuses on the core exceptions derived from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, which are universally accepted in the school. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for effectively administering an estate without unnecessary delays or disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Start by recognizing that the default position is joint action: two executors must agree on decisions regarding the estate. The exceptions are limited and precisely defined.
The general rule is based on the testator's intent to have both executors oversee the estate collectively.
2
List the Core Exceptions
From the matn, enumerate the following: preparing the deceased (tajhīz), purchasing the shroud (kafan), providing food and clothing for minor children, returning a specific trust (wadī‘a), paying a debt (dayn), executing a specific bequest (waṣiyya mu‘ayyana), and manumitting a specific slave (‘itq ‘abd mu‘ayyan). These are the first seven exceptions.
The phrase 'specific' (mu‘ayyan) is key: the testator has already designated the item or slave, so no deliberation is needed.
3
Understand the Rationale for Each
For burial matters, delay causes physical decay of the body—thus urgency outweighs the need for joint consultation. For minors' needs, delay risks hunger and cold. For returning a trust or paying a debt, the act is one of assistance (i‘āna) rather than guardianship; the right-holder can even take it himself. For executing a specific bequest or manumitting a specific slave, no opinion or judgment is required—the testator's instructions are clear.
The common thread: either necessity (ḍarūra) or the absence of need for independent judgment (ra’y).
4
Differentiate Guardianship vs. Assistance
Acts of guardianship (wilāya) require joint authority; acts of assistance (i‘āna) do not. Returning a trust is assistance—the owner can reclaim it directly. In contrast, collecting a debt from a third party involves an element of exchange (mubādala) and thus requires joint action.
This distinction explains why returning a trust is an exception but collecting a debt is not.
5
Memorize the Seven Items
Use a mnemonic: Bury, Shroud, Feed, Return, Pay, Execute, Free. Each item is rooted in either urgency or specificity.
These seven are the foundation; later scholars added more, but these are the most frequently cited.

Source Text

قال رحمه الله : (إلا في التجهيز وشراء الكفن لأن في التأخير فساد الميت ولهذا يملكه الجيران أيضاً في الحضر والرفقة في السفر. قال رحمه الله : ( وحاجة الصغار والاتهاب لهم لأنه يخاف هلاكهم من الجوع والعري وانفراد أحدهما بذلك خير ولهذا يملكه كل من هو في يده . قال رحمه الله : (ورد) وديعة عين وقضاء دين لأنه ليس هو من باب الولاية وإنما هو من باب الإعانة ألا ترى أن صاحب الحق يملكه إذا ظفر به بخلاف اقتضاء دین الميت لأنه رضي بأمانتهما جميعاً في القبض، ولأن فيه معنى المبادلة وعند اختلاف الجنس حقيقة المبادلة ورد المغصوب ورد المبيع في البيع هذا القبيل، وكذا حفظ المال فلذلك ينفرد به أحدهما دون صاحبه وما استثناه القدوري في الفاسد من كتاب الوصايا/ باب الوصي وما يملكه والخصومة في حق الميت ووصي الوصي وصي التركتين وتصح قسمته عن الورثة مع مختصره بقوله إلا في شراء الكفن للميت وتجهيزه وطعام الصغار وكسوتهم ورد وديعة بعينها وقضاء دين وتنفيذ وصية بعينها وعتق عبد بعينه والخصومة في حقوق الميت اهـ. وهذه تسعة أشياء كما ترى قصر القدوري الاستثناء عليها في مختصره، وأقتفى أثره صاحب الهداية وزاد فيها على ذلك أشياء بقوله : ورد المغصوب والمشتري شراء فاسداً وحفظ الأموال وقبول الهبة وبيع ما يخشى عليه التوى والتلف وجمع الأموال الضائعة وهذه التي زادها في الهداية على ما في الكتاب ستة أشياء فيصير مجموع الأشياء المعدودة خمسة عشر .اهـ. قال رحمه الله : (وتنفيذ وصية معينة وعتق عبد معين لأنه لا يحتاج فيه إلى رأي .
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'Except in preparing the deceased [for burial] and purchasing the shroud, because delay causes decay of the deceased, and therefore even the neighbors in the town and companions in travel may do that.' He said: 'And the need of minors and accepting gifts for them, because it is feared they will perish from hunger and nakedness, and one of them acting alone in that is better, and therefore everyone who has it in his hand may do that.' He said: 'And returning a specific trust and paying a debt, because it is not from the category of guardianship but from the category of assistance; do you not see that the right-holder may take possession if he finds it, unlike collecting the debt of the deceased because he consented to both their trustworthiness in receiving payment, and because there is an element of exchange, and when the types differ it is a genuine exchange, and returning a usurped item and returning an item sold in a null sale is of this type, and likewise preserving wealth. Therefore one of them may act alone without the other.' And what al-Quduri excepted in his abridgment from the book of wills, chapter of the executor and what he owns: 'And litigation regarding the rights of the deceased, and the executor of an executor is executor of both estates, and his division is valid among the heirs.' With his abridgment he said: 'Except in purchasing the shroud for the deceased and preparing him, and food for minors and their clothing, and returning a specific trust, and paying a debt, and executing a specific bequest, and manumitting a specific slave, and litigation concerning the rights of the deceased.' End quote. These are nine things, as you see, al-Quduri restricted the exceptions to them in his abridgment. The author of al-Hidayah followed him and added to that other things with his saying: 'And returning a usurped item, and an item purchased in a void sale, and preserving wealth, and accepting a gift, and selling what is feared will go to waste or be lost, and collecting lost properties.' These are six things that al-Hidayah added to what is in the book. So the total number of counted items becomes fifteen. End quote. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'And executing a specific bequest and manumitting a specific slave, because they do not require opinion.'
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement enumerating the exceptions where a single executor (waṣī) may act without the consent of a co-executor. The matn (from Kanz al-Daqa'iq) lists the initial exceptions: preparing the deceased, purchasing the shroud, providing for minor children's needs, returning a specific trust, paying a debt, executing a specific bequest, and manumitting a specific slave. The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) then discusses the earlier compilation by al-Quduri, who limited the exceptions to nine items in his Mukhtasar. Al-Marghinani, the author of al-Hidayah, followed al-Quduri but added six more items, bringing the total to fifteen. The additional items include: returning a usurped item, handling a void purchase, preserving wealth, accepting a gift, selling items at risk of perishing, and collecting lost properties. The passage explains the rationale for each exception: sometimes it is necessity (like the shroud to prevent decay), sometimes it is an act of assistance rather than guardianship (like returning a trust), and sometimes it involves no need for independent judgment (like executing a specific bequest already specified by the testator). The list is intended for memorization, as it specifies the limits of a co-executor's authority.

Key Terms
تجهيز (tajhīz): Preparing the deceased for burial, including washing, shrouding, etc.
كفن (kafan): The shroud used to wrap the deceased.
استثناء (istithnā'): Exception; the matters where the general rule (that two executors must act jointly) does not apply.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a reason for allowing a single executor to act alone, according to the passage?
A) Urgency due to potential harm (e.g., decay of the deceased)
B) The act is one of assistance rather than guardianship
C) The co-executor has explicitly delegated authority
D) The act does not require independent judgment

Key Takeaway
A single executor may act alone only in matters where delay would cause harm, or where the act is merely assisting a right-holder or following a specific instruction of the testator.

The Expanded List: Fifteen Exceptions in the Hanafi School
Expanding the list of exceptions from nine to fifteen, with historical transmission from al-Quduri and al-Hidayah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list all fifteen matters where a single executor may act alone, and differentiate between al-Quduri's nine and al-Hidayah's expanded fifteen.

Core Idea
Al-Marghinani expanded the exceptions to fifteen, covering all urgent and assistive acts a single executor may undertake.

While the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq provides a foundational list of exceptions, later Hanafi authorities like al-Quduri and al-Marghinani refined and expanded the list. Al-Quduri enumerated nine exceptions in his Mukhtasar, and al-Marghinani added six more in al-Hidayah, bringing the total to fifteen. Understanding the full list is essential for comprehensive estate administration.

Guided Path
1
Recall the Core Seven from the Matn
Begin with the seven items mentioned in the first lesson: preparing the deceased, shroud, minors' needs, returning trust, paying debt, specific bequest, specific manumission. These form the backbone.
These seven are included in both al-Quduri's and al-Hidayah's lists.
2
Learn al-Quduri's Complete Nine
Al-Quduri in his Mukhtasar lists nine: the above seven plus litigation regarding the deceased's rights (khuṣūma fī ḥuqūq al-mayyit) and the executor of an executor being executor of both estates? Actually the text says: 'And litigation regarding the rights of the deceased, and the executor of an executor is executor of both estates, and his division is valid among the heirs.' But these might be separate? The passage says al-Quduri's abridgment includes these nine. We need to extract them: the text says 'إلا في شراء الكفن للميت وتجهيزه وطعام الصغار وكسوتهم ورد وديعة بعينها وقضاء دين وتنفيذ وصية بعينها وعتق عبد بعينه والخصومة في حقوق الميت' That's eight? Actually count: 1) شراء الكفن, 2) تجهيز, 3) طعام الصغار, 4) كسوتهم, 5) رد وديعة, 6) قضاء دين, 7) تنفيذ وصية, 8) عتق عبد, 9) الخصومة. Yes, nine. So add litigation to the seven. Note that 'executor of an executor' and 'division' are separate statements not part of the nine exceptions? The passage says: 'وما استثناه القدوري في الفاسد من كتاب الوصايا/ باب الوصي وما يملكه والخصومة في حق الميت ووصي الوصي وصي التركتين وتصح قسمته عن الورثة مع مختصره بقوله إلا في...' This could mean al-Quduri's exception list includes the nine items, and then separate rulings. For simplicity, we focus on the nine.
Al-Quduri's list is still relied upon, but it is superseded by the more comprehensive Hidayah list.
3
Identify al-Hidayah's Six Additions
Al-Marghinani added: returning a usurped item (radd al-maghṣūb), handling an item purchased in a void sale (al-mushtarā sharā’ fāsid), preserving wealth (ḥifẓ al-amwāl), accepting a gift (qabūl al-hiba), selling perishable items (bay‘ mā yukhshā ‘alayhi al-talaf), and collecting lost properties (jam‘ al-amwāl al-ḍā’i‘a).
These additions cover situations where property is at risk or where the act is purely preservative.
4
Understand Rationale for Each Addition
Returning usurped property and void-purchased items are like returning a trust—they restore rights without need for judgment. Preserving wealth and collecting lost property are urgent to prevent loss. Accepting gifts and selling perishable items are also driven by urgency: gifts may be time-sensitive, and perishable goods must be sold before they spoil.
All additions align with the core principles of necessity or assistance.
5
Memorize the Full Fifteen
Combine the nine from al-Quduri (which include the seven core plus litigation and possibly others? Actually to avoid confusion, we list the fifteen as: 1. Preparing deceased, 2. Purchasing shroud, 3. Food for minors, 4. Clothing for minors, 5. Returning specific trust, 6. Paying debt, 7. Executing specific bequest, 8. Manumitting specific slave, 9. Litigation re deceased’s rights, 10. Returning usurped item, 11. Handling void purchase, 12. Preserving wealth, 13. Accepting gift, 14. Selling perishable item, 15. Collecting lost property. Use a mnemonic: Bury, Shroud, Feed, Clothe, Return, Pay, Execute, Free, Sue, Usurp, Void, Preserve, Gift, Sell, Collect.
Memorization is key for quick recall in practice.

قال رحمه الله : (إلا في التجهيز وشراء الكفن لأن في التأخير فساد الميت ولهذا يملكه الجيران أيضاً في الحضر والرفقة في السفر. قال رحمه الله : ( وحاجة الصغار والاتهاب لهم لأنه يخاف هلاكهم من الجوع والعري وانفراد أحدهما بذلك خير ولهذا يملكه كل من هو في يده . قال رحمه الله : (ورد) وديعة عين وقضاء دين لأنه ليس هو من باب الولاية وإنما هو من باب الإعانة ألا ترى أن صاحب الحق يملكه إذا ظفر به بخلاف اقتضاء دین الميت لأنه رضي بأمانتهما جميعاً في القبض، ولأن فيه معنى المبادلة وعند اختلاف الجنس حقيقة المبادلة ورد المغصوب ورد المبيع في البيع هذا القبيل، وكذا حفظ المال فلذلك ينفرد به أحدهما دون صاحبه وما استثناه القدوري في الفاسد من كتاب الوصايا/ باب الوصي وما يملكه والخصومة في حق الميت ووصي الوصي وصي التركتين وتصح قسمته عن الورثة مع مختصره بقوله إلا في شراء الكفن للميت وتجهيزه وطعام الصغار وكسوتهم ورد وديعة بعينها وقضاء دين وتنفيذ وصية بعينها وعتق عبد بعينه والخصومة في حقوق الميت اهـ. وهذه تسعة أشياء كما ترى قصر القدوري الاستثناء عليها في مختصره، وأقتفى أثره صاحب الهداية وزاد فيها على ذلك أشياء بقوله : ورد المغصوب والمشتري شراء فاسداً وحفظ الأموال وقبول الهبة وبيع ما يخشى عليه التوى والتلف وجمع الأموال الضائعة وهذه التي زادها في الهداية على ما في الكتاب ستة أشياء فيصير مجموع الأشياء المعدودة خمسة عشر .اهـ. قال رحمه الله : (وتنفيذ وصية معينة وعتق عبد معين لأنه لا يحتاج فيه إلى رأي .
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'Except in preparing the deceased [for burial] and purchasing the shroud, because delay causes decay of the deceased, and therefore even the neighbors in the town and companions in travel may do that.' He said: 'And the need of minors and accepting gifts for them, because it is feared they will perish from hunger and nakedness, and one of them acting alone in that is better, and therefore everyone who has it in his hand may do that.' Therefore one of them may act alone without the other.' End quote. These are nine things, as you see, al-Quduri restricted the exceptions to them in his abridgment. Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement enumerating the exceptions where a single executor (waṣī) may act without the consent of a co-executor. The matn (from Kanz al-Daqa'iq) lists the initial exceptions: preparing the deceased, purchasing the shroud, providing for minor children's needs, returning a specific trust, paying a debt, executing a specific bequest, and manumitting a specific slave. The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) then discusses the earlier compilation by al-Quduri, who limited the exceptions to nine items in his Mukhtasar. Al-Marghinani, the author of al-Hidayah, followed al-Quduri but added six more items, bringing the total to fifteen. The additional items include: returning a usurped item, handling a void purchase, preserving wealth, accepting a gift, selling items at risk of perishing, and collecting lost properties. The passage explains the rationale for each exception: sometimes it is necessity (like the shroud to prevent decay), sometimes it is an act of assistance rather than guardianship (like returning a trust), and sometimes it involves no need for independent judgment (like executing a specific bequest already specified by the testator). The list is intended for memorization, as it specifies the limits of a co-executor's authority.

Key Terms
تجهيز (tajhīz): Preparing the deceased for burial, including washing, shrouding, etc.
كفن (kafan): The shroud used to wrap the deceased.
استثناء (istithnā'): Exception; the matters where the general rule (that two executors must act jointly) does not apply.
الخصومة في حقوق الميت (al-khuṣūma fī ḥuqūq al-mayyit): Litigation concerning the rights of the deceased, e.g., filing a lawsuit to recover property.
رد المغصوب (radd al-maghṣūb): Returning a usurped (wrongfully taken) item to its rightful owner.
المشتري شراء فاسداً (al-mushtarā sharā’ fāsid): An item purchased in a void (fasid) sale, which must be returned to the seller.
حفظ الأموال (ḥifẓ al-amwāl): Preserving and safeguarding the estate's assets from loss or damage.
قبول الهبة (qabūl al-hiba): Accepting a gift offered to the deceased or the estate.
بيع ما يخشى عليه التوى والتلف (bay‘ mā yukhshā ‘alayhi al-talaf): Selling items that are feared to be lost or spoiled if kept.
جمع الأموال الضائعة (jam‘ al-amwāl al-ḍā’i‘a): Collecting and safeguarding lost or scattered property of the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Nine
C) Seven
D) Fifteen

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes fifteen specific matters where a single executor may act without the co-executor's consent, compiled from al-Quduri and expanded by al-Hidayah.

Litigation Alone by an Executor on Behalf of the Deceased
Ruling on executor's independent litigation and replacement upon death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that one executor may litigate alone on behalf of the deceased and explain the intra-Hanafi difference regarding replacement when an executor dies.

Core Idea
One executor can litigate alone because joint action is impracticable.

The matn states that litigation for the deceased is an exception to the general rule requiring joint action by multiple executors. The commentator explains the reasoning and then discusses a disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf about what happens when one executor dies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exception
The general rule is that multiple executors must act together; the exception is litigation because joint action is impracticable.
The reason given is 'li-anna al-ijtima'a fihi muta'adhdhir' (because collective action is impracticable).
2
State the ruling
One executor may litigate alone; the action is valid and binding on the estate.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
When one executor dies, two opinions: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say the remaining cannot act alone, so judge appoints another. Abu Yusuf says the remaining could act alone but to honor testator's intent, judge appoints a replacement.
Note the pronoun: 'عندهما' (according to the two) refers to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; 'عند أبي يوسف' is the opposing view.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon view is that of Abu Hanifa: the judge must appoint a replacement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (والخصومة في حق الميت لأن الاجتماع فيه متعذر ولهذا ينفرد بها أحد الوكيلين أيضاً. ولو مات أحدهما جعل القاضي مكانه وصياً آخر، أما عندهما فظاهر لأن الباقي منهما عاجز عن الانفراد بالتصرف فيضم القاضي إليه وصياً ينظر إلى الميت عند عجز الميت، وأما عند أبي يوسف فلأن الحي منهما وإن كان يقدر على التصرف فالموصي قدر أن يجعل وصيين يتصرفان وذلك ممكن لتحقيق نصب وصي آخر مكان الأول. قال في الهداية وقضاء دين قال في الغاية والمراد بالتقاضي الاقتضاء وكذا كان المراد في عرفهم اهـ. وهذا أن لا يكون الاقتضاء الذي هو القبض معنى التقاضي في الوضع واللغة يوهم بل كان معناه في العرف مع أن الأمر ليس كذلك كما صرح به المصنف في باب الوكالة بالخصومة من كتاب الوكالة حيث قال : الوكيل بالتقاضي يملك القبض على أصل الرواية في معناه وضعاً إلا أن العرف بخلافه وهو قاض على الوضع ا هـ. ويدلك على كون معناه ذلك في الوضع ما ذكر في كتب اللغة. قال في القاموس : تقاضاه الدين قبضه منه . وقال في الأساس: تقاضيته ديني وبديني واقتضيته ديني واقتضيت منه حقي أي أخذته اهـ. ولم يتعرض المصنف لتصرفات الأب ووكيل الأب والجد والقاضي وأمين القاضي في مال الصغير ونحن نذكر ذلك .
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'And litigation concerning the rights of the deceased, because collective action is impracticable, and therefore one of the two agents also acts alone. If one of them dies, the judge appoints another executor in his place. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is obvious because the remaining one is incapable of acting alone in disposal, so the judge appoints an executor with him to look after the deceased's interests due to the deceased's incapacity. According to Abu Yusuf, because the living one, even though he is capable of disposal, the testator had the power to appoint two executors to act, and that is possible to achieve by appointing another executor in place of the first.' He said in al-Hidayah: 'And paying a debt.' He said in al-Ghayah: 'The intended meaning of taqadi is iqtida (demanding payment), and that was the meaning in their custom.' End quote. And this is not that iqtida which is receipt (qabd); the meaning of taqadi in its established usage and language may be misconstrued, but its meaning in custom was that, though the matter is not so, as the author clarified in the chapter of agency for litigation in the Book of Agency where he said: 'The agent for taqadi has the right to receive payment according to the basis of the narration in its established meaning, except that custom is contrary to it, and he is a litigator according to the established meaning.' End quote. And evidence that its meaning is that in the established usage is what is mentioned in books of language. Al-Qamus said: 'He demanded payment from him of the debt: he received it from him.' And al-Asas said: 'I demanded payment of my debt from him, and I took my debt from him and I collected my right from him, meaning I took it.' End quote. And the author did not address the dispositions of the father, the father's agent, the grandfather, the judge, and the judge's trustee over the minor's property; we will mention that.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (talil). The matn states that litigation on behalf of the deceased is an exception where one executor may act alone because joint action is impracticable (muta'adhdhir). The commentator explains that if one executor dies, the judge replaces him. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two), if one executor remains, he is incapable of acting alone, so the judge appoints another; according to Abu Yusuf, the living executor could act alone, but the testator intended two, so the judge appoints a replacement to fulfill the testator's intent. The passage then clarifies the term 'taqadi' (litigation) in al-Hidayah: it actually means 'iqtida' (demanding payment), not necessarily 'qabd' (receiving). The commentator cites al-Ghayah and al-Qamus to show that in the language, taqadi includes receiving. However, custom might restrict it. The author (Ibn Nujaym) notes that he has not yet discussed the powers of the father, grandfather, judge, etc., over the minor's property; he will cover that later. The commentary follows the order of the matn: first the ruling, then the intra-madhhab reasoning, then the linguistic clarification.

Key Terms
خصومة (khuṣūmah): Litigation or legal dispute; here specifically, filing and pursuing claims on behalf of the deceased.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must a judge do when one of two executors dies, based on the mufta bihi view?
A) Allow the remaining executor to act alone.
B) Appoint a new executor to replace the deceased.
C) Declare the will void.
D) Appoint a new executor only if the estate is large.

Key Takeaway
Litigation on behalf of the deceased is an exception where one executor can act alone; if one executor dies, the judge appoints a replacement per the predominant Hanafi view.

The Meaning of Taqadi and the Agent's Authority to Receive Payment
Definition and scope of the term 'taqadi' in executor appointments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain that 'taqadi' in Hanafi legal terminology includes the authority to receive payment based on linguistic evidence, and identify the nuance introduced by custom.

Core Idea
Taqadi linguistically includes receiving payment, but custom may restrict it to litigation.

The passage clarifies a key term from al-Hidayah: 'taqadi' (litigation for debt) is defined as 'iqtida' (demanding payment). The commentator shows, through quotations from al-Qamus and al-Asas, that in the Arabic language, taqadi includes the act of receiving (qabd). However, customary usage may differ, affecting the agent's authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The term 'taqadi' appears in al-Hidayah in the context of an executor's authority to 'pay a debt' (qada' dayn). The commentator clarifies its meaning.
2
Examine the linguistic definition
The passage quotes al-Qamus: 'Taqadahu al-dayn: qabadahu minhu' (He demanded payment of the debt: he received it from him). Al-Asas similarly defines it as taking the debt. This shows that in language, taqadi includes receipt.
Note the verb 'taqada' is given in the past tense, indicating completion of the act including receipt.
3
Distinguish between legal/ customary usage
The commentator notes that although the linguistic meaning includes receipt, custom ('urf) may restrict taqadi to merely filing a lawsuit (khusumah) without the authority to collect. He cites the author's own clarification from the chapter on agency: 'The agent for taqadi has the right to receive payment according to the basic narration, except that custom is otherwise.'
4
Apply the rule
The default ruling: an agent appointed for taqadi has authority to receive payment. However, if local custom restricts the term, the agent's authority is limited accordingly unless specified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (والخصومة في حق الميت لأن الاجتماع فيه متعذر ولهذا ينفرد بها أحد الوكيلين أيضاً. ولو مات أحدهما جعل القاضي مكانه وصياً آخر، أما عندهما فظاهر لأن الباقي منهما عاجز عن الانفراد بالتصرف فيضم القاضي إليه وصياً ينظر إلى الميت عند عجز الميت، وأما عند أبي يوسف فلأن الحي منهما وإن كان يقدر على التصرف فالموصي قدر أن يجعل وصيين يتصرفان وذلك ممكن لتحقيق نصب وصي آخر مكان الأول. قال في الهداية وقضاء دين قال في الغاية والمراد بالتقاضي الاقتضاء وكذا كان المراد في عرفهم اهـ. وهذا أن لا يكون الاقتضاء الذي هو القبض معنى التقاضي في الوضع واللغة يوهم بل كان معناه في العرف مع أن الأمر ليس كذلك كما صرح به المصنف في باب الوكالة بالخصومة من كتاب الوكالة حيث قال : الوكيل بالتقاضي يملك القبض على أصل الرواية في معناه وضعاً إلا أن العرف بخلافه وهو قاض على الوضع ا هـ. ويدلك على كون معناه ذلك في الوضع ما ذكر في كتب اللغة. قال في القاموس : تقاضاه الدين قبضه منه . وقال في الأساس: تقاضيته ديني وبديني واقتضيته ديني واقتضيت منه حقي أي أخذته اهـ. ولم يتعرض المصنف لتصرفات الأب ووكيل الأب والجد والقاضي وأمين القاضي في مال الصغير ونحن نذكر ذلك .
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'And litigation concerning the rights of the deceased, because collective action is impracticable, and therefore one of the two agents also acts alone. If one of them dies, the judge appoints another executor in his place. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is obvious because the remaining one is incapable of acting alone in disposal, so the judge appoints an executor with him to look after the deceased's interests due to the deceased's incapacity. According to Abu Yusuf, because the living one, even though he is capable of disposal, the testator had the power to appoint two executors to act, and that is possible to achieve by appointing another executor in place of the first.' He said in al-Hidayah: 'And paying a debt.' He said in al-Ghayah: 'The intended meaning of taqadi is iqtida (demanding payment), and that was the meaning in their custom.' End quote. And this is not that iqtida which is receipt (qabd); the meaning of taqadi in its established usage and language may be misconstrued, but its meaning in custom was that, though the matter is not so, as the author clarified in the chapter of agency for litigation in the Book of Agency where he said: 'The agent for taqadi has the right to receive payment according to the basis of the narration in its established meaning, except that custom is contrary to it, and he is a litigator according to the established meaning.' End quote. And evidence that its meaning is that in the established usage is what is mentioned in books of language. Al-Qamus said: 'He demanded payment from him of the debt: he received it from him.' And al-Asas said: 'I demanded payment of my debt from him, and I took my debt from him and I collected my right from him, meaning I took it.' End quote. And the author did not address the dispositions of the father, the father's agent, the grandfather, the judge, and the judge's trustee over the minor's property; we will mention that.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (talil). The matn states that litigation on behalf of the deceased is an exception where one executor may act alone because joint action is impracticable (muta'adhdhir). The commentator explains that if one executor dies, the judge replaces him. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two), if one executor remains, he is incapable of acting alone, so the judge appoints another; according to Abu Yusuf, the living executor could act alone, but the testator intended two, so the judge appoints a replacement to fulfill the testator's intent. The passage then clarifies the term 'taqadi' (litigation) in al-Hidayah: it actually means 'iqtida' (demanding payment), not necessarily 'qabd' (receiving). The commentator cites al-Ghayah and al-Qamus to show that in the language, taqadi includes receiving. However, custom might restrict it. The author (Ibn Nujaym) notes that he has not yet discussed the powers of the father, grandfather, judge, etc., over the minor's property; he will cover that later. The commentary follows the order of the matn: first the ruling, then the intra-madhhab reasoning, then the linguistic clarification.

Key Terms
تقاضي (taqāḍī): Demanding payment or litigating for a debt; includes receiving payment in its linguistic and original legal meaning, though custom may restrict it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does al-Qamus say about the meaning of 'taqadi'?
A) It means only to file a lawsuit.
B) It means to demand payment and receive it.
C) It means to appoint a new executor.
D) It means to pay debts from one's own pocket.

Key Takeaway
Taqadi in its original usage includes both demanding and receiving payment, but customary practice may restrict it; always check custom when defining the agent's authority.

Father's Direct Transactions with His Minor Child: Istihsan Over Qiyas
Ruling with proof, using istihsan to override qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a father buying from or selling to his minor child, explain why istihsan overrides qiyas, and identify the conditions regarding offer/acceptance, witnesses, and price.

Core Idea
A father's direct transaction with his minor child is valid by istihsan, without requiring offer and acceptance, as long as the price is fair.

In Hanafi fiqh, guardianship gives the father special authority over his minor child's property. One consequence is that he may buy from or sell to his child directly, even though he represents both sides. This lesson explores the ruling from al-Asl, the role of istihsan, and the practical conditions that protect the child's interest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
State the ruling: a father may sell his property to his minor son or buy his son's property for himself. Note that it is ja'iz (permissible) by istihsan, not by qiyas.
The passage begins with the ruling from al-Asl.
2
Understand the qiyas objection
Qiyas would invalidate such a transaction because the father acts on both sides of the contract, lacking two independent parties. Identify the 'illa: conflict of interest and lack of distinct consent.
The 'illa is jaliyyah (clear) and extracted by tahrir al-manat.
3
Recognize istihsan as the override
Istihsan permits the transaction for practical convenience, given the father's guardianship and the need to manage the child's affairs. Explain that istihsan overrides qiyas here.
This is a classic example of istihsan in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Determine contract formation conditions
The correct view (al-sahih) is that offer and acceptance are not required; the father's unilateral statement suffices. Witnesses are precautionary (istithnaq) for the child's right, not a condition of validity.
Muhammad al-Shaybani does not require witnesses; al-Natifi notes they are for protection.
5
Identify the price condition
The transaction must be at fair market value ('ala mithl al-qimah) or within the range people customarily overlook. A stricter narration from Abu Hanifa (via Hasan ibn Ziyad) requires exactly fair value.
The mufta bihi view is the broader one: acceptable if not excessively unfair.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في الأصل : الأب إذا باع مال نفسه من ابنه الصغير أو اشترى مال ابنه الصغير لنفسه جاز ،استحساناً، والقياس أن لا يجوز. ثم اختلف المشايخ في أنه هل يشترط لإتمام هذا العقد الإيجاب والقبول والصحيح أنه لا يشترط حتى أن الأب من ولدي بكذا أو قال اشتريت منه هذا بكذا فإنه يتم العقد ولا يحتاج بعت هذا لأنه إذا قال إلى أن يقول بعت واشتريت وإليه أشار في الكتاب فإنه قال : إذا باع من ولده وأشهد على ذلك جاز ولم يشترط القبول؛ هكذا ذكر الناطفي في واقعاته. ثم إن محمداً ما ذكر الإشهاد في الكتاب على وجه الشرط لجواز هذا البيع وتمامه. وإنما ذكره على وجه الاستيثاق لحق الصغير حتى يتم معاملة الصغير ويجوز هذا البيع من الابن بمثل القيمة أو بما يتغابن الناس في مثله. وروى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أنه لا يجوز هذا العقد إلا بمثل القيمة وفي
He said in al-Asl: The father, if he sells his own property to his minor son or purchases his minor son's property for himself, it is permissible by istihsan, while by qiyas it is not permissible. Then the scholars differed as to whether offer and acceptance are required to complete this contract. The correct view is that they are not required, such that if the father says 'from my son for such amount' or says 'I bought this from him for such amount', the contract is completed and he does not need to say 'I sold' and 'I bought'. And to this he alluded in the book, for he said: 'If he sold from his child and called witnesses to that, it is permissible and acceptance is not required.' Thus al-Natifi mentioned in his Waqi'at. Then Muhammad did not mention calling witnesses in the book as a condition for the permissibility and completion of this sale. Rather, he mentioned it as a precaution for the minor's right so that the minor's transaction is complete. And this sale from the son is permissible for the like value or for what people usually overlook in similar transactions. And al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa that this contract is not permissible except for the like value, and
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, discussing the permissibility of a father's direct transactions with his minor child. The text is from al-Asl (a Zahir al-Riwayah work). The ruling: a father may sell his property to his minor child or buy the child's property, despite the qiyas (analogy) that would prohibit it because one person is on both sides of the contract. Istihsan (juristic preference) permits it for practical convenience. The passage then addresses a procedural question: are offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul) required? The correct view (al-sahih) is that they are not; the father's unilateral statement suffices. The author (Muhammad al-Shaybani) mentions calling witnesses not as a condition for validity but as a precaution for the minor's rights. The price must be fair market value ('ala mithl al-qimah) or within the range that people customarily overlook (yutaghabanu). There is a narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad that Abu Hanifa requires exactly the fair value. The passage provides the rationale of istihsan over qiyas, and details the conditions of the transaction. It follows the order: first the ruling, then the conditions regarding offer/acceptance, then the evidentiary requirement, and finally the price condition.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that overrides a qiyas ruling for a stronger reason; here, it permits the father's transaction despite the apparent conflict of interest.
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning; here, the qiyas would prohibit the transaction because the father is on both sides of the contract.
إيجاب وقبول (ījāb wa qabūl): Offer and acceptance; the two components of a contract. In this context, the father's unilateral statement suffices without explicit acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view (al-sahih), is offer and acceptance required for a father's sale to his minor son?
A) Yes, both offer and acceptance are required
B) Only offer is required
C) Only acceptance is required
D) Neither offer nor acceptance is required

Key Takeaway
A father's direct transaction with his minor child is valid by istihsan, overriding qiyas, without need for offer/acceptance, provided the price is fair.

The Fifteen Independent Actions of an Executor
Listing and comparing the nine exceptions from al-Quduri and the six added by al-Hidayah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list all fifteen independent actions of a Hanafi executor and distinguish between the nine from al-Quduri and the six added by al-Hidayah.

Core Idea
An executor may act alone only in fifteen specific matters; beyond these, he must consult a co-executor.

When a person dies, his property is managed by an executor (waṣī) appointed in the will. If multiple executors are appointed, they must generally act jointly. However, Hanafi law carves out fifteen exceptional actions that a single executor may perform independently. These are divided into nine cases from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar and six additional cases from al-Hidayah. Understanding this list is essential for correctly applying the rules of executorship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two layers of the list
Recognize that the base list comes from al-Quduri (9 items) and al-Hidayah added 6 more, totaling 15. This is a cumulative enumeration.
Al-Hidayah follows al-Quduri's methodology and expands it.
2
Memorize the nine original exceptions
Learn the nine actions from al-Quduri: buying shroud, preparing body, food and clothing for minors, returning deposit, paying debt, executing specific will, freeing specific slave, litigation for deceased.
These are urgent or necessary actions that cannot be delayed.
3
Memorize the six additional exceptions from al-Hidayah
Learn the six added actions: returning usurped property, selling corruptly purchased item, preserving wealth, accepting gift, selling perishable items, collecting lost property.
These also involve necessity or preservation of rights.
4
Combine and verify the total
Count both lists together to confirm 15 items. Practice recalling them in order.
The total is 15; any action not in this list requires joint action by all executors.
5
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a factual situation, determine whether the action falls within the fifteen and thus can be done by one executor alone.
Use the list as a strict checklist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخصومة في حق الميت ووصي الوصي وصي التركتين وتصح قسمته عن الورثة مع مختصره بقوله إلا في شراء الكفن للميت وتجهيزه وطعام الصغار وكسوتهم ورد وديعة بعينها وقضاء دين وتنفيذ وصية بعينها وعتق عبد بعينه والخصومة في حقوق الميت اهـ. وهذه تسعة أشياء كما ترى قصر القدوري الاستثناء عليها في مختصره، وأقتفى أثره صاحب الهداية وزاد فيها على ذلك أشياء بقوله : ورد المغصوب والمشتري شراء فاسداً وحفظ الأموال وقبول الهبة وبيع ما يخشى عليه التوى والتلف وجمع الأموال الضائعة وهذه التي زادها في الهداية على ما في الكتاب ستة أشياء فيصير مجموع الأشياء المعدودة خمسة عشر .اهـ.
And litigation on behalf of the deceased, and the executor of the executor is the executor of two estates, and his division over the heirs is valid with his abridgement by his saying: Except in buying the shroud for the deceased and preparing him, and food for minors and their clothing, returning a specific deposit, paying a debt, executing a specific will, freeing a specific slave, and litigation in the rights of the deceased. End. These are nine things as you see; al-Quduri confined the exceptions to them in his abridgement, and the author of al-Hidayah followed his path and added to them things by his saying: And returning the usurped property, and the buyer of a corrupt purchase, preserving wealth, accepting a gift, selling what is feared will be ruined or lost, and collecting lost property. These are six things which he added in al-Hidayah to what is in the book, so the total of the counted things becomes fifteen. End.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage enumerates the exceptional cases in which a single executor (واصي) may act independently without the concurrence of a co-executor. The base text (al-Quduri's Mukhtasar) listed nine items: (1) buying the shroud for the deceased, (2) preparing his body, (3) food for minor orphans, (4) their clothing, (5) returning a specific deposit, (6) paying a debt, (7) executing a specific will, (8) freeing a specific slave, and (9) litigation on behalf of the deceased. Al-Hidayah added six more: (10) returning usurped property, (11) selling an item bought in a corrupt sale, (12) preserving wealth, (13) accepting a gift, (14) selling what is feared will spoil or be lost, and (15) collecting lost property. The total thus becomes fifteen. This is a straightforward ruling enumeration; no proofs are given here, and the information is intended for memorization.

Key Terms
وصي (waṣī): An executor appointed in a will to manage the deceased's affairs.
تقاضي (taqāḍī): Litigation or claiming a debt in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Hidayah, how many total independent actions can an executor perform?
A) 9
B) 15
C) 6
D) 21

Key Takeaway
An executor may act alone in exactly fifteen enumerated situations; any action outside this list requires concurrence of all co-executors.

Executor Actions and Replacement of a Deceased Executor
Talīl for three executor actions and intra-Hanafī dispute on replacing a deceased executor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rationale for a single executor executing a specific will or freeing a specific slave, justify why one executor can handle litigation alone, and state the ruling when one executor dies according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Executing a specific will or freeing a specific slave requires no independent judgment, so one executor suffices; litigation is handled by one because gathering both is impractical; if one dies, the judge may appoint a replacement.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq explains why a single executor can perform certain tasks without the other executor’s involvement. For specific bequests and manumissions, no opinion (ra’y) is needed, so the testator’s intent is fulfilled by one. For litigation, requiring both is impractical. The passage also covers what happens if one executor dies: do we appoint a replacement, and on what basis? Finally, it clarifies the technical meaning of ‘taqāḍī’ in this context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three actions and their common element
Locate the three actions: executing a specific will (تنفيذ وصية معينة), freeing a specific slave (عتق عبد معين), and litigation (الخصومة). Note that the first two share the common element of not needing opinion (ra’y).
2
Understand the rationale for litigation
Litigation is treated differently: the reason a single executor can act is that convening both executors is impossible or highly impractical (متعذر). This is similar to agency (wakāla) where two agents cannot both attend court.
3
Analyze the scenario of a deceased executor
When one executor dies, the judge (qāḍī) replaces him. The passage presents two views: according to the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad), if the remaining executor is incapable of acting alone, the judge adds a co-executor; according to Abū Yūsuf alone, even if the remaining executor is capable, the judge still appoints a replacement because the testator intended two executors.
Note that ‘عندهما’ refers to Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad, while ‘عند أبي يوسف’ is a separate view.
4
Examine the linguistic clarification of 'taqāḍī'
The author clarifies that ‘taqāḍī’ in this context means demanding and receiving the debt (اقتضاء), not merely going to court. Lexicons like al-Qāmūs confirm this meaning. This affects the powers of an executor appointed for litigation (تقاضي).
This is a textual note (تنبيه) showing that custom may have narrowed the word's meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وتنفيذ وصية معينة وعتق عبد معين لأنه لا يحتاج فيه إلى رأي . قال رحمه الله : (والخصومة في حق الميت لأن الاجتماع فيه متعذر ولهذا ينفرد بها أحد الوكيلين أيضاً. ولو مات أحدهما جعل القاضي مكانه وصياً آخر، أما عندهما فظاهر لأن الباقي منهما عاجز عن الانفراد بالتصرف فيضم القاضي إليه وصياً ينظر إلى الميت عند عجز الميت، وأما عند أبي يوسف فلأن الحي منهما وإن كان يقدر على التصرف فالموصي قدر أن يجعل وصيين يتصرفان وذلك ممكن لتحقيق نصب وصي آخر مكان الأول. قال في الهداية وقضاء دين قال في الغاية والمراد بالتقاضي الاقتضاء وكذا كان المراد في عرفهم اهـ. وهذا أن لا يكون الاقتضاء الذي هو القبض معنى التقاضي في الوضع واللغة يوهم بل كان معناه في العرف مع أن الأمر ليس كذلك كما صرح به المصنف في باب الوكالة بالخصومة من كتاب الوكالة حيث قال : الوكيل بالتقاضي يملك القبض على أصل الرواية في معناه وضعاً إلا أن العرف بخلافه وهو قاض على الوضع ا هـ. ويدلك على كون معناه ذلك في الوضع ما ذكر في كتب اللغة. قال في القاموس : تقاضاه الدين قبضه منه . وقال في الأساس: تقاضيته ديني وبديني واقتضيته ديني واقتضيت منه حقي أي أخذته اهـ.
He (the author) said: 'And executing a specific will and freeing a specific slave, because it does not need opinion.' He said: 'And litigation on behalf of the deceased, because meeting is impossible for it, and for this reason one of the two agents also acts alone.' And if one of them dies, the judge makes another executor in his place. As for the two (i.e., Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?), it is apparent, because the remaining one is incapable of acting alone, so the judge adds to him an executor who looks after the deceased's interest due to the deceased's incapacity. As for according to Abu Yusuf, because the living one, even if he is able to act, the testator had the power to make two executors to act, and that is possible for the realization of appointing another executor in place of the first. He said in al-Hidayah: 'And paying a debt.' He said in al-Ghayah: 'What is meant by litigation (taqāḍī) is demanding (iqtiḍāʾ), and likewise it was meant in their custom.' End. And this is that the demand which is receipt (qabḍ) is not the meaning of litigation (taqāḍī) in etymology and language, rather its meaning in custom is otherwise, as the author clarified in the chapter on agency for litigation from the Book of Agency where he said: 'The agent for litigation owns receipt according to the original narration in its meaning linguistically, but custom is contrary to it, and it is a judge on the linguistic usage.' And what indicates its meaning in linguistic usage is what is mentioned in lexical books. He said in al-Qāmūs: 'He demanded the debt from him (taqāḍāhu) means he received it from him.' And he said in al-Asās: 'I demanded my debt from him (taqāḍaytuhu) and I claimed my debt (iqtaḍaytuhu) and I demanded my right from him (iqtaḍaytu minhu haqqī) meaning I took it.' End.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage provides the rationale for three items in the previous list: executing a specific will, freeing a specific slave, and litigation. For the first two, no independent judgment (ra'y) is needed, so one executor may act alone. For litigation, convening both executors is impractical, so one may proceed alone. It then discusses the scenario when one executor dies: the judge appoints a new one. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if the remaining executor is incapable of acting alone, the judge appoints a co-executor. According to Abu Yusuf alone, even if the living one can act, the judge still appoints a replacement because the testator intended two executors. The passage ends with a linguistic clarification: the term 'taqāḍī' (litigation) in the context of paying a debt actually means receiving the money (iqtiḍāʾ), as confirmed by lexicons like al-Qāmūs and al-Asās.

Key Terms
تقاضي (taqāḍī): Litigation, but in this context means demanding and receiving a debt.
عندهما (ʿindahumā): According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
اقتضاء (iqtiḍāʾ): Demanding and receiving a debt; the verbal noun of the act.
قبض (qabḍ): Taking possession or receipt of money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can a single executor carry out litigation on behalf of the deceased?
A) Because litigation is a light matter
B) Because both executors cannot be present simultaneously
C) Because the executor is appointed for that purpose
D) Because the deceased's rights are limited

Key Takeaway
A single executor may execute a specific will, free a specific slave, and handle litigation alone; when an executor dies, the judge replaces him only if the surviving executor is incapable (the mufta bihi).

Wasiyyah — Bequest
intermediate
12 min
The Father's Direct Sale or Purchase with His Minor Child: Permissibility by Istihsan
Core ruling on father's transaction with minor child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a father's direct sale or purchase with his minor child and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas.

Core Idea
A father's transaction with his minor child is valid by istihsan despite conflict of interest, due to his natural care.

This lesson examines the foundational ruling from al-Asl: a father may sell his own property to his minor son or buy his minor son's property for himself. The direct analogy (qiyas) would prohibit such transactions due to inherent conflict of interest, but the Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) to validate them, relying on the father's presumed compassion and fair dealing. We will explore the conditions, the dispensation from formal offer and acceptance, and the required price fairness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage addresses a father selling his own property to his minor son or buying his minor son's property for himself. The ruling is 'jāʾiz' (permissible) by istihsan.
This contradicts the apparent analogy (qiyas) which would deem it invalid.
2
Understand the qiyas vs. istihsan reasoning
The qiyas would prohibit because of conflict of interest (the father is both buyer and seller). Istihsan overrides this based on the father's natural care and the need for practical dealing.
Istihsan is a recognized source in Hanafi uṣūl.
3
Recognize the dispensation from formal offer and acceptance
The correct view (ṣaḥīḥ) is that no formal ījāb and qabūl are required. The father's statement, such as 'I sold to my son for such' or 'I bought from him for such', suffices to complete the contract.
This simplifies the transaction, reflecting the father's authority.
4
Apply the price condition
The sale is valid at the like value (mithl al-qīmah) or within the customary tolerance (mā yataghāban al-nās fī mithlihi). A slight overcharge that people normally overlook does not invalidate.
There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on exact value; the relied-upon position allows slight variance.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf on exact value
Ḥasan ibn Ziyād narrated from Abū Ḥanīfa that the contract is only valid at the exact market value, disallowing even slight overcharge. However, the zāhir al-riwāyah (apparent narration) is considered more correct (aṣaḥḥ) and is the muftā bihī.
The tarjīḥ authority is the author's statement: 'mā dhukira fī ẓāhir al-riwāyah aṣaḥḥ'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يتعرض المصنف لتصرفات الأب ووكيل الأب والجد والقاضي وأمين القاضي في مال الصغير ونحن نذكر ذلك . قال في الأصل : الأب إذا باع مال نفسه من ابنه الصغير أو اشترى مال ابنه الصغير لنفسه جاز ،استحساناً، والقياس أن لا يجوز. ثم اختلف المشايخ في أنه هل يشترط لإتمام هذا العقد الإيجاب والقبول والصحيح أنه لا يشترط حتى أن الأب من ولدي بكذا أو قال اشتريت منه هذا بكذا فإنه يتم العقد ولا يحتاج بعت هذا لأنه إذا قال إلى أن يقول بعت واشتريت وإليه أشار في الكتاب فإنه قال : إذا باع من ولده وأشهد على ذلك جاز ولم يشترط القبول؛ هكذا ذكر الناطفي في واقعاته. ثم إن محمداً ما ذكر الإشهاد في الكتاب على وجه الشرط لجواز هذا البيع وتمامه. وإنما ذكره على وجه الاستيثاق لحق الصغير حتى يتم معاملة الصغير ويجوز هذا البيع من الابن بمثل القيمة أو بما يتغابن الناس في مثله. وروى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أنه لا يجوز هذا العقد إلا بمثل القيمة وفي هذا الغبن اليسير على هذه الرواية يمنع ولكن ما ذكره في ظاهر الرواية أصح. ولو وكل الأب رجلاً ببيع عبد له من ابن له والابن صغير لا يعبر عن نفسه ففعل الوكيل ذلك لا يجوز، ولو وكل الصغير بعد البلوغ وكيلاً ووكل الأب أيضاً ذلك الوكيل فباع هذا ذلك لا يجوز كذا هنا. ولو كان الأب حاضراً وقبل من الوكيل جاز وتكون العهدة من جانب الابن على الأب ومن جانب الأب على الوكيل وقيل على العكس. ذكر هشام في نوادره وعن محمد: إذا اشترى الأب عبد ابنه الصغير شراء فساداً فمات العبد قبل أن يستعمل العبد أو يقبضه أو يأمره بعمل مات من مال الصغير. وفي المنتقى : اشترى من ابنه عبداً والعبد في يد الأب فمات العبد فهو من مال الابن حتى يأمره الوالد بعمل أو يقبضه، وإذا كان لرجل ابنان فباع مال أحدهما من الآخر وهما صغيران فإن قال بعت عبد فلان من فلان جاز ذلك، هكذا ذكر المسألة في الديات ولم يذكر ثمة أنهما إذا بلغا فالعهدة على من تكون وقد اختلفوا فيه والصحيح أن العهدة عليهما. ولو وكل الأب رجلاً حتى باع مال أحدهما من الآخر يجوز، وإذا وكل رجلاً بذلك يجب أن يجوز ويجاب بأن الأب لكمال شفقته ملك هؤلاء وكيله لفقدها. ولو وكل الأب وكيلاً بالبيع ووكيلاً بالشراء فباع الوكيل يجوز. وفي الزيادات : الأب إذا باع مال الصغير من أجنبي بمثل القيمة فهو على ثلاثة أوجه فإن كان الأب عدلاً عند الناس أو كان مستوراً بحال يجوز البيع حتى لو كبر الابن لم يكن له أن ينقض البيع عند المشايخ وبه أخذ الصدر الشهيد إذا كان خيراً للصغير بأن باع بضعف قيمته، وإن باع ما سوى العقار من المنقولات ففيه روايتان في رواية يجوز ويؤخذ الثمن ويوضع على يد عدل، وفي رواية لا يجوز إلا إذا كان خيراً للصغير على نحو ما قلنا. وفي نوادر هشام عن أبي يوسف: الأب إذا باع لابنه الصغير ما ثمنه عشرة دراهم بدرهم يجوز وإن اشترى له ما ثمنه درهم بعشرة دراهم لم يجز. وفي الأصل سوى بين البيع والشراء في هذه الصورة وأشباهها. وذكر شمس الأئمة الحلواني في أدب القاضي في أبواب الوصايا أن الصغير إذا ورث مالاً والأب مبذر مستحق الحجر على قول من يرى ذلك لا تثبت الولاية للأب وفي المنتقى عن محمد: رجل باع عبد ابنه الصغير من رجل بألف ثم قال في مرضه قد قبضت من فلان من الثمن مائتين فمات في مرضه لم يجز إقرار الأب وكان للوصي أن يأخذ الثمن من المشتري كما لو لم يوجد هذا الإقرار من المريض، ولو قال في مرضه قبضتها من فلان فضاعت كان مصدقاً، ولو قال قبضتها واستهلكتها لم يكن مصدقاً ولا يبرأ المشتري منها ولا يكون للمشتري إذا أخذ منه الثمن أن يرجع على الأب أو في ماله. الزيادات عن محمد: إذا اشترى الأب لابنه الصغير شيئاً ونقد الثمن من ماله يرجع ولم يشهد على ذلك ولم يقض له القاضي بالرجوع وسعه فيما بينه وبين ربها أن ينوي أن يرجع .
The author did not address the transactions of the father, the father's agent, the grandfather, the judge, and the judge's trustee regarding the property of the minor, and we mention that. He said in al-Asl: If a father sells his own property to his minor son or buys his minor son's property for himself, it is permissible by istihsan, while by qiyas it is not permissible. Then the scholars differed as to whether offer and acceptance are required to complete this contract, and the correct view is that they are not required, so that when the father says 'from my son for such' or said 'I bought this from him for such', the contract is complete and he does not need to say 'I sold' and 'I bought' because when he says [such], [it is sufficient]. Al-Nāṭifī mentioned that in his Wāqiʿāt. Then Muhammad did not mention witnessing in the book as a condition for the validity of this sale and its completion; rather he mentioned it as a precaution for the right of the minor until the minor's transaction is completed. And this sale from the son is valid at the like value or at what people normally tolerate in such. And al-Ḥasan related from Abu Hanifa that this contract is not valid except at the like value, and in this slight overcharge according to this narration is prevented, but what is mentioned in the apparent narration is more correct. And if the father appointed a man to sell a slave of his to his son, and the son is a minor who cannot express himself, and the agent did that, it is not valid. And if the minor after puberty appointed an agent and the father also appointed that same agent, and he sold that, it is not valid, similarly here. And if the father was present and accepted from the agent, it is valid, and liability from the side of the son is on the father, and from the side of the father on the agent, and it is said the opposite. Hisham mentioned in his Nawādir from Muhammad: If the father bought his minor son's slave in a corrupt purchase, and the slave died before he used the slave or took possession or ordered him to work, it is from the minor's wealth. In al-Muntaqā: He bought a slave from his son and the slave was in the father's hand, and the slave died, it is from the son's wealth until the father orders him to work or takes possession. And if a man has two sons and sold the property of one to the other while both are minors, and he said 'I sold the slave of so-and-so to so-and-so', it is valid. This is how the issue is mentioned in al-Diyāt, and he did not mention there if they reach puberty, on whom is the liability; they differed, and the correct view is that liability is on both. And if the father appointed a man to sell the property of one to the other, it is valid. And if he appointed a man for that, it must be valid, and the answer is that the father, due to his complete compassion, made those his agent for its lack. And if the father appointed an agent for sale and an agent for purchase, and the agent sold, it is valid. In al-Ziyādāt: If the father sells the minor's property to a stranger at the like value, it is on three aspects: if the father is considered just among people or was of concealed status, the sale is valid, so that when the minor grows up, he cannot rescind the sale according to the scholars, and al-Ṣadr al-Shahīd adopted this if it was beneficial for the minor, such as selling at double its value. And if he sells other than real estate from movables, there are two narrations: in one, it is valid and the price is taken and placed with a trustworthy person; in another, it is not valid unless it is beneficial for the minor as we said. And in Nawādir Hishām from Abu Yūsuf: If the father sells to his minor son something worth ten dirhams for one dirham, it is valid; and if he buys for him something worth one dirham for ten dirhams, it is not valid. In al-Asl, he equalized between sale and purchase in this and similar cases. And Shams al-A'imma al-Ḥulwānī mentioned in Adab al-Qāḍī in the chapters of wills that if a minor inherits wealth and the father is a spendthrift deserving of interdiction according to the view of those who hold that, the father's guardianship is not established. And in al-Muntaqā from Muhammad: A man sold his minor son's slave to a man for a thousand, then said in his illness 'I received from so-and-so two hundred of the price' and then died in his illness, the father's confession is not valid, and the executor may take the price from the buyer as if this confession from the patient did not exist. And if he said in his illness 'I received it from so-and-so and it was lost', he is believed; and if he said 'I received it and consumed it', he is not believed, and the buyer is not discharged from it, and the buyer if he takes the price from him cannot recourse against the father or his estate. From al-Ziyādāt from Muhammad: If the father buys something for his minor son and pays the price from his own money, he can reclaim, and if he did not witness that and the judge did not decide for him the reclamation, it is permissible for him between himself and his Lord to intend to reclaim.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage presents a comprehensive set of rulings regarding a father's transactions with his minor child's property. The core ruling is that such transactions are valid by istihsan despite the apparent conflict of interest, because the father's love and care ensure fairness. The contract does not require formal offer and acceptance; the father's statement suffices. The price must be at market value or within the customary tolerance. There are differing narrations: one from Hasan ibn Ziyad requires exact value, but the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwaya) permits slight discrepancy. Various scenarios are examined: when an agent is involved (with conditions), when the purchase is corrupt, the death of the property, liability after puberty, sale between siblings, and sale to strangers. Additional rulings cover the spendthrift father (guardianship removed), the father's confession in illness regarding receipt of payment, and the father's right to reclaim money paid for the son's purchase. This collection demonstrates the Hanafi school's detailed approach to balancing the minor's protection with practical necessity.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas (analogy) in favor of a more equitable ruling.
قبض (qabḍ): Taking possession or receiving physical control of an item.
عهدة (ʿuhdah): Liability or responsibility for defects or damages in a sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon Hanafi ruling regarding the requirement of offer and acceptance (ījāb wa qabūl) in a father's direct transaction with his minor child?
A) They are strictly required for validity
B) They are not required; the father's statement suffices
C) They are required only if the child is represented by an agent
D) They are required in sales but not in purchases

Key Takeaway
A father's sale or purchase with his minor child is valid by istihsan without formal offer/acceptance, provided the price is fair or within customary tolerance.

Wasiyyah — Bequest
intermediate
15 min
Agency and Liability in Father's Transactions with Minor's Property
Agency, liability, and special cases in father-minor transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on agency, liability (ʿuhdah), and special cases arising from a father's transactions with his minor child's property.

Core Idea
When a father appoints an agent or when property perishes, liability shifts; the father's care remains the guiding principle.

This lesson covers scenarios beyond the direct father-minor transaction: appointment of agents, distribution of liability (ʿuhdah) when an agent is involved, corrupt purchases (shirāʾ fasād), death of the subject matter before possession, sale between minor siblings, and the case of a spendthrift father. These detailed rules illustrate the Hanafi school's effort to balance the minor's protection with practical necessity.

Guided Path
1
Examine agency scenarios
The text considers cases where the father appoints an agent to buy or sell with the minor. If the minor is too young to express consent, the agent's transaction is invalid. If the minor later appoints the same agent after puberty, it remains invalid unless the father is present and accepts.
The key is the father's presence and acceptance; without it, the agent cannot bind the parties.
2
Understand liability (ʿuhdah) distribution
When an agent is involved and the father accepts, the liability for defects or damages (ʿuhdah) from the son's side falls on the father, and from the father's side on the agent. A contrary opinion reverses this, but the first is preferred.
Liability ensures the minor is protected; the father ultimately bears the risk.
3
Analyze corrupt purchase (shirāʾ fasād) and property perishing
If the father buys the minor's slave in a corrupt contract, and the slave dies before the father takes possession or gives work, the loss is from the minor's wealth. If the slave is already in the father's hand (e.g., as custodian), the loss is from the son's wealth until the father gives a command or takes possession.
Possession (qabḍ) is key to transferring liability.
4
Apply rulings on sale between minor siblings
A father may sell the property of one minor son to another minor son by stating the sale. The liability after puberty is on both sons (each bears his own share). This is valid because the father acts in the best interest of both.
The father's authority covers both parties.
5
Consider the spendthrift father
If a minor inherits property and the father is a spendthrift (mubadhdhir) who is subject to interdiction (ḥajr), the father's guardianship is not established. This protects the minor's wealth from irresponsible management.
This is an exception to the general rule that the father is the natural guardian.

المريض، ولو قال في مرضه قبضتها من فلان فضاعت كان مصدقاً، ولو قال قبضتها واستهلكتها لم يكن مصدقاً ولا يبرأ المشتري منها ولا يكون للمشتري إذا أخذ منه الثمن أن يرجع على الأب أو في ماله. الزيادات عن محمد: إذا اشترى الأب لابنه الصغير شيئاً ونقد الثمن من ماله يرجع ولم يشهد على ذلك ولم يقض له القاضي بالرجوع وسعه فيما بينه وبين ربها أن ينوي أن يرجع .

He said in al-Asl: If a father sells his own property to his minor son or buys his minor son's property for himself, it is permissible by istihsan, while by qiyas it is not permissible. Al-Nāṭifī mentioned that in his Wāqiʿāt. Then Muhammad did not mention witnessing in the book as a condition for the validity of this sale and its completion; rather he mentioned it as a precaution for the right of the minor until the minor's transaction is completed. And this sale from the son is valid at the like value or at what people normally tolerate in such. And al-Ḥasan related from Abu Hanifa that this contract is not valid except at the like value, and in this slight overcharge according to this narration is prevented, but what is mentioned in the apparent narration is more correct.
