And if the minor after puberty appointed an agent and the father also appointed that same agent, and he sold that, it is not valid, similarly here. And if the father was present and accepted from the agent, it is valid, and liability from the side of the son is on the father, and from the side of the father on the agent, and it is said the opposite. Hisham mentioned in his Nawādir from Muhammad: If the father bought his minor son's slave in a corrupt purchase, and the slave died before he used the slave or took possession or ordered him to work, it is from the minor's wealth. In al-Muntaqā: He bought a slave from his son and the slave was in the father's hand, and the slave died, it is from the son's wealth until the father orders him to work or takes possession. And if a man has two sons and sold the property of one to the other while both are minors, and he said 'I sold the slave of so-and-so to so-and-so', it is valid. This is how the issue is mentioned in al-Diyāt, and he did not mention there if they reach puberty, on whom is the liability; they differed, and the correct view is that liability is on both. And if the father appointed a man to sell the property of one to the other, it is valid. And if he appointed a man for that, it must be valid, and the answer is that the father, due to his complete compassion, made those his agent for its lack. And if the father appointed an agent for sale and an agent for purchase, and the agent sold, it is valid. In al-Ziyādāt: If the father sells the minor's property to a stranger at the like value, it is on three aspects: if the father is considered just among people or was of concealed status, the sale is valid, so that when the minor grows up, he cannot rescind the sale according to the scholars, and al-Ṣadr al-Shahīd adopted this if it was beneficial for the minor, such as selling at double its value. And if he sells other than real estate from movables, there are two narrations: in one, it is valid and the price is taken and placed with a trustworthy person; in another, it is not valid unless it is beneficial for the minor as we said. And in Nawādir Hishām from Abu Yūsuf: If the father sells to his minor son something worth ten dirhams for one dirham, it is valid; and if he buys for him something worth one dirham for ten dirhams, it is not valid. In al-Asl, he equalized between sale and purchase in this and similar cases. And Shams al-A'imma al-Ḥulwānī mentioned in Adab al-Qāḍī in the chapters of wills that if a minor inherits wealth and the father is a spendthrift deserving of interdiction according to the view of those who hold that, the father's guardianship is not established. And in al-Muntaqā from Muhammad: A man sold his minor son's slave to a man for a thousand, then said in his illness 'I received from so-and-so two hundred of the price' and then died in his illness, the father's confession is not valid, and the executor may take the price from the buyer as if this confession from the patient did not exist. And if he said in his illness 'I received it from so-and-so and it was lost', he is believed; and if he said 'I received it and consumed it', he is not believed, and the buyer is not discharged from it, and the buyer if he takes the price from him cannot recourse against the father or his estate. From al-Ziyādāt from Muhammad: If the father buys something for his minor son and pays the price from his own money, he can reclaim, and if he did not witness that and the judge did not decide for him the reclamation, it is permissible for him between himself and his Lord to intend to reclaim.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage presents a comprehensive set of rulings regarding a father's transactions with his minor child's property. The core ruling is that such transactions are valid by istihsan despite the apparent conflict of interest, because the father's love and care ensure fairness. The contract does not require formal offer and acceptance; the father's statement suffices. The price must be at market value or within the customary tolerance. There are differing narrations: one from Hasan ibn Ziyad requires exact value, but the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwaya) permits slight discrepancy. Various scenarios are examined: when an agent is involved (with conditions), when the purchase is corrupt, the death of the property, liability after puberty, sale between siblings, and sale to strangers. Additional rulings cover the spendthrift father (guardianship removed), the father's confession in illness regarding receipt of payment, and the father's right to reclaim money paid for the son's purchase. This collection demonstrates the Hanafi school's detailed approach to balancing the minor's protection with practical necessity.

Key Terms
عهدة (ʿuhdah): Liability or responsibility for defects or damages in a sale.
قبض (qabḍ): Taking possession or receiving physical control of an item.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas (analogy) in favor of a more equitable ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a father buys his minor son's slave in a corrupt purchase and the slave dies before the father takes possession or gives any order, the loss is from:
A) The father's wealth
B) The minor's wealth
C) The agent's wealth
D) Shared equally

Key Takeaway
Agency, liability, and special cases in father-minor transactions are governed by detailed rules that protect the minor while allowing practical management by the father.

Father's Capacity: Executor, Not Judge
Intra-Hanafi dispute on father's borrowing and lending with pledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the father's legal capacity from that of an executor and a judge regarding a minor's property, and state the mufta bihi Hanafi ruling on borrowing and lending with pledge.

Core Idea
The father is like an executor, not a judge: he cannot borrow from a stranger for the minor, but can lend his own money and take a pledge.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced question: what financial authority does a father have over his minor child's property? It clarifies that the father's capacity is limited, akin to an executor, not a judge. This distinction determines whether he can borrow the minor's property from a third party or lend his own money with a pledge from the child's estate. We will trace the dispute within the Hanafi school and identify the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Actions
The passage discusses two distinct acts: (1) the father borrowing the minor's property from a stranger, and (2) the father lending his own money to the minor and taking a pledge.
2
Recognize the Key Analogy
The correct view equates the father with an executor (wasi), not a judge (qadi). An executor has limited authority; a judge has broader powers.
The distinction affects what transactions are valid.
3
Trace the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
There are two narrations from Abu Hanifa; the soundest is that the father is like an executor. Additionally, Muhammad al-Shaybani in the Nawadir reports that even lending with a pledge is not permissible.
The dispute is on lending with pledge, not on borrowing.
4
Extract the Mufta Bihi Ruling
The relied-upon position: (a) borrowing from a stranger is prohibited; (b) lending one's own money and taking a pledge from the minor's property is permissible.
This is supported by scholars like al-Halwani and Khaherzadeh.
5
Understand the Practical Consequence
The father cannot incur debt on the minor's estate, but he can transact using his own funds while securing repayment from the minor's assets.
This protects the minor's property from unnecessary liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوصايا/ باب الوصي وما يملكه
حنيفة أنه ليس للأب أن يستقرض مال الصغير من الأجنبي. وذكر شمس الأئمة السرخسي في الروايات الظاهرة ليس له ذلك. وفي الذخيرة واختلف المشايخ في الأب في اختلاف الروايتين عن أبي حنيفة، والصحيح أن الأب بمنزلة الوصي لا بمنزلة القاضي، والأب إذا أقرض مال نفسه لولده الصغير وأخذ رهناً من مال ولده جاز له ذلك؛ هكذا ذكر شمس الأئمة الحلواني وخواهر زاده وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن محمد : لا يجوز وسيأتي له مزيد مسائل.
Book of Wills / Chapter on the Executor and What He Possesses. [Abu] Hanifa: It is not permissible for the father to borrow the property of a minor from a stranger. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned in the apparent narrations that he does not have that. In al-Dhakhira: The scholars differed regarding the father based on the two narrations from Abu Hanifa, and the correct [view] is that the father is in the position of an executor, not in the position of a judge. And if the father lends his own property to his minor child and takes a pledge from his child's property, it is permissible for him; thus mentioned Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani and Khaherzadeh. In the Nawadir of Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad: it is not permissible. And further issues will come.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq opens the chapter on executors. It presents a ruling on the father's capacity in financial dealings with his minor child's property. The key point is a comparison: the father is likened to an executor (wasi) rather than a judge (qadi). An executor has limited authority; he cannot borrow the minor's property from a third party. The text notes a difference of narration from Abu Hanifa, but the relied-upon (sahih) view is that it is not allowed. However, when the father lends his own money to his child and takes a pledge from the child's property, this is permissible according to most scholars, though Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani in a rare report (Nawadir) prohibits it. The phrase 'further issues will come' indicates the reader will encounter more details later.

Key Terms
وصي (wasi): An executor appointed by the father or judge to manage a minor's property, with limited powers compared to the father.
قاضي (qadi): A judge with authority over minors, including power to sell property without special permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, can a father borrow money from a stranger for his minor child?
A) Yes, because he is the natural guardian.
B) No, because he is like an executor, not a judge.
C) Yes, but only with the judge's permission.
D) No, but he can lend his own money to the child.

Key Takeaway
The father is legally akin to an executor: he cannot borrow the minor's property but can lend his own and take a pledge.

Father's Authority Over Executor's Will and Debts
Ruling on father's right to execute will and inability to take from estate with debts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two scenarios regarding the father's authority to execute the will and to take from the estate, and identify the condition that restricts his ability.

Core Idea
A father may execute a will, but debts and minor heirs prevent him from taking anything from the estate.

This passage from al-Khassaf's Adab al-Qadi clarifies the father's legal position after his son's death. It distinguishes between executing the will and taking from the estate. The father's role as a natural guardian does not automatically grant him the right to take property when debts and minor heirs are involved. Let us break down the two rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note that it presents two distinct situations: one where the father may execute the will, and another where the father cannot take anything from the estate.
2
Examine the actors and conditions for the first scenario
The first scenario involves a deceased man leaving a father and an executor. The father has the authority to execute the will.
The presence of an executor does not block the father's role.
3
Analyze the second scenario
The second scenario adds many debts, minor heirs, and both movable and immovable property. Here, the father cannot take anything from the estate.
The condition of debts and minors restricts his ability.
4
Understand the legal rationale
The prohibition stems from the priority of creditors' rights and the need to preserve the estate for the protection of minor heirs. The father, as a potential heir, cannot take property that would prejudice these interests.
5
Apply the ruling to analogous cases
Reflect on how this ruling applies when debts are small or when minors are not present. The text implies that the father's authority is restored when these obstacles are absent.
This is an expansion from the text's principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا هلك الرجل وترك أباً وأوصى كان للأب أن ينفذ وصاياه، ولو مات وعليه ديون كثيرة وورثة صغار وترك متاعاً وعقاراً لم يكن للأب أن شيئاً من التركة ؛ هكذا ذكر الخصاف في أدب القاضي.
And if a man dies and leaves a father and an executor, the father may execute his wills. But if he dies with many debts and minor heirs and leaves movable goods and immovable property, the father may not [take] anything from the estate. Thus mentioned al-Khassaf in Adab al-Qadi.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage from al-Khassaf's Adab al-Qadi addresses two scenarios. First, when a deceased person leaves both a father and an appointed executor (wasi), the father has the authority to execute the wills. This implies that the father's position as a natural guardian (wali) does not prevent him from carrying out the testator's instructions. Second, if the deceased dies with substantial debts and minor heirs, and leaves both movable (mata') and immovable (aqar) property, the father cannot take anything from the estate. This is because the debts take priority over distribution to heirs, and the father, as a potential heir, cannot take possession that would prejudice the creditors' rights. The father's role here is limited to preserving the estate for debt repayment and protection of the minors.

Key Terms
أب (ab): Father of the deceased; natural guardian.
وصي (wasi): Executor appointed by the testator to carry out the will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on the father's authority when the deceased leaves many debts and minor heirs?
A) The father can take the movable goods.
B) The father can execute the will only if there are no debts.
C) The father cannot take anything from the estate.
D) The father becomes the executor.

Key Takeaway
The father may execute wills but cannot take from the estate when debts and minor heirs exist.

Precedence Among Executor, Father, and Grandfather
Ruling on precedence in estate management

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the order of precedence among executor, father, and grandfather in estate management, and apply the rule to hypothetical scenarios.

Core Idea
The executor appointed by the deceased takes precedence over the father.

This lesson clarifies the hierarchy of authority over the estate of a deceased person. The ruling from Imam Muhammad establishes a clear order: the executor first, then the father, then the father's executor, and finally the qadi's appointee. Understanding this priority ensures that estate management is handled by the most authorized person.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary rule
State the first priority: the executor appointed by the deceased takes precedence over the father.
This is based on the deceased's own choice.
2
Determine secondary priority
If there is no executor, the father becomes the primary manager of the estate.
The father is the natural guardian.
3
Extend to the father's executor
If the father dies and had appointed an executor, that executor takes precedence over the qadi's appointee.
The father's choice is respected.
4
Recognize the grandfather's status
The grandfather is treated like the father in this hierarchy, so the same rules apply.
The text equates them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الذخيرة قال محمد يبيع رحمه الله : لم يذكر هذا الفصل في المبسوط على هذا البيان فإنه أقام الجد مقام الأب فإنه قال : إذا ترك وصيا وأبا فالوصي اولى، فإن لم يكن له وصي فالاب اولى وإن مات الأب وأوصى لوصيه فهو أولى ثم وصي القاضي.
And in al-Dhakhira, Muhammad ibn [Yabi'] said: He did not mention this chapter in al-Mabsut in this exposition, for he established the grandfather in the position of the father. He said: If he leaves an executor and a father, the executor takes precedence. If he has no executor, then the father takes precedence. If the father dies and appoints an executor, he takes precedence, then the qadi's executor.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage from al-Dhakhira, attributed to Imam Muhammad, clarifies the hierarchy of authority over the estate of a deceased person. The order is: first, the executor appointed by the deceased; if none, then the father (as natural guardian); if the father dies and has appointed an executor, that executor takes precedence over the qadi's appointee. The grandfather (jadd) is treated like the father in this context. This precedence is based on the closeness of the relationship and the degree of delegation from the deceased.

Key Terms
جد (jadd): Grandfather, who stands in the position of the father in guardianship.
أولى (awla): More entitled or having precedence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who takes precedence in managing the estate when a deceased person leaves both an executor and a father?
A) The father
B) The executor
C) The grandfather
D) The qadi

Key Takeaway
The executor appointed by the deceased has the highest priority in managing the estate.

The Qadi's Self-Dealing with Orphan's Property: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on qadi's self-dealing with orphan's property and intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Muhammad and Abu Hanifa on the qadi selling orphan property to himself, identify the reasoning behind each position, and explain the practical consequence of the dispute.

Core Idea
A qadi cannot judge in his own favor — but does managing orphan property count as judgment or guardianship?

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines a specific intra-Hanafi disagreement: can a qadi purchase orphan property from the estate he oversees? The answer hinges on whether the qadi's role is purely judicial (hukm) or includes guardianship (wilaya). By analyzing the arguments of Imam Muhammad and Abu Hanifa, you will understand how a core principle—'no one can be a judge for himself'—applies differently depending on how the qadi's capacity is defined. The passage also clarifies the distinct position of an executor (wasi) appointed by the deceased.

Guided Path
1
State the two Hanafi positions
Identify that Imam Muhammad rules the sale not permissible, while Abu Hanifa suggests it should be permissible.
This is an internal school disagreement (ikhtilaf).
2
Identify Imam Muhammad's reasoning
The qadi's sale of orphan property is a judicial act (hukm), and a judge cannot judge in his own favor, so the transaction is void.
This principle is extended to marriage contracts and any benefit to the qadi or his immediate family.
3
Identify Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa likely considers the qadi as a guardian (wali) for the orphan, allowing transactions akin to a parent managing a child's property.
The text does not provide his explicit analogy, but it is inferred from the context.
4
Examine the distinction with the executor (wasi)
If the qadi appoints an executor, that executor can buy from the orphan because he represents the deceased, not the qadi. His actions are not judicial.
The wasi is free from the restriction that binds the qadi.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If Muhammad's view is followed, the transaction is void and must be rescinded. If Abu Hanifa's view is taken, it is valid provided no exploitation occurs.
Later Hanafi fatwa often relies on Muhammad's view due to caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى : القاضي إذا باع مال اليتيم من نفسه أو باع مال نفسه من اليتيم ذكر في السير الكبير أنه لا يجوز، وأشار إلى المعنى وقال : لأن بيع القاضي مال الصغير يكون على وجه الحكم وحكم القاضي لنفسه باطل. وذكر في نوادر ابن رستم في أول مسائل النكاح عن محمد أن القاضي إذا زوج الصغير اليتيمة من ابنه الصغير وكذلك لو زوجها ممن لا تقبل شهادته له لا يجوز لأن نكاح القاضي يكون على وجه الحكم ولا يجوز حكمه لابنه الصغير ولا لمن لا تقبل شهادته له. قال الناطفي في أجناسه من مسائل البيوع : ذكر محمد في السير الكبير أن بيع القاضي مال الصغير من نفسه لا يجوز على قول محمد، وأما على قول أبي حنيفة ينبغي أن يجوز. وفي واقعات الناطفي : إذا اشترى مال اليتيم اليتيم يجوز وإن كان القاضي جعله وصياً لأن الوصي نائب عن الميت لا القاضي.
And in al-Muntaqa: If the qadi sells the orphan's property to himself or sells his own property to the orphan, it is mentioned in al-Siyar al-Kabir that it is not permissible. He pointed to the meaning and said: because the qadi's sale of the minor's property is in the nature of a judgment (hukm), and the qadi's judgment for himself is void. And it is mentioned in Nawadir Ibn Rustam at the beginning of marriage issues from Muhammad that if the qadi marries the orphan girl to his minor son, and similarly if he marries her to someone whose testimony is not acceptable for him, it is not permissible because the qadi's marriage is in the nature of a judgment, and his judgment for his minor son or for someone whose testimony is not acceptable for him is not permissible. Al-Natafi said in his Ajnas from issues of sales: Muhammad mentioned in al-Siyar al-Kabir that the qadi's sale of the minor's property to himself is not permissible according to Muhammad's opinion, but according to Abu Hanifa's opinion, it should be permissible. And in Waqi'at al-Natafi: If an orphan buys from another orphan, it is permissible even if the qadi made him an executor, because the executor is a deputy of the deceased, not of the qadi.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the qadi's transactions with the orphan's estate. According to the majority (Imam Muhammad), the qadi cannot buy from the orphan's estate or sell to himself, because his actions are judicial in nature and a judge cannot be a party to his own judgment. Similarly, he cannot marry the orphan to his son or to someone whose testimony is not acceptable for him. However, Abu Hanifa's view is that such a sale should be permissible, implying that the qadi's capacity as guardian (wali) allows it. The passage also clarifies that an orphan executor (wasi) who buys from another orphan is valid because he acts as a deputy of the deceased, not of the qadi. This highlights the distinction between judicial capacity and testamentary agency.

Key Terms
حكم (hukm): Judicial decision; in this context, the qadi's action in managing the orphan's property is considered a judicial act.
وصي (wasi): Executor appointed by the deceased, not by the qadi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, what is the ruling if the qadi sells orphan's property to himself?
A) Permissible
B) Not permissible
C) Permissible if it benefits the orphan
D) Not permissible unless the orphan agrees

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a qadi's self-dealing with orphan property is generally void (according to the preferred view) because a judge cannot make a judgment in his own favor.
